HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Help Wanted?

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
If President Clinton is re-elected, his biggest cabinet decision may be whether to retain Warren Christopher as Secretary of State, and if not, with whom to replace him. The chatter inside the Administration is that Mr. Clinton will neither ask Mr. Christopher to go nor beg him to stay, but will leave the decision to him. While White House insiders say Mr. Christopher seems inclined to leave -- he'll soon break Secretary of State James Baker's record for frequent-flier miles -- he's made no final decision, and no one will rule out Christopher succeeding Christopher.
Whom might Mr. Clinton choose to replace Mr. Christopher? Ideally, that should depend on what the President hopes to accomplish in foreign policy in a second term, but he hasn't made that clear at all. Until then, here's the roster from which Mr. Clinton will likely make his choice: 
 Anthony Lake, the national security adviser. Strengths: After four years as national security adviser he probably knows Mr. Clinton's instincts on foreign policy better than anyone. He seems to have learned from all the Administration's early ditherings in foreign policy, and he showed a lot of grit in sticking with heavily criticized Clinton policies on Haiti, North Korea, Bosnia, Mexico and Russia that have, in the end, at least defused these problems. Weaknesses: With his absent-minded-professor demeanor, he does not project authority very well. Often seems more interested in his pet boutique projects, like Northern Ireland, than in what matters, like Russia and China. Aloof, quirky and obsessively secretive, he doesn't bond easily with colleagues, subordinates or allies.
Richard Holbrooke, the former Bosnia negotiator. Strengths: Smart. Creative. Energetic. Understands American power and how to wield it. Proved in Bosnia that he can turn lemons into lemonade. Weaknesses: Maniacal self-promoter, addicted to appearing on CNN. Not the kind of person who would ever fall on a grenade for his boss. Overly calculating -- makes Machiavelli look like a simple, straightforward kinda guy. High-maintenance.
Senator Sam Nunn. Strengths: Has written with real insight about all the big issues -- Russia, China and NATO. Has had the courage to go against the rest of Congress and declare NATO expansion one of the dumbest ideas of the post-cold-war era. Commands genuine respect in the foreign policy community. Was way ahead of his time in developing the Nunn-Lugar program to help Russia dismantle its nuclear arsenal. Weakness: Would challenge Mr. Clinton more than Mr. Christopher ever did. Not clear Bill & Sam could get along for four years.
Madeleine Albright, the U.N. representative. Strengths: Did a very strong job articulating U.S. interests at the U.N. An iron lady unafraid to use force. Has dealt with the same breadth of issues as the Secretary of State. Weaknesses: While she is passionate on human rights, and always ready to confront dictators from Karadzic to Castro, it's much easier to do that from the U.N. podium than from the President's elbow in the Oval Office, where the trade-offs are much harder and more complex. Not clear she has the right stuff for that.
Thomas Pickering, the U.S. Ambassador to Russia. Strengths: Has an excellent grasp of the world, having served as U.S. Ambassador to Nigeria, El Salvador, Jordan, Israel, the U.N., India and Russia. Projects authority well. Strong personality. Very tough. Would lift sagging morale at the State Department to finally have one of their own as a boss. Weaknesses: Unclear whether he has a global vision that could guide a second-term Clinton Administration.
Former Senator George Mitchell. Strengths: Disciplined, judicious, experienced problem-solver. He still carries a big punch with, and a lot of i.o.u.'s from, colleagues in Congress. Weaknesses: A bit of a loner. He has no known world view. Complicated past relations with Mr. Clinton; possible year 2000 rival of Al Gore.
Senator Richard Lugar. Strengths: Can speak thoughtfully about everything from Russian nukes to Latin American debt. A Republican, he would bring a bipartisan flavor to a second Clinton term at a time when foreign policy has become rancorously partisan. Anyone who based his campaign for President in 1996 on foreign policy deserves consideration as Secretary of State. Weakness: Though he shares Mr. Christopher's Midwestern decency, he is only slightly more charismatic or intimidating. He's sort of Warren Christopher adjusted for inflation.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Doubts on Europe Money Plan Set Off a Rally in the Mark

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar lost ground against the German mark yesterday as traders sought a safe haven in the face of heightened anxiety about the timetable for Europe's conversion to a single currency.
The dollar finished slightly higher against the Japanese yen after being lower most of the day as traders awaited today's release of the Tankan, the Bank of Japan's quarterly report on business conditions. 
 In late New York trading, the dollar stood at 1.4783 marks, down from 1.4787 on Monday. The dollar was at 107.68 yen, up from 107.65. The British pound was worth $1.5545, down from $1.5570.
Europe's single currency, the Euro, is to be introduced in January 1999, but officials of several European governments have wondered publicly in recent days if the timetable can be met.
Some countries must cut budget deficits to meet economic guidelines before the Euro's introduction and doubts have arisen as to whether enough can meet the criteria.
Otmar Issing, the Bundesbank's chief economist, said Monday that Germany might not meet the requirements unless economic growth turned stronger.
Since Germany's economy is still considered the healthiest in Europe, any uncertainty about the monetary union prompts traders to buy marks.
Earlier in the day, the dollar traded lower against the mark and the yen after a surprisingly upbeat report on consumer confidence in the United States sent inflation fears into the bond market, causing bond prices to drop. However, the dollar recovered somewhat with bonds later in the day.
A perception that the Tankan report will show strong economic growth has buoyed the yen in recent days.
"There have been some surprisingly good earnings reports and people are beginning to revive their expectations for Japan," said Christopher Low, senior economist at HSBC Markets in New York.
Late in the day, the dollar recovered somewhat against the yen in what Lizbeth Goldberg, assistant vice president at Hypo Bank, described as a "drift, more than anything else," without any fundamental influences.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Monday, were: 5.0590 French francs, up from 5.0495; 1.1933 Swiss francs, up from 1.1932; 1,512.90 Italian lire, up from 1,510.50; and 1.3680 Canadian dollars, down from 1.3707.

HEADLINE: Consumer Economic Confidence Is Up, Reaching a Six-Year High

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Consumers' faith in the economy surged in August for a second consecutive month, reaching a new six-year high and raising doubts that the economy is headed for a slowdown.
Economists, who had been expecting a decline in the economic barometer, said news of the unexpected optimism yesterday could give the Federal Reserve Board more reason to raise interest rates by the end of the year. 
 "It's not going to be the straw that broke the camel's back, but it's another piece of anecdotal evidence that maybe the economy isn't slowing as much as the markets and the Fed may have hoped for," said Kevin Flanagan, a money market economist with Dean Witter Reynolds Inc.
The Conference Board, the private business research group based in New York, said its Consumer Confidence Index rose 2.4 points, to 109.4 this month. Most economists had expected a decline to about 105 in the survey's index.
The increase was especially surprising coming after a sharp 7-point gain in July, indicating consumer sentiment may be "on an upward trend after remaining stagnant for more than a year," said Lynn Franco, associate director of the board's Consumer Research Center.
The statistics reinforced recent reports of strength in the job market, retail sales and housing. Economists said the rosy consumer outlook, if confirmed by further signs of vigor, could derail a predicted slowdown in 1996's second half, and at the same time raise warning signs of inflation.
That, in turn, could prompt the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates as early as its next policy-making meeting, on Sept. 24.
The central bankers said at their early July meeting, when they left rates unchanged, that they expected robust second-quarter economic growth to "slow appreciably over the second half of the year." The minutes of the meeting were released last Friday.

HEADLINE: U.S. Demands Vesco's Return From Cuba, but It Is Unlikely

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
A day after a Cuban court convicted Robert L. Vesco, the fugitive American financier, of economic crimes, the Clinton Administration demanded again today that he be returned to face trial at home, though officials acknowledged that the chances seemed remote at best.
"As long as Vesco remains in Cuba, and the Cuban Government refuses to return him to the U.S., there's not much that can be done," a State Department spokesman, John R. Dinger, said today. 
 Mr. Vesco fled the United States a quarter-century ago to avoid a series of indictments on charges of fraud and racketeering. He spent 13 or 14 years living in exile under the patronage of President Fidel Castro until his arrest last year on charges of "being a provocateur and an agent of foreign special services," although he was eventually convicted for fraud and "illicit economic activity."
Mr. Vesco, now 60, was convicted in a Cuban court on Monday and sentenced to 13 years in prison, while his wife, Lidia Alfonso Llauger, was convicted of lesser charges and sentenced to 9 years.
The Cuban Government accused Mr. Vesco of a complex scheme to cheat Cuba's Health Ministry and investors including a company run by Mr. Castro's nephew, by trying to market an unproven drug to fight cancer and arthritis.
In his trial, Mr. Vesco denied any guilt, saying he would not defraud a country that had harbored him for so many years, even as he was being indicted on new, unrelated charges in the United States.
Although declining to comment specifically on Mr. Vesco's trial, the State Department sharply criticized Cuba's criminal justice system, saying it did not meet "international standards of fair, public trials."
That is not to say that officials expressed much sympathy for Mr. Vesco's plight, although the State Department did seek access to him while he was in custody, as is routine when Americans are tried abroad. "We remain interested in Vesco's return to face prosecution here," Mr. Dinger said.
The United States has an extradition treaty with Cuba that dates to 1904, but it has not been invoked since the Castro takeover in 1959. Although the Cuban Government hinted that it might hand over Mr. Vesco shortly after his arrest, it has since ignored repeated requests to do so.
All along, officials in Washington speculated that Cuba would never turn over someone reputed to have information linking Cuban officials to criminal activities, like drug smuggling. Moreover, relations between the United States and Cuba have deteriorated in the last year -- with the shooting down of two civilian planes carrying members of the group Brothers to the Rescue and the passage of a law aimed at curbing foreign investment in Cuba -- making a good-will gesture even less likely.
In the United States, Mr. Vesco faces six Federal indictments in New York and Florida dating back to 1973, when he was accused of skimming more than $200 million from a mutual fund based in Geneva and of making an illegal $200,000 contribution to the re-election campaign of President Nixon. He also faced civil charges brought by the Securities and Exchange Commission.
After he fled the United States and finally settled in Cuba, he was also charged in 1984 with cocaine trafficking and again in 1989 for conspiring to smuggle drugs with Carlos Lehder Rivas, the leader of a drug cartel in Colombia.
While Mr. Vesco may appeal his conviction to a higher court in Cuba, the long sentence appeared to all but rule out the possibility of a return to the United States. But John K. Russell, a spokesman for the Justice Department, said, "We always have hope."

HEADLINE: Beijing Journal;
History's Fellow Travelers Cling to Mao's Road

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: BEIJING

 BODY:
There was a time, nearly 50 years ago, when revolutionary fervor in China attracted left-leaning Americans, with its mantras of self-reliance and manual labor, and its promise of achieving a socialist dream.
Joan Hinton, for one, gave up a promising career as a nuclear physicist in 1948 to come and work on a farm, enduring severe hardship in the name of a political ideal. Most Chinese who labored beside her believed in the same dreams, although they abandoned them when other options -- fresh vegetables, telephones and television -- eventually came along. 
 Ms. Hinton, however, is still here, working at a dairy on the outskirts of Beijing. She toils on, a practicing believer in proletarian values, even though almost everything she has worked for in China has been washed away by an onslaught of change, much of it economic.
"I always say you need three things to survive in China," said Ms. Hinton, relaxing in jeans and army-issue green sneakers. "A sense of humor, a sense of history and a sense of struggle. If we didn't know how to struggle, we'd still be up in the trees."
Ms. Hinton is one of a handful of veteran leftists from the United States and Europe who remained in China through years of political turmoil, and are disillusioned by the transformation that has both brought the nation's economy to life and allowed an array of social ills to flourish.
Once oddities only because they stood out physically, these "foreign experts," as the Chinese Goverment still classifies them, even though some have become Chinese citizens, attract attention for the way they cling to political beliefs that most Chinese abandoned years ago.
Sidney Shapiro, 80, a lawyer from Brooklyn who came to China in 1947, and Ruth Weiss, 87, an Austrian who moved here in 1951, say they still believe that socialism will somehow prevail, even though, in China, it seems further out of reach each day.
Among the dozen or so long-staying "foreign experts," however, none are as hard-core in their devotion to Maoist ideals as Ms. Hinton, 74, and her husband, Sid Engst, 76. And yet their admiration for the militant leftism that erupted in the Cultural Revolution of the late 1960's and early 70's, and their disdain for the changes that followed, do not seem to have dampened their enthusiasm for living here.
With her husband, and with the three children who came along the way, Ms. Hinton lived in rural Shaanxi Province for 18 years -- first turning old scrap metal into cooking utensils, then raising cattle and then ducks -- before moving to the Beijing area, living on a commune and, after it was disbanded, settling in at the dairy. The grown children no longer live in China.
Ms. Hinton speaks nostalgically of "the 30 years" -- 1949 to 1979 -- a period that many Chinese see as essentially wrong-headed in its efforts to mold human nature to be something it is not.
"It was like that journalist who went to Russia and said 'I've seen the future and it works,' " said Ms. Hinton, referring to Lincoln Steffens's trip in the 1920's. "That's how I see the 30 years. It worked. There was very little stealing, there was great community spirit, there was a common goal. That's all been thrown away now, except for the memory."
Ms. Hinton, whose mother founded the Putney School in Vermont, began her professional career in the 1940's as a nuclear physicist at Los Alamos to help produce the first atomic bomb in 1945. She became disillusioned as the cold war began, and quit physics.
After coming to China in 1948 to join Mr. Engst in an area "liberated" by Communist soldiers, she was accused by McCarthy-era Americans of being a spy who spilled nuclear secrets to China.
Ms. Hinton giggles girlishly as she shows a visitor a laminated copy of a magazine article about her, "The Spy Who Got Away," with a drawing that depicts her as a Lauren Bacall look-alike in a trenchcoat, scribbling in a notebook as she observes a nuclear test in the desert.
"I never looked that good," said Ms. Hinton, her blond hair now silver. Nor, she said, did she ever use her physics expertise in China.
If they cling to political views that sound antiquated in today's China, Ms. Hinton and Mr. Engst are acute critics of problems in Chinese society.
"It's a mess," said Mr. Engst. "Nobody listens to the central government anymore. They keep putting out laws, and nobody listens. You can pass all the laws you want, it makes no difference."
Mr. Shapiro, a translator whose Brooklyn accent is still healthy after 49 years in China, similarly mourns the rise of crime and corruption, and the loss of the communal spirit he once knew in Beijing. Yet he blames something deeper than current policy.
"The great flaw of Chinese society has been recognized: It is not Socialism or Communism, but Confucianism," said Mr. Shapiro, who became a Chinese citizen in 1963. "That means blind worship of authority, which has been used very much to the advantage and to the disadvantage of the Communist Party over the years."
Ms. Weiss, a retired translator, seems perplexed by what she sees out her window at the Friendship Hotel, where she lives alone in an apartment cluttered with Chinese knickknacks.
"In the 1950's, there was a real feeling of cohesion among people," she said. "Now, everything you have to pay, no one takes responsibility for anything. For me, Mao Zedong was always a father figure. I thought he was really interested in the welfare of the people. Obviously, he wasn't."
Ms. Hinton, however, is still a believer in Mao.
"Mao started the Cultural Revolution to cure the disparity between the few and the many," said Ms. Hinton. "How could that be wrong?"
She dismisses the current view, that Mao's misguided policies led to the deaths of more than 30 million people in the famine of 1960-62, as revisionist history.
"We were in the countryside then, and there was malnutrition, not starvation," said Ms. Hinton. "Without socialism, we would have starved. We banded together, sharing grain coupons."
Mr. Engst chimed in: "The Cultural Revolution achieved a lot. It identified the main problem, which is the dark side of the party. It just didn't succeed in eradicating it."
The "dark side" of the party means those led by Deng Xiaoping, who championed the transformation of the last 17 years.
In fact, Mr. Engst had a condition for being interviewed: "You have to promise not to describe us as pro-Deng Xiaoping or pro-reform."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Austrian born, Ruth Weiss has lived in China nearly 50 years. She is one of a group of Westerners who remain loyal to Mao's vision of China. (Seth Faison/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Business Travel;
Despite increased security at airports, there have been surprisingly few long lines or delays.

BYLINE: By Edwin McDowell 

 BODY:
AIRLINE passengers have been pleasantly surprised that their trips to the airport have been more or less routine, even in the aftermath of the crash last month of Trans World Airlines Flight 800. Despite heightened security procedures, there have been few long lines or delays.
More passengers are being questioned more often, and passengers who fly frequently report that carry-on luggage is inspected far more systemically than in the past. Laptop computers, cellular phones and other carry-on electronic items are virtually certain to be inspected. 
 But lines to pass through security checkpoints have been infrequent, except for the busiest seasonal flights, for which the delays have little to do with tightened security.
"Passengers understand and agree with the need to come to the airport prepared, including having a photo ID," said Victoria E. Pannell, the spokeswoman for the Airlines Council International. "At most airports, we're only talking about 10-to- 15-minute delays at most." Members of the council operate about 850 airports worldwide, from such giants as the three New York-area airports to Yuma International Airport in Yuma, Ariz.
Los Angeles International Airport and Chicago's O'Hare International Airport, the nation's busiest, have reported a relatively smooth flow of passengers. "We haven't had any significant delays," said Megan Hughes, a spokeswoman for the Chicago Department of Aviation. "If we see lines starting to form at some terminals we've opened more checkpoints to accommodate passengers."
Airport spokesmen reiterate that the "new" security procedures are not new at all, merely a tightening of procedures decreed last year after the Oklahoma City bombing and the conviction of terrorists in the World Trade Center bombing. Until recently at many airports, laptops were given only perfunctory examinations and corporate ID cards alone were readily accepted as proof of identity. While security is still far from airtight, as some enterprising reporters have demonstrated, frequent travelers say that security checks are no longer perfunctory.
Despite the lack of delays, airport officials are still asking that passengers arrive two hours before domestic flight times and three hours before international departures. They cite the willingness of most passengers to do so as a major factor in keeping security lines to a minimum.
 
Panama Project
Eager to attract tourists, Panama has signed an agreement with a United States-South Korean consortium to build what it says will be the biggest single tourist investment in Central America and the Caribbean -- a $300 million resort complex at Fort Amador, the 207-acre United States Army base. The base, built to help protect the Panama Canal, is being phased out by the end of the year.
The consortium, United Enterprise Trust Group, with headquarters in Seoul, has agreed to build a 500-room luxury hotel on an island in Fort Amador connected by a causeway to the mainland. It will also build a monorail system, a shopping center and 300 time-share apartments. Meanwhile the Interoceanic Region Authority of Panama, which is responsible for administering United States properties reverting to Panama under terms of the Panama Canal treaty, will spend an additional $50 million on infrastructure, hoping to attract investors to build a cruise ship port at the mouth of the canal.
But there is no need to make reservations just yet. Construction on the various projects is not scheduled to begin until early next year.
 
Reservations Update
On the subject of reservations, with airlines and hotels enjoying some of their most profitable months in years, spokesmen for both industries warn that travelers who wait until the last minute to make bookings risk missing out on preferred flights or a favorite hotel room. Travel agents also say this is the first time in years they have felt compelled to warn clients to book as far in advance as possible.
And flights that are available on short notice often require high fares. When Gordon Lambourne, a spokesman for Marriott International, had to change his flight from Washington to San Diego recently, the only nonstop available was a flight that required rerouting and at a much higher fare. As a result he flew Southwest Airlines from Baltimore-Washington International Airport, stopping at Nashville and changing planes in Phoenix, and on the return flight from San Diego he stopped at Las Vegas, Nev., and changed planes in Nashville.
While fall is rarely as busy as summer, industry officials say flights and hotel rooms will be in greater demand than perhaps any fall this decade. They say this is not just because of the growing popularity of traditional foliage tours, college homecomings, parents' weekends, baseball playoffs and the like, but above all because of an improving economy. Mr. Lambourne said that Marriott's fall bookings in many major cities were "already at an all-time high."

HEADLINE: Appeals Panel Lifts a Bar to Lloyd's Plan

BYLINE: By JOSEPH B. TREASTER 

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, Aug. 27

 BODY:
A Federal appeals court here cleared away a major legal obstacle today to the financial recovery plan for Lloyd's of London, the venerable insurance organization that has been crippled by billions in losses.
Lloyd's officials said they would begin to put the plan into effect and hoped to receive the approval of the British Government within a few days. 
 The plan -- which would split off Lloyd's losses into a new company -- had been put in jeopardy by a lower court's ruling last Friday that would have extended for two months the deadline for acceptance by American investors. Lloyd's argued that it needed a commitment on the plan by noon tomorrow to avoid financial collapse.
The recovery plan would force the company's "names" -- the individuals who pledge their assets to back Lloyd's policies -- to pay as much as $150,000 to capitalize a new company that would take on all of Lloyd's liabilities.
Today, the appeals court dismissed a suit by the American investors, ruling that they had agreed when they signed up with Lloyd's to sue only in the British courts.
"We've now got all the legal obstacles out of the way," said Peter Lane, managing director of Lloyd's in North America, moments after Judge Paul V. Niemeyer read the decision in the modern stucco-and-dark wood-trimmed courtroom.
Mr. Lane said that Lloyd's intended to stick with its deadline Wednesday, but that because of "honest confusion" that may have arisen among American investors over the weekend, the insurance organization would continue to accept their support for the recovery plan for another day or so.
He said the 18-member managing body of Lloyd's would determine on Thursday if the plan had sufficient backing from investors to go ahead. Next, he said, the rescue package would be presented for the approval of British Government officials, which is considered likely.
The plan would create a company called Equitas with funds of $18 billion. The new company would be responsible for current and anticipated claims. To participate, Lloyd's 34,000 names around the world are being asked to pay up to $150,000 and to relinquish any claims on future profits at Lloyd's or Equitas.
When losses began mounting in the late 1980's and early 1990's, reaching a peak of $12 billion in 1992, thousands of investors sued. Investors joining the recovery plan must now abandon these suits.
Lloyd's officials in London said that by late today 82 percent of its 34,000 names, including 53 percent of the Americans, had accepted the plan. But Mr. Lane said it was not immediately clear whether that level of acceptance would yield the payments needed by Lloyd's and whether enough of the legal challenges would be disposed of so that the establishment of Equitas could proceed. One reason for the uncertainty, other Lloyd's officials said, was that the size of the payments required of individual investors varied and a tally was not expected until Wednesday or Thursday.
While the recovery plan may proceed, it is not clear whether Lloyd's will face further legal challenges in the United States.
In the courtroom today, A. Stephens Clay, the lead lawyer representing 93 American investors in the suit, told reporters, "I'm sure this is not the end of the case by any means."
But he said he would need to read the full opinion of the appellate judges before determining his next move. That opinion is not likely to be written for at least a day or so, a court official indicated.
Mr. Clay had argued that the investments made by his clients as well as the creation of Equitas involved transactions subject to the standards of United States securities laws -- that is, much more disclosure of financial details than Lloyd's had been willing to provide.
Even today, Lloyd's was unyielding on the financial data.
At one point, referring to the financial information sought by the names, Judge Diana Gribbon Motz told a Lloyd's lawyer, Harvey L. Pitt: "What you're saying is, 'We don't want to give it to them.' "
No, Mr. Pitt said: "It would not be meaningful to them."
To which, the third judge, M. Blane Michael, interjected: "That's not what they say."
But for the judges, the crucial point was not the matter of disclosure, but the fact that all of Lloyd's investors, in the United States and worldwide, had signed contracts agreeing to settle any disputes with Lloyd's in the British courts.
In its order today, the court said its decision rested on the belief that the contract conceding British jurisdiction should be enforced. Several Federal district courts had reached the same conclusion earlier. So it surprised Lloyd's when District Judge Robert Payne had ruled against them on that point, as well as on the issue of disclosure, last Friday.
When the decision was announced, a colleague hugged Mr. Pitt, and Mr. Lane said, as he headed out of the room, "Clearly it's the right decision."
Across the courtroom, Mr. Clay and his colleagues had fallen silent. They had been ready for a setback, such as a lifting of Judge Payne's injunction that extended the deadline for acceptance. But they had hoped that Judge Payne's order for Lloyd's to produce more financial details and to debate the issues at a trial in November would stand. Instead, the appeals court dismissed Mr. Clay's entire case.
The judges conducted a feisty review of the case, giving each side about an hour to speak and peppering the lawyers with questions. Mr. Pitt had gone first and when he sat down, it seemed to many in the courtroom that the judges were anything but favorably disposed toward him. But then Mr. Clay took the podium, and the judges went after him.

HEADLINE: Municipal Elections in Bosnia Postponed, Raising Doubts About Troop Pullout

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES 

DATELINE: SARAJEVO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The Western official in charge of the September elections in Bosnia today postponed the vote for municipal officers around the country, citing "widespread abuse of rules and regulations."
Monitors have recently documented a pattern of abuses, especially by Bosnian Serb officials, in which refugees were directed to register for voting in strategically important towns, in an effort to consolidate the Serbs' control of Bosnian towns that were once ethnically mixed. 
 Even as he put off the elections for local leaders, the official, Robert Frowick, said the the vote should proceed on Sept. 14 for a three-member shared national presidency, a national legislature and separate Muslim-Croat and Bosnian Serb regional legislatures because the detected abuses would have less serious effects in those races.
Still, the delay raised fears in Western capitals and among NATO commanders, who are supposed to begin pulling out part of the 53,000-member peacekeeping force by the end of the year.
Concerns about the timetable for the troop withdrawal are especially acute in Washington, where President Clinton faces his own election in November and has pledged that American soldiers will be largely out of Bosnia by year's end.
At a news conference early this afternoon Mr. Frowick, a retired American diplomat who is the representative of the Organization for Security and Economic Cooperation in Europe, said that in view of the harsh winter conditions that visit Bosnia by December, it would be unrealistic to reschedule the local elections before next spring.
But later in the afternoon -- after he had telephone consultations with Washington -- Mr. Frowick said in an interview that it was likely that the municipal elections could go ahead before the departure of the peacekeeping force at the end of December.
In the interview, he insisted that the problems that led to the postponement of the local vote could be rectified in the coming weeks.
Senior NATO officers, calling the municipal vote the most volatile, say they need to be at full strength when it takes place. Many Muslims who were displaced during the war, for example, have said they want to return to their former homes, now in Serb hands, to vote. Such moves are likely to lead to clashes.
"The Dayton agreement," Mr. Frowick said, referring to the Bosnian peace accord negotiated last year in Dayton, Ohio, "said that these municipal and cantonal elections should take place if feasible, but then it did not say what to do if we decide they are not feasible. There is a gap here that needs to be filled."
In Washington, Assistant Secretary of State John C. Kornblum expressed support for the decision, saying that the evidence of manipulation of voting lists was too egregious to ignore.
In addition to Bosnian Serb efforts to pack the voting rolls in some towns, Western monitors have reported other problems with the election preparations including confusing registration lists, intimidation of opposition candidates and a refusal by state-run television stations and newspapers to give all candidates a voice.
Bosnian Serbs, who denounced today's decision, said they would hold their own municipal elections. Mr. Frowick and Mr. Kornblum both said that any such vote would be considered invalid. Officials of the Muslim-Croat Federation, who had complained loudly about the Serb tactics, welcomed the move to delay the municipal voting.
Mr. Frowick said the countries involved in the peace effort in Bosnia would hold meetings in early September to determine how to handle the postponement.
"We are trying to do too much in too short a time frame," Mr. Frowick said. "The principle objective behind all this is to see if there is a way if we can strengthen the integrity of the whole process."
Speaking of the irregularities that he felt would hinder a fair vote, Mr. Frowick said, "They're across the board; they're too pervasive; and that's what drove this decision."
It was unclear why many of these problems should affect the local and not the national vote. Mr. Frowick, insisting that tensions were greater in local contests, said the electoral guidelines have "been seriously distorted at the municipal level."
"The real problems, the searing problems of the country, are centered on the municipalities," he said.
American and European officials agree that the vote will not be completely free and fair, given the continued presence of hard-line nationalists running the three ethnic enclaves in Bosnia. But they argue that they will still help rebuild a unified country. Critics say they will only give legitimacy to Bosnia's ethnic partition.
Under terms of the Dayton agreement, Bosnia is divided into two substates: the Muslim-Croat Federation, which controls 51 percent of Bosnia, and the Bosnian Serb republic, which controls 49 percent.
Jeffrey Fisher, a senior European official, said the numerous logistical problems in the municipal election also made it impossible to proceed with the vote as scheduled.
"Fifty-five municipalities have been split by the ethnic boundary line," he said, presenting unresolved practical difficulties such as where to locate the polling places. In addition, he cited such problems as discrepancies in the number of candidates, a failure of some parties to field candidates and voter confusion about the process.
In Washington, Mr. Kornblum said the postponement of the local elections would not be a decisive factor in any decision on whether a second NATO force will replace the current one after December, and whether the American troops should play a part in any such force. He said such a decision will not be made for many weeks and only after consultation with NATO allies.
Mr. Kornblum also said the Pentagon has issued no orders to the 16,000 American troops in Bosnia to prepare them to stay past December. About 5,000 Army troops in Germany have begun preparing to head to Bosnia to provide extra security for the planned withdrawal of American soldiers at the end of the year.
The issue is to be discussed at a meeting of NATO Defense Ministers in Norway in September. Defense Secretary William J. Perry has said that if NATO decides a continued peacekeeping mission is necessary he expects American troops will be part of it.
The Bosnian Serbs have, in a move that has seen the Muslims threaten to boycott the elections, taken advantage of the current rules that allow voters to register where they lived in 1991, where they reside now or where they plan to live.
Because of their differing goals, it had been expected that many Bosnian Serb refugees from areas now controlled by the Government would choose to live and vote inside Serb-controlled territory.
Diplomats note, for example, that there were 80,000 Serbs in Sarajevo before the war, but less than 40 have actually registered to vote in their old city, now controlled by the Muslim-led Bosnian Government.
But recent evidence indicates that Serb leaders have gone beyond that, packing the voter rolls in towns with particular strategic importance like Brcko or Srebrenica, with thousands of registrants who have no intention of living in those towns.

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS;
Excerpts From Platform Adopted at Democratic Convention

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the platform adopted last night at the Democratic National Convention.
INTRODUCTION 
 In 1996, America will choose the President who will lead us from the millennium which saw the birth of our nation, and into a future that has all the potential to be even greater than our magnificent past. Today's Democratic Party is ready for that future. Our vision is simple. We want an America that gives all Americans the chance to live out their dreams and achieve their God-given potential. We want an America that is still the world's strongest force for peace and freedom. And we want an America that is coming together around our enduring values, instead of drifting apart. . . .
 OPPORTUNITY
 For 220 years, America has been defined by a single ideal: Opportunity for all who take the responsibility to seize it. President Andrew Jackson put it best: We believe in equal opportunity for all, and special privileges for none. The mission of the Democratic Party in 1996 is to insure that the great American Dream of opportunity for all is within reach for all and that it travels with us, whole and intact, as we walk together into tomorrow.
 Economic Growth
 . . . In the next four years we have to make the new economy work for all Americans: balancing the budget, creating more jobs, making sure all families can count on good health care and a secure retirement, and, most of all, expanding educational opportunities. . . .
 President Clinton has put forward a plan to balance the budget by 2002 while living up to our commitments to our elderly and our children and maintaining strong economic growth. The Republican Congress' own economists admit the President's plan will balance the budget by 2002. It cuts hundreds of wasteful and outdated programs, but it preserves Medicare and Medicaid, it protects education and the environment, and it defends working families. The President's plan reflects America's values. The Republican plan does not. . . .
 We insist that any tax cuts are completely paid for, because we are determined to balance the budget.
 We want to strengthen middle-class families by providing a $500 tax cut for children. We want to cut taxes to help families pay for education after high school and to guarantee the first two years of college. We want people to be able to use their I.R.A.'s to buy a first home, deal with a medical emergency, or provide for education. We want to cut taxes for small businesses that invest in the future and set up pensions for their workers. And we want to cut taxes for people who are self-employed and self-insured so their health care is more affordable. . . .
 We believe that if we want the American economy to continue strong growth, we must continue to expand trade, and not retreat from the world. America's markets are open to the world, so America has a right to demand that the world's markets are open to our products. . . .
 Education
 . . . Every child should be able to read by the end of the third grade. Students should be required to demonstrate competency and achievement for promotion or graduation. Teachers in this country are among the most talented professionals we have. They should be required to meet high standards for professional performance and be rewarded for the good jobs they do. . . .
Schools should be held accountable for results. We should redesign or overhaul schools that fail. We should expand public school choice, but we should not take American tax dollars from public schools and give them to private schools. . . .
 . . . We should expand work-study so one million students a year can work their way through college by the year 2000. We should allow people to use money from their I.R.A. to help pay for college. We should give a $1,000 honor scholarship for the top 5 percent of graduates in every high school. And we must make 14 years of education the standard for every American. The Democratic Party wants to create a $10,000 tax deduction for families to help pay for education after high school. And we want to create a $1,500 tax cut for Americans, modeled after Georgia's successful Hope scholarships, to guarantee the first year of tuition at a typical community college, and the second year if individuals earn it by maintaining a B average. . . .
 Economic Security
 
 . . . We need to find new ways to help working families find economic security: better training to help workers learn skills to get new and better jobs; the security of good health care and safe pensions so they can take care of themselves and their families. This is a challenge that American workers and managers are ready to face, and the Democratic Party will continue to tackle.
 . . . In the next four years, we must take further steps to insure that Americans have access to quality, affordable health care. We should start by making sure that people get help paying premiums so they do not lose health care while they're looking for a new job. We support expanded coverage of home care, hospice, adult day-care, and community-based services, so the elderly and people with disabilities of all ages can live in their own communities and as independently as possible. . . .
 RESPONSIBILITY
 Today's Democratic Party knows that the era of big government is over. Big bureaucracies and Washington solutions are not the real answers to today's challenges. We need a smaller government, and we must have a larger national spirit. Government's job should be to give people the tools they need to make the most of their own lives.
 Fighting Crime
 . . . Nothing is more effective in the fight against crime than police officers on the beat, engaged in community policing. The Crime Bill is putting 100,000 new police officers on the street. We deplore cynical Republican attempts to undermine our promise to America to put 100,000 new police officers on the street. We pledge to stand up for our communities and stand with our police officers by opposing any attempt to repeal or weaken this effort. But we know that community policing only works when the community works with the police. We echo the President's challenge to Americans: If 50 citizens joined each of America's 20,000 neighborhood watch groups, we would have a citizen force of one million strong. . . .
 We must do everything we can to stand behind our police officers, and the first thing we should do is pass a ban on cop-killer bullets. Any bullet that can rip through a bulletproof vest should be against the law. . . .
 
 Immigration
 . . . As we work to stop illegal immigration, we call on all Americans to avoid the temptation to use this issue to divide people from each other. We deplore those who use the need to stop illegal immigration as a pretext for discrimination. . . .
Democrats want to protect American jobs by increasing criminal and civil sanctions against employers who hire illegal workers, but Republicans continue to favor inflammatory rhetoric over real action. We will continue to enforce labor standards to protect workers in vulnerable industries. We continue to firmly oppose welfare benefits for illegal immigrants. We believe family members who sponsor immigrants into this country should take financial responsibility for them, and be held legally responsible for supporting them.
 Welfare Reform
 . . . Republicans wanted to eliminate the guarantee of health care for the poor, the elderly and the disabled. They were wrong, and we stopped them. Republicans wanted to destroy the food stamp and school lunch programs that provide basic nutrition to millions of working families and poor children. They were wrong, and we stopped them. Republicans wanted to gut child abuse prevention and foster care. They were wrong, and we stopped them. Republicans wanted to cut off young, unwed mothers -- because they actually thought their children would be better off living in an orphanage. They were dead wrong, and we stopped them. The bill Republicans in Congress passed last year was values-backward; it was soft on work and tough on children, and we applaud the President for stopping it.
 We know the new bill passed by Congress is far from perfect -- parts of it should be fixed because they go too far and have nothing to do with welfare reform. . . . Choice
 The Democratic Party stands behind the right of every woman to choose, consistent with Roe v. Wade, and regardless of ability to pay. President Clinton took executive action to make sure that the right to make such decisions is protected for all Americans. Over the last four years, we have taken action to end the gag rule and insure safety at family planning and women's health clinics. We believe it is a fundamental constitutional liberty that individual Americans -- not government -- can best take responsibility for making the most difficult and intensely personal decisions regarding reproduction. . . .
 We support contraceptive research, family planning, comprehensive family life education and policies that support healthy childbearing. . . .
 Reinventing Government
 . . . For years, Republicans talked about making government smaller while letting it grow -- Democrats are doing it. For years, Republicans talked about cutting the deficit while letting it climb -- Democrats are doing it. For years, Republicans talked about shifting power back to states and communities -- Democrats are doing it. For years, Republicans talked about making government more businesslike and efficient -- Democrats are doing it. . . .
 Political Reform
 . . . The President and the Democratic Party support the bipartisan McCain-Feingold campaign finance reform bill. It will limit campaign spending, curb the influence of PAC's and lobbyists and end the soft-money system. Perhaps most important of all, this bill provides free TV time for candidates. . . .
 SECURITY, FREEDOM,
 AND PEACE

 . . . Four years ago, there was good reason to worry that the world was dividing into separate, isolated, regional trading blocks. Today, thanks to Clinton Administration efforts to find new markets for American products and strengthen our existing ties, America's relations with our trading parties around the world are stronger than ever. . . .
 
 Strengthening Our Security
 . . . Today's Democratic Party is committed to strengthening our military and adapting it to new challenges; reducing the threat of weapons of mass destruction; and meeting new challenges to our security such as terrorism, international crime and drug trafficking.
 . . . The Democratic Party is committed to a strong and balanced National Missile Defense (N.M.D.) program. The Administration is spending $3 billion a year on six different systems to protect our troops in the field and our allies from short and medium range missiles. To prepare for the possibility of a long-range missile attack on American soil by a rogue state, the Clinton Administration is committed to developing by the year 2000 a defensive system that could be deployed by 2003, well before the threat becomes real. The Democratic Party opposes the Republican N.M.D. plan -- spending up to $60 billion on a revival of the "Star Wars" program that would force us to choose a costly system today that could be obsolete tomorrow. . . .
 COMMUNITY
 . . . Putting Families First
. . . After 12 years of all family-values talk and no family-values action by the Republicans, President Clinton took office determined to put families first. We support the fundamental themes of the Families First Agenda -- promoting paycheck, health care, retirement, and personal security; creating greater educational and economic opportunity, and requiring greater responsibility from individuals, businesses and government. We believe parents should be able to take unpaid leave from work and choose flex time so they can do their job as parents: to do things like go to parent-teacher conferences or take a child to the doctor. . . .
 America is the leading exporter of intellectual property built on a strong foundation of artistic freedom. We are proud to have stopped the Republican attack on the Corporation for Public Broadcasting; we want our children to watch "Sesame Street," not "Power Rangers." And we echo the President's call to the entertainment industry: Work harder to develop and promote movies, music and TV shows that are suitable -- and educational -- for children. . . .
 Protecting Our Environment
 Today's Democratic Party wants all Americans to be able to enjoy America's magnificent natural heritage, and we want our people to know that the air they breathe is pure, the water they drink is clean, and the land they live on is safe from hazard. . . .
 Environmental protection should include more education on compliance for small and medium-sized business, more strategies to increase compliance for all businesses, and tough enforcement -- including criminal prosecution -- for those who put human health and the environment at risk.
 The American Community
 . . . Today's Democratic Party knows we must renew our efforts to stamp out discrimination and hatred of every kind, wherever and whenever we see it. . . .
We believe everyone in America should learn English so they can fully share in our daily life, but we strongly oppose divisive efforts like English-only legislation, designed to erect barriers between us and force people away from the culture and heritage of which they are rightly proud. We must remember we do not have an American to waste.
We continue to lead the fight to end discrimination on the basis of race, gender, religion, age, ethnicity, disability, and sexual orientation. The Democratic Party has always supported the Equal Rights Amendment, and we are committed to ensuring full equality for women and to vigorously enforce the Americans with Disabilities Act. We support continued efforts, like the Employment Non-Discrimination Act, to end discrimination against gay men and lesbians. . . .
 We know there is still more we can do to einsure equal opportunity for all Americans, so all people willing to work hard can build a strong future. President Clinton is leading the way to reform affirmative action so that it works, it is improved, and promotes opportunity, but does not accidentally hold others back in the process. Senator Dole has promised to end affirmative action. He's wrong, and the President is right. When it comes to affirmative action, we should mend it, not end it.

GRAPHIC: Photo: At the beginning of proceedings on the floor of the Democrats' convention, women protested for a woman's right to choose an abortion. (Keith Meyers/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Ex-Candidate to Stay True to Party

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: DENVER, Aug. 27

 BODY:
Professor Lamm spent his summer vacation running for President. Back on campus as practically a national household name, the failed Reform Party candidate vowed today to keep battling to break the two-party monopoly on American politics.
"There is a reform movement building in the U.S. that is far bigger than Ross Perot," the professor, Richard D. Lamm, said at the University of Denver, where he directs a public policy center. "I don't think that the role of the Reform Party is to sting like a bee and die." 
 On Wednesday, Mr. Lamm, a former three-term Governor of Colorado, is to announce in Chicago, where the Democrats are holding their convention, whether he will endorse Mr. Perot's Presidential candidacy.
While refusing to tip his hand today, Mr. Lamm said he planned to campaign this fall for Reform Party candidates across the nation. He said he would not endorse either President Clinton or Bob Dole, the Republican nominee.
At his office, Mr. Lamm said supporters had urged him to stay active with an eye to a repeat Presidential bid in the year 2000.
Showing little rancor after his six-week campaign against Mr. Perot, Mr. Lamm was relaxed today, dressed in a blue blazer and white slacks -- as if he was about to go yachting -- and let slip that he planned to go hiking in the Rockies this weekend.
Many of his supporters, however, are openly bitter over Mr. Perot's Aug. 17 victory in the Reform Party primary.
Mr. Perot "is a man who must be kept far, far away from the White House," Mr. Lamm's former spokesman, Eric Anderson, wrote this week in The Denver Post. "Perot and his employees have maintained that there was nothing wrong with the election, with the same sort of illogical insistence as tobacco executives who maintain that nicotine is not addictive."
As the Lamm-for-President office here closed today, Mr. Anderson ran through a list of complaints against Mr. Perot.
Reform Party officials first refused to give Lamm campaigners computer lists of the names and addresses of party members. After relenting on the list, the party officials wanted the right to pre-approve any Lamm mailings.
After Lamm workers had printed up signs for the party's convention, party officials ruled that they were too large. Before the primary vote, party officials mailed thousands of postcards reminding the party faithful to vote. The cards carried Mr. Perot's name and photograph but neither Mr. Lamm's name nor his photograph.
When the primary results were tabulated, 64,078 votes were declared invalid, more than the 49,302 that were declared valid. In the end, Mr. Perot won the nomination with the votes of only 2.8 percent of an officially estimated party membership of 1.13 million people.
In response to the complaints, Sharon Holman, a Perot spokeswoman, said today that Mr. Lamm "had a level playing field."
Bitterness over the nomination process has run so deep that two leaders in the party's largest state organization, that of California, are no longer in the party.
One of them, Mark Sturdevant, resigned on Monday as vice chairman of the Reform Party California. "I believe in the reform movement," Mr. Sturdevant said, "but I don't believe in the party revolving around one person."
The other, Ted Muga, was ousted last week as chairman of one of the party's largest county groups, in San Diego, after a vote by the group's board, which is controlled by Mr. Perot's supporters.
The board's resolution regarding Mr. Muga read, "He has the gall to call our founder, hero and favored, 'drafted' candidate, Perot, a 'dictator.' "
In response, Mr. Muga said in an interview: "The Dallas purge has started. I was the first one."
But, nationwide, no other major Lamm supporters are known to have left the party. A national caucus of party officials is scheduled for Sept. 28 in Schaumburg, Ill.
Mr. Lamm, a lifelong Democrat until this summer, said today that the major parties' conventions convinced him all the more of the necessity of a third party.
"We have Christopher Reeve calling for billions of dollars for more medical research, while 40 million Americans don't have health insurance," he said, referring to the speech by the paralyzed actor on Monday at the Democratic National Convention. "Every year, 600,000 American women give birth without pre-natal care. The Democrats have no concept of setting priorities, of making choices.
"Dole's 15 percent tax cut, and the Democrats' demagoging on health care, are the poster boys of the irresponsibilties in American politics. We cannot do politically what we need to do economically."
To get these issues before the American voters, Mr. Lamm said he hoped that Mr. Perot would be invited to join television debates with Mr. Clinton and Mr. Dole.
"I would endorse Ross Perot as the teach-in professor of America," Mr. Lamm said, adding that he and Mr. Perot had not spoken since the primary night.

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: THE DEMOCRAT;
Clinton Makes Stops in Midwest for Politics and Calls for Literacy Project

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: WYANDOTTE, Mich., Aug. 27

 BODY:
President Clinton barnstormed Ohio and Michigan today, mixing traditional Democratic politicking at a Jeep factory with the announcement of a new Government proposal to fight illiteracy. But the strains inside his coalition also became evident when an Ohio Congresswoman sharply criticized the Nafta trade pact before a crowd gathered to hear the President.
Mr. Clinton's literacy proposal is part of a series of child-focused initiatives that he hopes will appeal to suburban parents. 
 But an otherwise harmonious day of campaigning was interrupted when Representative Marcy Kaptur denounced the North American Free Trade Agreement, which Mr. Clinton pushed through Congress in 1993 over the protests of many Democrats, while she was warming up a crowd of workers at the Chrysler Jeep Factory in Toledo, Ohio.
The contrasting images from the third day of the President's train trip to the Democratic National Convention showed the distinctive mix of positions he has used this year to create an image as a values-oriented Democrat as well as the opposition of many more traditional Democrats to some of his policies.
At the Jeep factory, Ms. Kaptur -- who has been a vigorous campaigner for Mr. Clinton's re-election, despite her opposition to Nafta -- charged that while Mr. Clinton's trade policies were increasing car exports, they were allowing jobs in other fields to slip overseas. "Those immigrant workers of the Lower East Side, they have a right to work, just like our people here. Those 14,000 tomato workers in Florida that lost their jobs because of Nafta -- it's a crime! It's a crime!"
The President had expected some criticism from Ms. Kaptur over Nafta, said Michael D. McCurry, the White House press secretary. "It's a demonstration of the strength of the President's position," Mr. McCurry said, "that Democrats are willing to set aside some of their disagreements to rally to his candidacy."
The President's aides say the initiatives being put forward this week will add up to $8.45 billion in new programs or tax cuts through 2002. He will finish unveiling them in a convention address that will include a $3.4 billion program of tax incentives and grants to help provide jobs for people being forced off welfare benefits. Mr. Clinton plans to propose on Wednesday an environmental program costing about $2 billion.
To explain how the Federal budget would be balanced, White House officials today announced measures to offset the costs of the proposals, including a $5.3 billion tax increase on export sales by multinational corporations, $541 million in new fees on corporate jets and $500 million to be raised by auctioning part of the communications spectrum.
In Toledo, Mr. Clinton told cheering auto workers, "For the first time since the 1970's, America -- America -- is the No. 1 producer and seller of automobiles again in the entire world."
He gave only the most oblique nod to Ms. Kaptur's criticism, saying: "We want to lift countries up to our level. We don't want to see people dragged down to the lowest level in the global economy. We want it to lead to growth everywhere."
The workers greeted Mr. Clinton with cheers and applause. "When he took over, we were working on a week, off a week for 12 years," said Floyd King, a 58-year-old test driver. "Since President Clinton, we're been working to capacity because people are back at work and people could afford new cars."
Riding the rails into Michigan, another battleground state, Mr. Clinton switched themes at a rally outside a public library and talked about education, a topic aimed at the middle-class suburban parents who his strategists believe will be crucial to the outcome of the election.
"I have come here to this wonderful community to ask all of you to join me, without regard to your political party or your views on other issues, in a simple, straightforward, critical national goal," Mr. Clinton said. "All America's children should be able to read on their own by the third grade."
Playing to the crowd, Mr. Clinton listened, and even read along, as two towheaded children, Justin Whitney, 7, and Elizabeth Schweye, 9, read him "The Little Engine That Could."
He wants to spend $2.75 billion over five years in an effort to make all third graders literate. Of that, $1.45 billion would finance reading specialists for after-school tutoring, $1 billion would be used to assign 11,000 young people in the Americorps national service program to tutoring, and $300,000 would be used to help parents with poor reading skills learn to teach their children.
Tony Blankley, a spokesman for Speaker Newt Gingrich, scoffed at the President's education proposals, saying on the "Fox Morning News": "Bill Clinton is defending the status quo on education. He is beholden to the teachers' unions."

HEADLINE: Dole Tries To Focus On Economy

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: SANTA BARBARA, Calif., Aug. 27

 BODY:
Bob Dole took a break from sunbathing today to showcase his plan that he says is needed to perk up the economy -- even as new monthly figures showed that consumer confidence had reached a six-year high.
The Republican Presidential nominee, more deeply tanned than usual, fairly glowed as he met with his economic advisers and later held a round-table discussion with local business owners at a food distribution plant here to promote his plan to cut taxes and balance the budget at the same time. 
 Today's event came as the Conference Board's Consumer Confidence Index reported rising this month to its highest level since March 1990 -- an important economic indicator that clouds Mr. Dole's contention that the economy under President Clinton has flagged and needs Republican supply-side prodding to reinvigorate it.
Mr. Dole played down the report, saying consumer confidence "is just not there." At another point he told reporters: "You didn't look at the right facts. The economy's in the tank."
But if Mr. Dole doubted consumer confidence, he radiated his own, even in the face of mounting skepticism about him and his economic plan being played out on national television at the Democratic National Convention in Chicago.
At his economic round-table discussion, where his Vice-Presidential running mate, Jack Kemp, joined him, he seemed far more polished and engaged than he has at such outings earlier in his 18-month campaign.
"When you hear President Clinton say we can't do it, it's going to blow a hole in his deficit, in his budget -- it's already blown a hole in his lead, and I think that's his problem," said Mr. Dole, referring to Mr. Clinton's drop in public opinion polls since Mr. Dole announced his economic plan earlier this month.
John Taylor, an economist from Stanford University, said Mr. Dole's economic advisers expected the economic growth rate to slow next year to just 1.9 percent, down from this year's average of 2.4 percent. Mr. Dole has promised to raise it to 3.5 percent, and he asserted today that his plan would "free up the American dream, free up the American entrepreneur" to increase growth and productivity.
The candidate's confidence was also on display as he waded into crowds and signed autographs during a stroll down the main street of this coastal resort in search of Mexican food.
At one point, he passed a man talking on a pay phone with his wife, who was home in Santa Cruz.
"This is my wife, Amy," said the man, Ted Pearson, as he extended the receiver to the passing candidate. "Say 'Hi' to Amy."
Grabbing the phone, the 73-year-old candidate chirped, "Hey, Amy, how you doing?" Mr. Dole asked if she had registered to vote and betrayed no disappointment when she said she was a Democrat.
Later, Amy Pearson told The Santa Barbara News-Press that she had wanted to grill Mr. Dole about health care, but that she had missed her chance.
"I totally caved," Mrs. Pearson said. "I wished him luck. I meant it, but don't win."
But Mr. Dole, like a heedless teen-ager, brandished his greatest display of self-assurance when it came to soaking up the sun.
His favorite pursuit, at work or play, is getting tan, and he has devoted himself mightily to this task in the first two days of his four-day vacation here -- so much so that a passing dermatologist scolded him for not protecting himself better from the ravages of the rays.
Dr. Pamela Rand, the dermatologist, and her husband were guests at the Biltmore Hotel where Mr. Dole and his wife, Elizabeth, are staying. As the Rands posed for photographs with Mr. Dole, Dr. Rand asked him if he was wearing sun screen.
Mr. Dole said, "Yes, No. 4."
The doctor told him, "That's not good enough."
As the Doles made their way later to Joe's Cafe for dinner, along with Ken Khachigian, a top campaign adviser, and Donald Rumsfeld, Mr. Dole's top policy adviser, another bystander asked Mr. Dole about the amount of sun he was getting.
"I got about three hours this morning," he boasted. Pointing to his reddened nose, he added, "Look at this!"
Perhaps chastened by Dr. Rand's scolding, he admitted, "A dermatologist came by and said, 'You shouldn't be out there.' "
But the candidate, famous for ignoring the advice of his campaign advisers, gave no indication that he was any more prone to following the advice of the doctor. Before a rally tonight, he spent a few hours lounging in the sun.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1538 words

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: Man in the News;
'Four (12?) More Years': Albert Arnold Gore Jr.

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
"This two-headed monster of Dole-Gingrich," Vice President Al Gore declared on Sunday, a sparkle in his eyes, a sinister tone in his voice, his entire body in motion, "has launched an all-out assault on decades of progress on behalf of working men and women."
Mr. Gore had struck a chord. His friendly audience of labor delegates to the Democratic National Convention rose, and hissed and booed the Republican demons. 
 The Vice President stood on the podium with the kind of broad, natural grin that comes easily to most other politicians. Then the crowd began to chant, "Four more years!"
People who have watched Mr. Gore throughout his career had never seen him so animated. It was a good beginning to a crucial week for him. For four more years is not enough for Mr. Gore. He dreams of 8 or 12 more.
But to fulfill his dream of being elected President in the year 2000, the 48-year-old Mr. Gore may have to dispel the rap against him that his style is too flat and wooden to generate enthusiasm among voters.
The convention here is a big chance to do that. He has appearances scheduled across the city all week and two addresses to the convention itself, one on Wednesday night, just before President Clinton's name is placed in nomination, and again on Thursday night, when he himself accepts the nomination for another term as Vice President.
History is on his side. Except for Spiro T. Agnew, who resigned in disgrace, and Dan Quayle, whose political career may not yet be over, everyone else elected Vice President since 1952 has later won his party's nomination to be President.
Plenty of obstacles beyond his personality lie in Mr. Gore's path to the Presidential nomination four years from now.
First, of course, he and Mr. Clinton must win re-election in November. Then he must avoid being blamed for problems over which he will have little control that are sure to arise in the economy and elsewhere during the next four years.
"These are times," said Prof. Charles O. Jones, a political scientist at the University of Wisconsin, "when politicians are taking things away from voters -- cutting Medicare, balancing the budget -- rather than giving to them, and it's very difficult to remain popular."
But Mr. Gore does have an important advantage. More than any other Vice President, he has been deeply involved in White House decision-making. That would help him remain in public view during a second term and could help him protect his flanks politically.
"Al Gore clearly has taken the Vice Presidency to a new level," said Prof. Michael Nelson, a political scientist at Rhodes College in Memphis, who has made a specialty of studying the Vice Presidency. "It is clear to me that no other Vice President in history has enjoyed the same level of responsibility and good personal relations with the President."
With an office in the White House -- he calls it the Square Office -- less than 20 paces down the hall from the Oval Office, Mr. Gore has the freedom to pop in to see the President at will. Staff members say he does so many times a day. They say that he operates essentially as a very senior adviser and that in four years hardly a policy decision has been made in which he did not have a voice.
In addition, the President has delegated entire areas of responsibility to him, including environmental protection, telecommunications policy, the space program and trimming the size of the Government. The program Mr. Gore calls Reinventing Government had reduced Federal employment by 240,000 positions by the first of this year, according to the White House, making the number of civilian Federal employees the smallest since the Kennedy Administration.
Just last week Mr. Clinton gave the Vice President credit for stimulating Administration interest that led to the new policy cracking down on smoking by children, a subject that has long concerned Mr. Gore, whose only sibling -- his sister, Nancy, 10 years older -- was a heavy smoker who died of lung cancer in 1984.
Mr. Gore has also become deeply involved in managing policy with Russia. He set up what he calls a commission with the Russian Prime Minister, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, that brings together Cabinet secretaries of both countries to work on bilateral issues like trade and arms control.
Mr. Gore, who has traveled abroad more than 30 times since becoming Vice President, has also taken responsibility for relations with Egypt and South Africa.
Every task has been undertaken with an effort not to overshadow the President.
"Once Clinton realized that Gore was in no way going to be a rival," Professor Nelson said, "he was free to take full advantage of Gore's talents."
Mr. Gore's advice to the President has not always been accepted. For example, he urged the President to stand fast to the promise to allow homosexuals to serve openly in the military, and was said by White House assistants to favor more aggressive action in Bosnia than the President was willing to take.
Mr. Gore was also embarrassed in early 1994 when he championed the nomination of Bobby Ray Inman to be Secretary of Defense. Having agreed to take the post, Mr. Inman withdrew in the face of criticism, largely over his prior role as a member of the outside board of a corrupt arms company.
But Mr. Gore has never taken issue with the President publicly. In fact, he has been so loyal to Mr. Clinton that he has not publicly mentioned his own Presidential ambition since the day he was tapped in 1992 to run for Vice President on the Clinton ticket. This is noteworthy for a man who first announced his candidacy for President before he was 40 years old and whose friends say he is certainly no less ambitious than he was then, in 1988.
In an interview last week, Mr. Gore brushed off a question about running for President. "I define my purpose in this job very simply, to help Bill Clinton be the best President he's capable of being," he said.
"I don't see this as primarily a preparatory job," the Vice President continued. "I'm not focused on making the race myself in the future."
But he has never made any secret of wanting to be President, his questioner protested.
"No," Mr. Gore responded, "but I haven't said it since I've had this job. I don't want to be misleading, but I have not proactively said that to anyone."
That is true. But the thought of his being President has been in his mind and that of his family since he was a child. "We raised him for it," his father, Al Sr., a Democratic Representative and Senator from Tennessee for more than 30 years, said in an interview four years ago, on the day his son was picked to run for Vice President.
His father was in Congress when Albert Arnold Gore Jr. was born in Washington on March 31, 1948. As a child, Al Jr. lived in his parents' suite in a hotel on Embassy Row, attended private schools and had his head patted by many of the most prominent politicians in the country. He spent his summers on the family farm in Carthage, Tenn., about 40 miles east of Nashville, working the fields with the hired hands.
The Vice President's parents -- his mother, Pauline, is a lawyer -- are now well into their 80's and live on the farm in Carthage.
As a student at Harvard, Mr. Gore opposed the Vietnam War. But after graduation he enlisted in the Army and spent a year in a noncombat job in Vietnam. Friends say the main reason he enlisted was to spare his father, who was in what would prove to be a losing race for re-election to the Senate in 1970, the political burden of having a draft-dodging son.
Just before he left for Vietnam, he married Mary Elizabeth Aitcheson, known as Tipper. They now have four children: Karenna, 23; Kristin, 19; Sarah, 17, and Albert 3d, 13. The family is Baptist.
After leaving the Army, Mr. Gore returned to Tennessee. He worked for a time in Nashville as a reporter and editorial writer for The Tennessean, and went to Vanderbilt Law School.
When he was 28, the seat in the Congressional district that included Carthage came open, and he ran and won. Eight years later, in 1984, he was elected to the Senate. He was re-elected in 1990.
In 1988, only 39 years old and in his first term in the Senate, Mr. Gore entered the Democratic primaries for President.
His flat speaking style and tendency to keep his distance from the public cost him. He lost badly in the New York primary despite the support of Edward I. Koch, who was then Mayor, and withdrew from the race.
Mr. Gore's friends say he is anything but dull in private. A White House official says that when Mr. Clinton is in an especially bad mood, Mr. Gore can often bring him out of his funk by teasing him.
But on stage the Vice President rarely seems comfortable. Too often his smile seems forced, his word emphasis misplaced. He has even taken to making fun of himself in his speeches.
"How can you tell Al Gore from a roomful of Secret Service agents?" he asked his audience at the Veterans of Foreign Wars convention in Louisville, Ky., last week. "He's the stiff one."
Asked why he had decided to call attention to what has often been his Achilles' heel, Mr. Gore responded: "I can't do anything about it. I might as well make the best of it."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Profit Motive Clouds Effort To Buy Up A-Bomb Material
The American Government-owned corporation that is charged with acquiring much of Russia's military stockpile of uranium for resale as civilian nuclear reactor fuel turned down repeated Russian offers this year to sell material sufficient to build 400 bombs comparable to the one that destroyed Hiroshima.
The incident raises the question of whether the corporation, the United States Enrichment Corporation, put its profits ahead of national security. The purchase agreement was a centerpiece of the urgent American effort to keep the ingredients of powerful weapons in the former Soviet Union out of the hands of terrorists or rogue nations. [Page A1.]
 
A Challenge to Milken Settlement
A challenge by TLC Beatrice International Holdings to the 1992 global settlement of claims against Michael R. Milken and others associated with Drexel Burnham Lambert was revived by the United States Court of Appeals in Manhattan. The court sent the case back to Judge Milton Pollack of Federal District Court in New York, who had dismissed it last September. [D2.]
 
A Fidelity Fund Manager Resigns
Robert A. Beckwitt, once one of the brightest stars among Fidelity Investments' mutual fund managers, has resigned to join Goldman, Sachs & Company. Mr. Beckwitt had managed the Fidelity Asset Manager funds. [D2.]
 
Tobacco Effort Grows in Congress
An effort is under way on Capitol Hill to pass legislation that would settle the lawsuits between tobacco companies and states. But it is far from clear that the proposal could be passed in this session of Congress. [B8.]
 
Stocks Rise on Dividend Prospects
Stocks rose on prospects for higher dividends and stronger-than-expected earnings. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 17.38 points, to 5,711.27. The Nasdaq composite index jumped 9.80 points, to 1,149.02. [D6.]
 
Prices of Treasury securities gained, despite a disappointing two-year Treasury note auction, sending the bond's yield down to 6.97 percent, from 7 percent on Monday. [D16.]

Consumer Confidence Up in August
Consumer confidence surged in August to a six-year high of 109.4 on the Conference Board's index. The advance raised doubts that the economy is headed for a slowdown and led to fears by some of higher interest rates. [D5.]
 
Boeing Plans to Add 5,000 Workers
Boeing plans to hire up to 5,000 employees to meet demand. The company also said that production of its 777 jet would increase to seven airplanes a month from five. [D2.]
 
Foxmeyer Unit Files for Bankruptcy
The Foxmeyer Health Corporation put its wholesale drug distribution unit into Chapter 11 bankruptcy, after efforts to sell the ailing company fell through. [D6.]
 
Matsushita Rebounds With Profit
Matsushita Electric Industrial rebounded to a profit of 18.5 billion yen, or about $170 million, in its latest quarter, aided by a weaker yen. A year earlier it lost 151.3 billion yen. [D3.]
 
British Insurer in Swiss Deal
Prudential, the British insurer, said it was selling its reinsurance unit to Swiss Reinsurance for $2.7 billion. [D3.]
 
The Regular Guys at the N.F.L.
What's under a professional football player's helmet, padding and uniform? An appealing human being, the National Football League feels. In an ambitious $50 million broadcast and print campaign, it is stressing players and their personalities in the hope of converting casual fans into real fanatics.
Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D4.]
