PUB-DATE: August 28, 1996

SECTION: Section A; Page 17; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 3622 words

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS;
Excerpts From Remarks by Cuomo, Jackson, Bayh and the First Lady

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from remarks yesterday at the Democratic National Convention by the Rev. Jesse Jackson; Gov. Evan Bayh of Indiana, the keynote speaker; former Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York, and and Hillary Rodham Clinton: 
 MR. JACKSON
Thirty-three years ago tonight, a young preacher about the same age as my son was putting the final touches on one of the great prophetic messages of our age. Aug. 28, 1963, Dr. King projected a vision of peace and equality that could heal our nation in a troubled world.
The Republicans in San Diego put forward the image, the vision of a big tent. On the cover was General Powell and Jack Kemp. But clearly you cannot judge a book by its cover. For inside, the book was written by Newt Gingrich and Ralph Reed and Pat Buchanan. All the rights that made General Powell possible are now under assault for the next generation, and all that Kemp believed in until last week is now under assault.
What is our challenge tonight? Just look around this place. This publicly financed United Center is a new Chicago mountaintop. To the south, Comiskey Park, another mountaintop. To the west, Cooke County jail.
Two ball parks, a jail.
That jail, mostly youthful inmates 80 percent drug-positive, 90 percent high school dropouts, 92 percent functionally illiterate, 75 percent recidivist rate. They go back sicker and slicker.
Between these mountains of the ball parks and the jails was once Campbell's Soup and Sears and Zenith and Sunbeam and stockyards. There were jobs, and there was industry; now there's a canyon of welfare and despair.
This canyon exists in virtually every city in America. One-tenth of all American children will go to bed in poverty tonight. Half of all America's African-American children grow up amidst broken sidewalks, broken hearts, broken cities and broken dreams.
The No. 1 growth industry in urban America: jail. Half of all public housing built to last 10 years. Jails. The top wealthiest 1 percent wealthiest Americans own as much as the bottom 95 percent, the great inequality since the 1920's. As corporations downsize jobs, outsource contracts, scab on workers' rights, a class crisis emerges as a race problem. But the strawberry pickers in California, the chicken workers in North Carolina deserve a hearing. We must seek a new moral center.
My brothers and my sisters, we have been here before. Hear this tonight: We've been here before. The last time we gathered in Chicago high winds ripped apart our big tent. We could not bridge that gap between strongly held opinions. We lost to Nixon by the margin of our despair.
In 1968, the tension within our party was over warfare. In 1996, it's welfare.
Last week, over the objections of many Democratic party leaders and the opposition of millions of Americans, Franklin Roosevelt's six-decade guarantee of support for women and children was abandoned. On this issue, many of us differed with the President. Patricia Ireland and I even picketed the White House. We're all for welfare reform, but we want jobs, and day care and health care as the alternatives.
The good news is this: When Pataki and Wilson disagreed in San Diego, they were sent to Siberia, and Gingrich had to do a volleyball and Trent Lott had to sing a song. We have the right to express ourselves, and that's diversity, and that's the Democratic Party.
My brothers and my sisters, diversity is the measure of the party's strength, how we handle adversity the measure of our character. We must find the bridge to keep our tent intact. We must make the commitment to right the wrongs in the bill. We need a Democratic Congress. Now that we have ended welfare as we know it, let's fight for jobs and day care and health education as we ought to know it.
Kid us if you will, the fight was never about welfare; it was always about jobs and opportunity. . . .
For us Democrats, when we change the course, the real issue is not "three strikes and you're out." If the crime is vicious enough, maybe one strike is enough. But what about the vision of "four balls and you're on"? Prenatal care and Head Start, ball one; an adequately funded public education, ball two; a marketable skill, ball three; and a job, ball four. Put America on. Lift our youth up, not lock them up.
What shall we do? Between these two ball parks and this jail, what shall we do in this canyon between the ball parks and the jail, we shall reclaim our children. Certainly, these stakes are very high in 1996. We have the burden and the obligation to win for yet unborn generations. What shall we do economically?
We have $6 trillion in private and public pension funds. Why can't we take 5 percent of that money, $300 billion, government secured -- use that money to reinvest in our infrastructure and put America back to work. We did it for Poland. We made for Poland 40-year loans at three quarters of 1 percent, first payment due in 10 years. If we can rebuild Poland and Europe and Japan, we can build Chicago and Atlanta and Memphis and Nashville. We can rebuild America.
Don't stop there. My friends, as I rise to support our President, let me make this case. Our Presidents must make strong positions on defense and offense.
President Clinton has been our first line of defense against the Newt Gingrich contract; America's right-wing assault on elderly, our students and our civil rights. We must maintain with integrity the first lines of defense as they attack the integrity of the First Lady. It's beneath the dignity of American citizens. We have that obligation. It's a moral obligation. It's a moral obligation.
My brothers and my sisters, when the President stands for the end of assault weapons, raises minimum wage, earned income taxes, stands with the mourning congregations of burned churches, stands for affirmative action and voting rights and social justice and general equality, he deserves four more years.
 
MR. BAYH
Tonight, I stand between my father and my sons. The dreams our parents had for us are the dreams Susan and I share for our boys. The times are vastly different now; the challenges we face are new. But the values that must guide us are the same.
They are the values that President Clinton has worked to restore to meet the challenges of our time: Opportunity for all Americans; responsibility from all Americans, and a sense of community among all Americans.
And he's meeting our economic challenges. Since President Clinton took office, over 10 million new jobs have been created -- four times the number during the previous four years; over a million new jobs in construction and manufacturing alone. And for the first time since the 1970's America leads the world in making cars -- ahead of Japan.
And President Clinton's protecting our traditional value of making hard work pay. He fought for an increase in the minimum wage -- and 10 million Americans got a pay raise. He cut taxes for 15 million families who get up every morning and work hard all day long -- only to get a paycheck on Friday that still leaves them in poverty. But now, anyone who works 40 hours a week in America will not live in poverty.
But we must go beyond these historic gains because our progress has yet to touch all Americans. In quiet corners across our country, families still struggle to pay the mortgage, save for college, make ends meet. To you, our neighbors, I say the president's agenda for the future reaches these quiet corners. His agenda balances the budget to keep interest rates down and the economy strong.
It protects against wholesale cuts for the elderly, the sick, the young, the environment and the fight against crime and drugs. And it provides for tax cuts -- not an election-year gimmick, but targeted tax cuts aimed at families trying to care for and educate our children, a $500 credit for every child, $1,500 to help pay for the first two years of college, a $10,000 deduction for education and training after high school.
We create real opportunity with these tax cuts -- and, what's more -- we can pay for them. Now that's something our opponents just can't say. They know their plan is too expensive -- it will explode the deficit, raise interest rates, slow the economy and still require deeper cuts in the things we for which we care.
Our President is committed to balancing the budget and giving families the tools they need to build their own lives. And that's what governing is all about. Getting priorities straight -- and holding to them. And that's just what we did in Indiana. First we balanced the budget, and then we passed the largest tax cut in history with more funding for schools every year. . . .
In this election, no issue more clearly defines the differences between the two major parties and our nominees than education. On that issue alone, President Clinton and Democrats who share his vision deserve the support of the American people.
And we're meeting the challenge of changing welfare while still protecting our values. President Clinton understands that welfare was intended to be temporary, make work possible. And across America he's made that happen.
 
MR. CUOMO
Some of the President's choices have been more controversial than others. The welfare reform bill has been one of the most difficult. Many of us,-- and I among them, believe that the risk to children was too great to justify the action of signing that bill, no matter what its political benefits.
I know because I've spoken with him and with the Vice President, and with Hillary, I know that they all understood and understand the risk. But the President is confident that he can advert this risk by further legislation before children are actually harmed.
We should all hope and pray -- we should all hope and pray that the President is right. But we should do something more than hope and pray. We cannot rely on the Republicans to cooperate with President Clinton to save our children. We need to give the President the strength of a Democratic Congress.
We need to help the President make this law better, as he has assured us that he will.
And we need to do a lot more even beyond welfare. When we talk of welfare, of course we all want people on welfare to work. So do most of them want to work. But there must be jobs, not in Thailand, not in China, not in Mexico, but here in Chicago and rural Mississippi and in East L.A.! And not jobs that can barely be called jobs; jobs that give real hope, jobs that give a man, give a woman the dignity of knowing that they can work their way up in this society. Real jobs.
Of course we want only the government we need, but we continue to insist, as Democrats, on all the government we need. And that includes government that will help more Americans find real health insurance, to make sure our day care options get better, not worse.
Of course we want and demand a balanced budget. No one in the modern history of this country, no president has done more to move toward a balanced budget than has President Bill Clinton.
But Democrats also know that it is a shameful thing that in some of our public schools the highest form of technology available is a metal detector. We know that our students must have access to the information superhighway, but we also know, because we're honest with ourselves, that the information superhighway is a toll road.
It has to be paid for, and the government can help with that, too. Frankly, it's one of the best things that government can do for us -- help us to educate ourselves.
As a matter of fact, ladies and gentlemen, brothers and sisters, we have at this moment a great opportunity. President Clinton, with the help of a Democratic Congress, can do for education what President John Kennedy did for space.
We are the richest the freest, the most powerful nation in world history -- the richest, the most powerful in world history. Is there any reason why we should not be the best educated and the most highly-skilled people in this fiercely competitive world. We are not, but we can be, and President Clinton can lead us there.
 
MRS. CLINTON
 You know, we are gathered here together to have a really good time. I am overwhelmed and very grateful to all of you. You know after this reception, I think you all are ready for the rest of this convention, which has already been so positive and good.
I know, and you know, that Chicago is my kind of town. And Chicago is my kind of village.
 I have so many friends here, people who have been important to me all my life, and it seems like every single one of them has given me advice on this speech. One friend suggested that I appear here tonight with Binti, the child-saving gorilla from the Brookfield Zoo.
You know, as this friend explained, Binti is a typical Chicagoan, tough on the outside but with a heart of gold underneath.
Another friend advised me that I should cut my hair and color it orange and then change my name to Hillary Rodman Clinton.
But after considering these and countless other suggestions, I decided to do tonight what I've been doing for more than 25 years; I want to talk about what matters most in our lives and in our nation -- children and families.
I wish we could be sitting around a kitchen table, just us, talking about our hopes and fears, about our children's futures. For Bill and me, family has been the center of our lives. But we also know that our family, like your family, is part of a larger community that can help or hurt our best efforts to raise our child.
Right now, in our biggest cities and our smallest towns, there are boys and girls being tucked gently into bed, and there are boys and girls who have no one to call Mom or Dad, and no place to call home.
Right now, there are mothers and fathers just finishing a long day's work. And there are mothers and fathers just going to work, some to their second or third jobs of the day.
Right now, there are parents worrying, "What if the baby sitter is sick tomorrow?" Or, "How can we pay for college this fall?" And right now there are parents despairing about gang members and drug pushers on the corners in their neighborhoods.
Right now, there are parents questioning a popular culture that glamorizes sex and violence, smoking and drinking, and teaches children that the logos on their clothes are more valued than the generosity in their hearts.
But also right now there are dedicated teachers preparing their lessons for the new school year. There are volunteers tutoring and coaching children. There are doctors and nurses caring for sick children, police officers working to help kids stay out of trouble and off drugs.
Of course, parents, first and foremost, are responsible for their children.
But we are all responsible for insuring that children are raised in a nation that doesn't just talk about family values, but acts in ways that values families.
Just think, as Christopher Reeve so eloquently reminded us last night, we are all part of one family, the American family. And each one of us has value. Each child who comes into this world should feel special, every boy and every girl.
Our daughter, Chelsea, will graduate from college in 2001, at the dawn of the next century. Though that's not so far away, it is hard for any of us to know what the world will look like then, much less when Chelsea is my age, in the year 2028. But one thing we know for sure is that change is certain -- progress is not.
Progress depends on the choices we make today for tomorrow, and on whether we meet our challenges and protect our values. We can start by doing more to support parents and the job they have to do. Issues affecting children and families are some of the hardest we face, as parents, as citizens, as a nation.
In October, Bill and I will celebrate our 21st wedding anniversary. And Bill was with me when Chelsea was born, in the delivery room, in my hospital room, and when we brought our baby daughter home. Not only did I have lots of help, I was able to stay in the hospital as long as my doctor thought I needed to be there.
But today, too many new mothers are asked to get up and get out after 24 hours, and that is just not enough time for many new mothers and babies. That's why the President is right to support a bill that would prohibit the practice of forcing mothers and babies to leave the hospital in less than 48 hours.
That's also why more hospitals ought to install 24-hour hot lines to answer questions once new mothers and fathers get home. That's why home nurses can make such a difference to parents who may not have grandparents or aunts and uncles around to help. We have to do whatever it takes to help parents meet their responsibilities at home and at work. The very first piece of legislation that my husband signed into law had been vetoed twice, the Family and Medical Leave Law.
That law allows parents time off for the birth or adoption of a child or for family emergencies without fear of losing their jobs. Already it has helped 12 million families, and it hasn't hurt the economy one bit.
You know, Bill and I are fortunate that our jobs have allowed us to take breaks from work not only when Chelsea was born, but to attend her school events and take her to the doctor. But millions of other parents can't get time off. That's why my husband wants to expand the Family and Medical Leave Law so that parents can take time off for children's doctors appointments and parent-teacher conferences at school.
We all know that raising kids is a full-time job and since most parents work, they are, we are stretched thin. Just think about what many parents are responsible for on any given day. Packing lunches, dropping the kids off at school, going to work, checking to make sure that the kids get home from school safely, shopping for groceries, making dinner, doing the laundry, helping with homework, paying the bills, and I didn't even mention taking the dog to the vet.
That's why my husband wants to pass a flex-time law that will give parents the option to take overtime pay either in extra income or in extra time off depending upon whichever is best for your family.
Our family has been lucky to have been blessed with a child with good health. Chelsea has spent only one night in the hospital after she had her tonsils out, but Bill and I couldn't sleep at all that night.
But our experience was nothing like the emotional strain on parents when their children are seriously ill. They often worry about where they will get the money to pay the medical bills. That is why my husband has always felt that all American families should have affordable health insurance.
Just last week the President signed a bill, sponsored by Senators Kennedy and Kassebaum, a Democrat and a Republican, that will enable 25 million Americans to keep their health insurance, even when they switch jobs or lose a job or have a family member who's been sick.
This bill contains some of the key provisions from the President's proposal for health care reform. It was an important step, achieved only after both parties agreed to build, not block, progress on making health care available to all Americans.
Now the country must take the next step of helping unemployed Americans and their children keep health insurance for six months after losing their jobs.
If you lose your job, it's bad enough, but your daughter shouldn't have to lose her doctor, too. And our nation still must find a way to offer affordable health care coverage to the working poor and the 10 million children who lack health insurance today.
The President also hasn't forgotten that there are thousands of children languishing in foster care who can't be returned home. That's why he signed legislation last week that provides for a $5,000 tax credit for parents who adopt a child. It also, it also abolishes the barriers to cross-racial adoptions. Never again will a racial barrier stand in the way of a family's love.
My husband also understands that parents are their child's first teachers. Not only do we need to read to our children and talk to them in ways that encourage learning; we must support our teachers and our schools in deeds as well as words.
The President announced today an important initiative called America Reads. This initiative is aimed at making sure all children can read well by the third grade. It will require volunteers, but I know there are thousands and thousands of Americans who will volunteer to help every child read well.
For Bill and me, there has been no experience more challenging, more rewarding, and more humbling than raising our daughter.
And we have learned that to raise a happy, healthy and hopeful child, it takes a family, it takes teachers, it takes clergy, it takes business people, it takes community leaders, it takes those who protect our health and safety, it takes all of us.
Yes, it takes a village.
And it takes a President.
It takes a President who believes not only in the potential of his own child, but of all children -- who believes not only in the strength of his own family, but of the American family, who believes not only in the promise of each of us as individuals, but in our promise together as a nation.
It takes a President who not only holds these beliefs but acts on them.
It takes Bill Clinton.
Sometimes, late at night, when I see Chelsea doing her homework, or watching TV, or talking to a friend on the phone, I think to myself, her life and the lives of millions of boys and girls will be better because of what all of us are doing together. They will face fewer obstacles and more possibilities. That is something we should all be proud of, and that is what this election is all about.

HEADLINE: U.S. OPEN 1996;
Next Year, a Closer Look Will Be Costly at Open

BYLINE: By RICHARD SANDOMIR 

 BODY:
The already extravagant cost of experiencing the United States Open tennis championships will get an expensive jolt next year when the tournament moves to a new 20,000-seat stadium being built within a lob of the old one.
Destined to bear the brunt of tennis inflation are those with the closest seats, the denizens of the 265 courtside boxes. Within that group are 67 choice front-row courtside box positions, 62 of which are operational. 
 The United States Tennis Association sent out letters on Aug. 14 to all of the individuals and corporations that own the courtside boxes, informing them of increases ranging from 50 to 100 percent.
Some of those that own the 265 courtside boxes, including one bought by the advertising department of The New York Times, get them with their sponsorship fees.
But other boxholders said the U.S.T.A. is imposing even heftier increases that total two and a half times the current 1996 rate -- raising the most costly premium seating to nearly $50,000 for the two-week event.
"I'm going from $17,000 to $35,000," said Robert Malaga of Cleveland, a former U.S.T.A. executive director, who will be moved from the front row to a second-row box. "I guess I'm going to find out if it's worth it. I gave it a lot of thought before I renewed. It's a shame it has to be like this."
Recalling the days of $1,500 courtside boxes when the tournament was in Forest Hills, Malaga added, "Everybody I know who has a box has re-upped for next year."
Among other owners of the courtside boxes are the comedian Alan King; the real estate developer Lewis Rudin; Joseph Cullman, the former Philip Morris executive, who brought in Virginia Slims as the women's tour's longtime sponsor; John Mack Carter, the head of Hearst Magazines, and the art dealer Allan Stone.
"They have us over a barrel because they have thousands of people on the waiting list," said one boxholder who requested anonymity.
Said another, who also asked for anonymity: "The subject is greed. I'm flabbergasted."
But not every boxholder is upset. "The price went up, so it must be prestigious," said Jerry Spiegel, a real estate developer, who said the cost of his box rose 100 percent. "We love it. The people we entertain love it. We get good reaction from it. I'm in real estate, so how much ocean-front property is there?"
Spiegel said he refused an offer to lease a new luxury suite. "I like being with the people," he said. "If I'm going to be in a sky box, I can stay home."
The 90 new 20-ticket luxury suites are at a more rarefied level, $85,000 to $100,000 each for the tournament only, and are already sold out. The current stadium has no suites.
Luxury suites at Giants Stadium for Jets and Giants football cost $106,000 each; $295,000 in Ericsson Stadium, the new home of the Carolina Panthers football team in Charlotte, N.C., and $300,000 at Madison Square Garden for Knicks basketball and Rangers hockey.
The U.S.T.A. admits that the $234 million being spent to build the new stadium, scale down the current stadium and renovate the rest of the Flushing Meadows tennis center required the price jumps.
"We feel it's entirely justified by the tournament being a first-class event," said Suzanne Maguire, the U.S.T.A.'s director of transition. "The market requires us to price the stadium in a way that reflects the pricing of other premium sports events."
The original $150 million price tag for the stadium project has increased since it was first proposed, and is now nearly as steep as the $255 million ball park being built privately by the San Francisco Giants.
Maguire said that the U.S.T.A. declined to sell personal seat licenses, a popular form of stadium financing, in which fans pay several hundred or several thousand dollars for the rights to purchase season tickets.
But seat licenses are generally considered most effective where a team is new to a city, rather than in raising money to keep a team in the same market. They have been used mainly in the National Football League, in Charlotte; Baltimore (for the Ravens); St. Louis (for the Rams); Oakland, Calif. (for the Raiders); and Nashville, where the Houston Oilers will move.
Maguire said the price increases were justified by the U.S.T.A.'s rising costs and from its market research. "Look at basketball and what they're getting for front-row seats -- about $1,000," she said.
And, Maguire said hopefully, there is a built-in, envy-driven demand for the courtside boxes. "Everybody in the loge seats wants a courtside box," she said.
Maguire said she has spoken to about 20 boxholders, some of whom will move from front- to second-row boxes, and those who will have lesser views in the new stadium because of a new configuration.
"They've been very understanding," she said.
Boxholders will get parking and passes to two V.I.P. clubs for their escalated fees. The box seats will be wider, with greater leg room. The wealthiest spectators at the new stadium will get the largest price hikes. But fans elsewhere will see already expensive seats get more costly.
Front-row loge seats, which cost $100 a session in the existing stadium, will require about 11 percent more in the new park. Remaining loge seats will increase by about 7 percent, from $75 in the current facility.
Reserved seats throughout the rest of the stadium will increase by 6 percent from the current prices of $32 to $39 per session's ticket.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Matsushita, Freed of MCA, Reports a Profit

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The Matsushita Electric Industrial Company rebounded to profit in the latest quarter, as a weaker Japanese yen and better sales helped the company overturn last year's losses from an ill-advised foray into the Hollywood movie business.
Net profit in the three-month period ending on June 30 was 18.5 billion yen -- roughly $170 million -- on sales of 1.72 trillion yen. Earnings for each share came to 8.29 yen, or 7.6 cents, in contrast to a loss of 72.16 yen a share a year earlier. 
 "This wasn't just a one-time benefit from the yen," said Reinier Dobbelmann of SBC Warburg Japan Ltd. "The company saw solid growth domestically as well as abroad."
Matsushita, the world's largest maker of consumer electronics, much of it under the Panasonic brand, reported a net loss of 151.3 billion yen in the similar period last year, after a one-time charge from its sale in April 1995 of 80 percent of the movie producer MCA.
Excluding the MCA charge, Matsushita's net profit in the quarter was 44 percent above the figure a year earlier. The company paid $6.1 billion for control of MCA and its Universal film studios in 1990. Matsushita had expected MCA's movie and music videotapes to complement its videocassette recorder and home stereo system businesses.
After five years of Matsushita's conservative approach to business clashing with the freewheeling, high-spending ethos at MCA, Matsushita called it quits and sold 80 percent of its stake to the Seagram Company.
Matsushita received the same amount of money in dollar terms for the stake that it originally paid, but the dollar had declined in value by 32 percent in the five years, sharply reducing the value of the holding.
Matsushita said its latest performance was helped by a rise in sales at home and abroad. Domestic sales rose 8 percent for the quarter, to 900.8 billion yen, pushed up by demand for high-definition television sets and mobile telecommunications equipment.
Steady economic growth in American and other Asian markets offset sluggishness in Europe, pushing Matsushita's foreign sales up 18 percent, to 818.8 billion yen.
The recent weakness in the yen helped profit because it made Matsushita's products cheaper outside Japan and increased the income in yen from foreign sales when that income was converted from other currencies. The company gained about 17.5 billion yen in pretax profit from the weaker yen. That, and about 57 billion yen saved from cost reduction, helped offset 60 billion yen in lost profit from the falling prices of home appliances, the company reported.
Matsushita said it saw no need to change its earnings forecasts for the fiscal year, which ends in March 1997. It projects a net profit of 115 billion yen on sales of 7.2 trillion yen.

CORRECTION-DATE: August 29, 1996, Thursday

SECTION: Section A; Page 19; Column 3; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 1138 words

HEADLINE: Clintons and Carters Don't Mix

BYLINE: By Douglas Brinkley; Douglas Brinkley, director of the Eisenhower Center and professor of history at the University of New Orleans, is writing a book on Jimmy Carter.

 BODY:
The Republicans devoted a good chunk of their convention's prime TV time in San Diego to saluting former Presidents Ford, Reagan and Bush. In Chicago, by contrast, the Democrats have gone out of their way to avoid even acknowledging the existence of their party's only living former President.
It may look odd, but the absence of Jimmy Carter from the Democratic National Convention vividly illustrates just how little love is lost between him and President Clinton. That has been clear since 1992, when candidate Clinton was consumed by fear of being labeled "another Jimmy Carter," a rap the Republicans had pinned on Walter Mondale and Michael Dukakis with devastating results. "Jimmy Carter and I are as different as daylight and dark," Mr. Clinton insisted in an interview in 1992. It is a posture he has diligently maintained ever since. 
 Four years ago, it was with mild trepidation that the Democrats eventually asked Mr. Carter to address their New York convention. Even though he had become popular for his freelance diplomatic and election-monitoring missions and for his humanitarian house-building work, the party bosses' only concern seemed to be to get him on and off the stage as quickly as possible before TV viewers had a chance to flash back to the Iran hostage debacle, double-digit inflation and gasoline lines.
That was when the Clinton team still reluctantly tolerated Mr. Carter. Four years later -- after successfully intervening on behalf of the Administration in North Korea and Haiti in 1994 -- he appears to be have been deemed persona non grata at 1600 Pennsylvania Avenue. Not that this is anything new: although Mr. Carter was asked to the White House in a show of support for the North American Free Trade Agreement, Mr. Clinton still has yet to invite his Democratic predecessor to a state dinner or even to an informal function.
The bad blood between them can be traced to 1980, when then-President Carter ordered 19,000 Cuban refugees, many of them common criminals whom Fidel Castro had let out of jail, to be housed at Fort Chaffee in Arkansas. It was an unpopular decision, and it cost Governor Clinton, who was up for re-election, dearly. In May, the Cubans rioted against squalid conditions; some 300 of them escaped, and Mr. Clinton had to call in the National Guard. By August the White House had decided to send even more Cuban refugees to Fort Chaffee. The Governor lost the election.
During the Reagan-Bush years, Mr. Carter didn't pay Democratic politics much notice. Still, he strongly supported Mr. Mondale in 1984 and Mr. Dukakis in 1988 once they were nominated. As a rule, Mr. Carter had refused to support any national Democratic candidate until after the Georgia primary.
Although Mr. Carter never technically endorsed anyone in the 1992 primaries, he gave several clear signs that he thought either Mr. Clinton or Paul Tsongas would make a good President. Mr. Clinton, he believed, had been a fine governor, and Mr. Tsongas had endeared himself when he visited Plains and called it "one of the 'mecca stops' along the way to the White House." At one point, with Mr. Tsongas beaming at his side, Mr. Carter said, "People are looking for somebody who is honest and tells the truth" -- causing some pundits to infer that Mr. Clinton did not fit the bill for the former President.
But Mr. Carter, who has long admired Al and Tipper Gore, agreed to play the good soldier and actively supported Mr. Clinton in 1992. He even had the clout to push Warren Christopher, his former diplomatic trouble-shooter and lawyer for the Carter Center in Atlanta, as Secretary of State in the new Administration. But it is obvious that Mr. Christopher got the message from the White House that he was being retained by only one Presidential client: Bill Clinton. As Inauguration Day approached, Mr. Carter found his calls to Mr. Christopher ignored or returned by underlings.
The situation declined further. At the inaugural gala, Mr. Clinton took the occasion to thank all the celebrities present, from Barbra Streisand to Kenny G., while ignoring Jimmy and Rosalynn Carter. The gesture was disrespectful not only toward the Carters, but toward the Presidency itself.
The relationship took yet another turn for the worse in April 1995, when Mr. Carter and Mr. Clinton both found themselves in Warm Springs, Ga., to commemorate the 50th anniversary of Franklin Roosevelt's death. Although Mr. Carter, who had just returned from brokering a cease-fire in the Sudan, was receiving the F.D.R. Four Freedoms medal, Mr. Clinton did not congratulate him, ask about his African endeavor or even mention his name.
Later, a group of distinguished Democrats worried about Mr. Clinton's posture toward Mr. Carter broached the sensitive matter at a White House luncheon. Mr. Clinton, who had been talking animatedly, suddenly fell mute, according to someone who attended the meeting. When the President was finally persuaded to praise Mr. Carter for his diplomatic work at a Harry Truman tribute dinner in Washington some months later, many found his remarks disingenuous.
Even more so than her husband, Rosalynn Carter has suffered from the shabby treatment from the Clinton Administration and the Democratic National Committee. Hillary Clinton, for instance, has telephoned Rosalynn only once in four years and has never paid her a courtesy call despite numerous visits to Atlanta. More recently, the D.N.C. sent the Carters a box of fund-raising note cards featuring pictures of Democratic Presidents -- with the notable exception of Jimmy Carter. Mrs. Carter later wrote Dan Fowler, the operational head of the Democratic National Committee, a blistering note in which she asked the committee to remove her name from its membership rolls.
When the time came for the Democrats to plan their Chicago convention, Jimmy Carter was asked to participate in drafting the platform, but he declined. Then, weeks went by before President Clinton phoned Mr. Carter in mid-July to ask if he planned to attend the convention. If it was the D.N.C.'s hope that Mr. Carter would refuse to attend if he was issued a half-hearted invitation and no meaningful role to play, the strategy worked: while the Democratic delegates danced the macarena, he would go fly-fishing in Montana with Ted Turner and Jane Fonda.
The party chairman, Senator Christopher Dodd of Connecticut, was asked on the eve of the convention why Mr. Carter would not be in Chicago. "What I was told by people coming over here this morning is that a request was made, but the timing of it was I don't know," Mr. Dodd allowed. What he meant to say was that the United Center, which has room enough for the Chicago Bulls to host the Seattle SuperSonics, isn't big enough for both of America's Democratic Presidents.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Gains on Yen as Chance Of a Japanese Rate Rise Dims

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar surged against the yen yesterday after unexpected weakness in a Bank of Japan survey dashed expectations of higher Japanese interest rates.
The Bank of Japan's quarterly survey, known as the tankan, showed an index for major manufacturers fell to -7 in August from -3 in the previous report in May. The index reflects the percentage of companies that said their business climate was improving minus the percentage that reported worsening conditions. 
The report allayed concern that signs of stronger growth would prompt Japan's central bank to raise lending rates from record lows, a move that would help the value of the yen by making deposits in the currency more attractive.
"When people saw the contents of the report, they realized the B.O.J. isn't moving anywhere soon," said Peter Wadkins, senior currency trader at Standard Chartered Bank. "That obviously helped drive the dollar higher against the yen."
Remarks from Japanese officials yesterday bolstered expectations that the Bank of Japan would not soon raise rates.
Masayuki Matsushima, director of the central bank's research and statistics department, said the tankan confirmed that Japan's economic recovery remained slow.
The dollar rose to 108.38 yen in Asian trading and by late in the day in New York stood at 108.42 yen, up from 107.68 yen on Tuesday. The dollar fell to 1.4765 German marks from 1.4783 marks. The British pound rose to $1.5591, from $1.5545.
The mark rose, continuing to benefit from concern about the timetable for European monetary union.
In recent weeks, several European officials have suggested that an extension may be needed to let more European countries meet the participation requirements, which include limits on the size of budget deficits and government debt.
Finance Minister Erik Aasbrink of Sweden wrote in the Dagens Nyheter newspaper yesterday that Sweden could join the single currency at a later date even if it decided next year against entering on the 1999 start date. Traders viewed his comments as a hint Sweden would not join the monetary union at its start.
"That's just another indication there are going to be some problems in bringing E.M.U. to fruition," Mr. Wadkins of Standard Chartered said.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Economic Recovery Seen as Weak in Japan

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The Bank of Japan's quarterly survey of business sentiment signaled that the nation's economic recovery had been weaker than expected, indicating that the central bank was not likely to raise interest rates soon.
The August survey of 10,000 companies, known as the tankan and released today, showed that major manufacturers had less confidence in the economy than they had three months earlier. Economists said that the finding would quash speculation that the central bank might raise interest rates, which are now at record low levels, to keep inflation from accelerating. 
 "There are many reasons why the Bank of Japan can't hike rates at this time, and I think this release is the nail in the coffin of any speculation of a rate hike for the next six months," said Andrew Shipley, an economist at Schroders Securities Japan Ltd.
Indeed, Finance Minister Wataru Kubo said he expected the central bank to keep interest rates low. Speaking at a news conference, Mr. Kubo said that the bank's governor, Yasuo Matsushita, "has continuously said the bank wants to conduct policies aimed at insuring the recovery." He added, "I believe that stance has remained unchanged."
In Japan, the Finance Ministry strongly influences the central bank's monetary policy, in contrast to the United States, where the central bank, the Federal Reserve, is rigidly independent.
The belief that monetary policy would remain unchanged lifted the dollar against the yen; higher interest rates generally raise a currency's value because they increase the returns available from interest-bearing investments denominated in that currency.
At the end of trading in Tokyo today, the dollar stood at 108.38 yen, up from 107.68 yen in late New York trading Tuesday. By late afternoon in New York today, the dollar rose further, to 108.42 yen.
On the Tokyo Stock Exchange, share prices rose following the tankan's release, but the market reversed course as concern grew about the slow pace of the economic recovery. The Nikkei average of 225 issues closed with a loss of 200.46 points, or about 1 percent, at 20,709.81.
The tankan's most closely watched component, the diffusion index of major manufacturers, stood at -7, weaker than the reading of -3 in the previous survey, which was taken in May. The index reflects the percentage of companies that said their business climate was improving minus the percentage that reported worsening conditions.
In the May survey, manufacturers had predicted that the August diffusion index would stand at zero.
Factors behind the negative response in the latest survey included a drop of 4.3 percent in industrial production during June, rising inventories and a decline in machine orders that reflected a slump in the semiconductor market and weakness in markets for other exports.
Still, the Bank of Japan and some economists said the recovery was on track. "This report confirms that the recovery is slow but continuing," said Masayuki Matsushima, director of the bank's research and statistics department.
Ron Bevacqua, an economist at Merrill Lynch & Company, said: "The worst is over. The pace of growth may have slowed, but the other tankan data says the recovery is continuing to broaden."
Positive signs included rising confidence among companies outside the manufacturing sector and among small companies, as well as upward revisions in capital investment plans, he said.
The central bank noted that the latest tankan did not reflect the recent weakening of the yen, which is likely to aid exports. It added that business at food and retail companies was hurt by the recent widespread outbreak of food poisoning in western Japan.

HEADLINE: City Hires Consultants To Seek Stadium Funds

BYLINE: By VIVIAN S. TOY 

 BODY:
Months after speculation that the New York Yankees would soon abandon their South Bronx home circulated and eventually subsided, the stadium debate resurfaced again yesterday when city officials announced that they had hired a financial consultant to help the city devise ways to pay for new or renovated stadiums for both the Yankees and the New York Mets.
The city will spend $750,000 over two years for the expertise of Public Financial Management, a Philadelphia-based firm that has cobbled multi-million-dollar stadium deals across the country and is working on a plan to find private financing for a new stadium for the San Francisco Giants. The firm will advise Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani on financing as the city enters into negotiations for stadiums with both baseball teams later this year. 
 But even as the city moves ahead, officials are still waiting for George M. Steinbrenner 3d, the principal owner of the New York Yankees, to announce whether he will move the team from the 73-year-old city-owned stadium.
Mr. Steinbrenner has repeatedly complained that the stadium's site in the South Bronx and traffic problems have depressed attendance at home games, even though the team is leading the American League's Eastern Division.
With his stadium lease set to expire in 2002, Mr. Steinbrenner had promised to declare his intentions at the beginning of the current baseball season, but that date passed without any decision. Instead, he has studiously avoided the public debate that was sparked last spring when another consulting firm recommended that the city and state build a new stadium for the Yankees in Manhattan, above the railyards beside the Hudson River in the West 30's.
City officials said yesterday that they expect team officials to focus again on stadium issues after the baseball season ends.
For months, the Mayor has pressed the case for a new stadium, despite the projected price tag of as much as $1.06 billion. He has said that he hopes to strike a deal that would require little taxpayer financing and that would use stadium revenues to pay off city- and state-issued bonds. A new stadium could bring revenues from ticket sales, luxury boxes and advertising, but negotiations over who would get to keep those revenues could become contentious, as Mr. Steinbrenner clearly hopes to use those same earnings to boost his own income from the team.
"We felt that it was important for us to be as knowledgeable as possible on all the options available to the city," said Fran Reiter, Deputy Mayor for Economic Development and Planning, adding that the consultants "will be sitting next to us as events unfold."
Howard Rubenstein, a spokesman for Mr. Steinbrenner, said that the team owner was traveling yesterday and could not be reached for comment. Other team officials did not return telephone calls.
Mets officials, too, did not respond to requests for an update on stadium plans. But they have said that they expect to complete a detailed study of a new stadium before the end of the year. Mets officials have said that they want to build a ball park with natural grass and a retractable dome in a parking lot next to Shea Stadium in Flushing, Queens.

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: THE SPEECH;
Clinton to Sum Up the Past and Look Ahead

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Days away from the acceptance speech that will propel him into the general election campaign, President Clinton gazed out from his train-car on Monday and pronounced his address near ready.
"It says what I want to say," he said. "Now I have to say it in the best way to say it -- and make sure it's not too long." 
 And so after a month of drafts sent back and forth between Mr. Clinton and a team of speech writers, the scramble to add the last oratorical flourishes was taking place on a rolling campaign train, while the writers competed for the President's attention with the crowds that kept drawing him out to the back platform of his Pullman car like a moth to light.
Four years ago, when Mr. Clinton last stood before a Democratic National Convention, his challenge was to introduce himself to the nation after a divisive party primary campaign. Now he is perhaps more well known than any predecessor, because of his own confessional impulses and the books his Presidency has already spawned.
That means he faces a challenge different from the one Bob Dole confronted in his own acceptance speech in San Diego earlier this month.
"What he needs to do is try to tie together his proposals into a coherent whole," said Mark Mellman, a Democratic strategist. "But most important, he has to reinforce the perception that he understands the problems that working Americans face, that he has done things to help solve those problems and has proposals for the future to help solve those problems."
Hoping to draw a pointed contrast to the sometimes elegiac tones of Mr. Dole's speech, Mr. Clinton, his aides say, plans to look forward and describe a second-term agenda in a speech intended to convey the gravitas of a State of the Union Message salted with legislative proposals and executive actions.
Aides to the President said he would also speak about the unfinished agenda in health care coverage and an urban policy to increase jobs for the welfare recipients who will now face strict benefit limits under legislation he signed last week.
Speeches have long been Mr. Clinton's great strength and occasionally his near undoing. He can spontaneously soar to oratorical glory on the campaign trail, mesmerizing crowds without a text. In the summer of 1995, he began to rebuild his Presidency with a series of addresses that staked out positions in the Republican arena of social values.
But at the 1988 Democratic National Convention, when he introduced the nominee, Michael S. Dukakis, Mr. Clinton spoke for more than an hour, leaving the delegates groaning and nearly ending his own ambitions. In his 1995 State of the Union Message, after the Republicans won control of Congress, he spoke for 54 minutes, although the length did not seem to cost him many television viewers, according to the Nielsen ratings.
To prevent such occasions, Mr. Clinton and his advisers now begin preparing a major address perhaps a month in advance, and make sure that he has enough unscheduled time to think and write and to practice.
An array of advisers have consulted with the President on this speech, chief among them Dick Morris, the President's political strategist, and the White House communications director, Don Baer, a former reporter for U.S. News & World Report.
For the last four days Mr. Baer and a full speech-writing team has been working on the train, including Michael Waldman and Jonathan Prince, as well as Mark Penn, Mr. Clinton's campaign pollster. Bruce Reed, a domestic policy adviser who has been with Mr. Clinton since his 1992 campaign, has also been on the train as the guru of the new policy proposals that will be featured.
The speech-writing group has been ensconced in a second antique rail car behind Mr. Clinton's. And for instant rewrites with the President, team members have been allotted space for a computer -- in the bathroom of his train car, on a vanity, between a shower and a toilet. They also have a second, roomier work space in a staff car several train cars back.
By midweek the writers were tightening up the policy sections of the speech while Mr. Clinton was working on the preamble and the conclusion, aides said, sometimes, with his glasses on, practicing his delivery in his train car.
"He has been done with it once or twice," said Michael D. McCurry, the White House press secretary, "then he resurrects it and works on it some more."
The President's schedulers have set aside a large block of time on Thursday for Mr. Clinton, notorious for his last-minute tinkering, to practice before teleprompters and finally sign off on the address.
Mr. McCurry added, "It will be his heart and soul by the time he stands up there tomorrow night, which means he won't be finished until tomorrow night."
Some advisers worried about whether Mr. Clinton's voice would hold out after a four-day train ride with as many as five full rally speeches a day. But unperturbed, the President was still hanging out the back of his train car today between speeches calling out to the onlookers who waved from corn fields or who waited trackside in hard hats and factory uniforms to see him go by.
"Hello, folks!" he shouted, "Good morning! How are you?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: After a month of drafts, President Clinton's acceptance speech was nearly ready, though he still worked on it on the train to the convention. (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS;
Supporting Cast

 BODY:

Among the speakers on this evening's program:
 
Nita M. Lowey
Congresswoman
Nita M. Lowey of New York was first elected to the House in 1988. She began her government career in 1975, as assistant to the New York Secretary of State for economic development and neighborhood preservation. Ms. Lowey, 59, is chairwoman of the House Pro-Choice Task Force and a member of the House Appropriations Committee. She will speak tonight on Congressional policies and how they affect families.
 
Rosa DeLauro
Congresswoman
Rosa DeLauro of Connecticut first became involved in politics in 1977, when she served as executive assistant to Mayor Frank Logue of New Haven. She later became chief of staff to Senator Christopher J. Dodd; executive director of Countdown '87, a lobbying organization that campaigned to end American aid to the contras, Nicaragua's anti-Marxist guerrillas, and executive director of Emily's List, a group that backs female Democratic candidates who favor abortion rights. She was first elected to the House in 1990. Ms. DeLauro, 53, is now chief deputy House minority whip and a member of the House National Security Committee. She will speak on teen-age pregnancy.
 
Patricia Schroeder
Congresswoman
Patricia Schroeder, 56, the sharp-tongued Coloradan who nicknamed Ronald Reagan the Teflon President, won her House seat in her first run for political office, in 1972. As a freshman, she became the first woman to sit on the House Armed Services Committee (now the National Security Committee). She has doggedly advanced women's issues. She is a House deputy whip, co-chairwoman of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee and a member of the House Judiciary and National Security Committees. Mrs. Schroeder, who is not seeking re-election, will speak on domestic violence.
 
Eleanor Holmes Norton
Delegate to Congress
Eleanor Holmes Norton, a native Washingtonian, won in her first race to be the District of Columbia's delegate to the House, in 1990. She was chairwoman of the Equal Employment Opportunity Commission under President Jimmy Carter and teaches law at Georgetown University. Ms. Norton, 59, is now vice chairwoman of the House Caucus on Women's Issues and a member of the Black Caucus. She will speak about challenges facing working women.
 
David E. Bonior
Congressman
David E. Bonior, a former Michigan state legislator, was first elected to the House in 1976. The 51-year-old Mr. Bonior has been a champion of the working middle class and of labor unions, and led a passionate crusade in 1993 against the North American Free Trade Agreement. He has been House Democratic whip since 1991, and a chief adversary of Speaker Newt Gingrich. Mr. Bonior is to speak about the concerns of working families.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Disappointing Auction Sends Bonds Lower

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The Treasury's one-day market rally was cut short yesterday, as traders gave the Government's sale of five-year notes a disappointing reception.
The 30-year bond slipped 4/32, to a price of 97 5/32; its yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, rose to 6.98 percent, from 6.97 percent on Tuesday. 
 Patrick R. Dimick, a Treasury market analyst at CS First Boston, attributed much of the day's selloff to dealer preparation for the 1 P.M. auction, rather than to the auction's results, which, he noted, were not far from expectations.
"I did not see selling on those results," Mr. Dimick said, "which suggests that it was a fairly neat auction. The bid-to-cover ratio was noticeably lower than historical norms, but I think the yield of 6.568 percent at which the Fed awarded securities was more important than the bid-to-cover ratio." Dealers were expecting 6.551 percent, according to a poll by Bloomberg Business News.
The bid-to-cover ratio, or the ratio of bids to the number of securities sold, was 2.13 to 1, less than the 2.46- to-1 average of the last 10 auctions.
"I think dealers made out all right on this auction," Mr. Dimick said. "I don't think a mass liquidation is expected since there was decent retail interest."
What disappointed dealers and traders was that while near expectations the results were not better.
The inability of overnight buying to follow through set a negative tone to the session, suggesting that prices were going to remain stuck in recent ranges. Dealers and traders did some selling heading into the auction, which is considered the harder of the two note auctions this week.
Market participants are beginning to focus on economic data scheduled for release this week and next. Today's weekly report on first-time jobless claims looms as important information for the market because the number of claims have been running low since mid-July; tomorrow's Chicago purchasing-managers report will set the expectation for next week's National Association of Purchasing Management survey.
"There is data in the first week of September which could push us into a new trading range," Mr. Dimick of CS First Boston said, adding that one factor for the market today and tomorrow is money managers who need to extend the duration of their portfolios to match bond-market indexes near the end of the month.
In futures trading, continued liquidations added to the market's malaise. Thin trading conditions exaggerated price movement as the holiday weekend approached, and few traders took positions ahead of next week's employment report.
Robin Mesch, chief fixed-income analyst for Thomson Research of Boston, said that given the long-term background of the market, the bias is toward another one-point drop in price in the coming months. "Any rallies we see over the next couple of days," Ms. Mesch said, "should be viewed as good selling opportunities. And a good spot to initiate a sell position would be toward a price of 109 16/32 to 109 24/32." She added, "Any surprises we see are likely to be on the downside."
In futures trading, which affects trading of current issues in the cash market, the September bond contract closed down 11/32 off its opening to 108 25/32.
After yesterday's auction, the market will get a long respite from new supply. Only bills are on the calendar until late September, when the next supply of two-year and five-year notes will be sold. Tomorrow the Treasury will be auctioning $30 billion of 14-day cash management bills, but that is not expected to be a problem for the market to absorb. The size of the weekly bill auctions has been cut to $24 billion and is likely to stay at that amount through the quarter.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Another accepted truth under fire: electric cars = cleaner air.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
ELECTRIC cars may be expensive today and barely capable of making the round trip to Grandma's house without a recharge. But just wait. Soon enough, they will be far more practical and start making a serious dent in the smog in Los Angeles, New York and half a dozen other cities that are light-years from compliance with Federal clean air standards.
That's the politically correct view. Which is why, despite California's decision to delay a regulation requiring substantial numbers of electric cars in the next few years, the state remains committed -- at least for now -- to force-feeding hundreds of thousands of battery-operated vehicles to auto buyers early in the next century. But is the reasoning correct? 
 Lester Lave, an economist at Carnegie-Mellon University, is convinced that the case for electric cars is deeply flawed. Along with three engineers -- A. G. Russell of Georgia Tech and Chris Hendrickson and Francis McMichael at Carnegie-Mellon -- he has just published in the journal Environmental Science and Technology an assessment of what 500,000 electric cars would probably mean to the air quality in Los Angeles and New York. It turns the standard argument for mandating zero-emissions vehicles on its head.
"The new car is no longer much of the smog problem," Mr. Lave concluded, "and therefore can't be much of the solution."
More depressing yet, Mr. Lave and his colleagues argue that electric cars are themselves environmentally suspect. For the foreseeable future they will store electricity in lead-acid batteries that will have to be manufactured from newly smelted ore or recycled from worn-out batteries -- and in the process release dangerous lead into the air, water and land. While there is considerable disagreement about how much of this lead would end up in children and other living things, it hardly seems a chance worth taking.
When cars burn gasoline they emit nitrous oxide and chemicals called volatile organic compounds. Mix them together in the presence of sunlight and you get ozone, which is the active ingredient in smog that irritates lungs and bronchia.
Electric motors emit no pollutants, which is why environmentalists (and entrepreneurs who want to sell electric vehicles) have long nurtured visions of sparkling-clear air after motorists switch from internal combustion engines. But Mr. Lave and his group offer a sobering reality check using the same computer simulation employed by the California Air Resources Board.
Substitute 500,000 electric cars for the same number of new gasoline- powered ones in Los Angeles -- a plausible number for the year 2010 -- and what happens? Not much. Peak levels of ozone are reduced, but only from 200 parts per billion to 199. The current safe level is estimated to be 120 parts per billion, and new research suggests that the target should be lowered to around 70 parts per billion to meet the law's requirement to eliminate all health risk.
New York City would fare no better. The effect of half a million electric cars would be virtually undetectable, leaving peak projected ozone levels at 190 parts per billion.
What's going on here? "We've known for quite some time that zero-emissions cars wouldn't make much difference if they substituted for very clean ones," Robert Hahn, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute, said.
And new cars are very clean: the gasoline-powered cars mandated for California remove 95 to 98 percent of the pollutants detrimental to health. Note, too, that most plants in California generating electricity for the electric cars burn natural gas, emitting pollutants that offset part of the already modest gains made at the tailpipe.
Indeed, thanks to cleaner engines, cars are no longer central to realistic strategies for dealing with smog. Extrapolating from California's own projections, Mr. Lave estimates that an all-electric car fleet would lower peak ozone in Los Angeles by just 10 percent from what it would be given the types of gasoline-powered cars projected to be on the road in 2010. By almost any standard, that makes the General Motors $35,000 two-passenger electric runabout a dubious social investment. "One big lesson here," argues Rob Stavins, an economist at the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard, "is that it doesn't pay to worry about the 'good' tail of the pollution distribution" when the "bad" tail -- much dirtier vehicles from earlier decades still on the road -- remains a factor.
More sobering still, all this illustrates how attacking one pollution problem can worsen another. The Environmental Protection Agency views the elimination of lead additives from gasoline as a great victory, with estimated benefits of roughly $100 billion annually in reduced health costs and lower rates of disease. That could be at risk unless the lead and battery industries can devise ways to contain virtually all the emissions from manufacturing and recycling the heavy metal.
But Mr. Lave wonders why anyone, other than the wannabe Bill Gateses of the battery world, would willingly take the chance with toxic lead. "The fact is," he said, "we'd not get any benefit from electric cars."

HEADLINE: In One Town, Delaying Bosnia Vote Is Bitter News

BYLINE: By MIKE O'CONNOR 

DATELINE: CAPLJINA, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Aug. 28

 BODY:
In the seething, if nearly clandestine, world of the political opposition in this Croatian town, the news that municipal elections throughout Bosnia will be postponed for months was crushing.
"Elections are the only way we can hope to change things legally," said a man in his thirties, whose years as a soldier in the army of the Bosnian Croats showed as much in his face as in the scars on his arms. 
 "Otherwise we will have to use some other way," he added. Then he paused. The word "violence" was left unsaid, but it hung in the air.
This town is still run by the people who took control during the war, as is the case in many Bosnian towns. Here the mayor, who was in office when the fighting began, became part of a power circle that included a few local military commanders and leaders of the dominant Croatian political party, according to townspeople. When the mayor's term expired, he just stayed on, accompanied by others who had come to power without being voted into office.
Muslims and ethnic Serbs who used to live here say these are the people behind the purge that sent hundreds of non-Croats to a concentration camp, then expelled them.
Townspeople and United Nations officials say that for the most part the same circle that fostered ethnic hatred remains in charge. Until there are elections, there is no legal course for removing the leaders of Capljina, or towns with similar histories.
But the international organization overseeing the elections postponed local voting at least until December because of widespread irregularities in registration. State and national elections remain set for Sept. 14.
"When you vote in the United States, it's about important social issues," said a shop owner. "Voting here means trying to get criminals out of city hall." Her store, like almost every other store here, is in a building owned by the town government. Her livelihood depends on that government's willingness to rent to her.
The town, and neighboring villages, which were made up predominantly of ethnic Croats, also housed some 4,000 Serbs and 8,000 Muslims before the war. Now, only a few hundred non-Croats remain.
Croatian refugees from other parts of Bosnia have taken the homes of the expelled people. Many feel grateful that the authorities have helped them, even those who don't like the way the town is run.
There is a group of people whose opposition to the ruling party is guaranteed: the expelled Bosnian Muslims. They are allowed to come back to vote here. But, until there are elections, any power they have to change their world is suspended.
Under the current balance of power, the term "political opposition" makes leaders of the ruling Bosnian Croat party smile. They deride the idea that the tiny group of opposition parties is a threat, and they will not respond to allegations that they took part in ethnic cleansing.
"This is the time of nationalist parties," Mijo Brajkovic, a leader of the ruling party, said today. "People will vote for us because we are strong and will protect them from the other ethnic groups."
But there are many Bosnian Croat men in this town who followed the leaders of the ruling party through nearly four years of war and who are now bitter at what it brought them.
"We fought when they told us to," said one veteran. "Then we expelled the Muslims and the Serbs when they told us to, even though many were our friends. And when they said it was to make our own government we believed that, but now it turns out it was to make a government where they can sit on a throne and steal."
No one who criticized the town government would let his or her name be used. "There are too many hand grenades flying into people's windows," said a woman.
Tales of intimidation are common, but the victims are reluctant to report incidents for fear of retaliation.
Many people said they feel the threat of violence from those in power, but the opposition is unlikely to respond with violence. That would go against recent history in the Balkans, where people have followed nationalist leaders into war, but almost never turned on leaders of their own ethnic group.
The small group at the top not only cleared out other ethnic groups, but it also controls every important aspect of the local economy, and is squeezing more tightly.
"In Capljina, everything that is bought or sold is controlled indirectly by the same few people," said a United Nations official. "I mean everything, every banana, every screw. The truck driver, the merchant, the barber, all of them depend on the good will of the government."
The businesses and farms left by expelled Serbs and Muslims are now town property. The factories, which were owned by the Government of Bosnia until Croats here rebelled, are also under its control. While few of the businesses and farms are working, they would be worth a great deal if they were sold to private investors, as is being contemplated.
"We are like chickens in their backyard," said a restaurant owner. "They have their hands around our necks and are about to strangle us."

Muslim Parties Urge Boycott
 SARAJEVO, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Aug. 28 (Reuters) -- The prospects for elections grew cloudier today when three Muslim political parties urged refugees living abroad not to cast ballots until voter registration irregularities are resolved.
About 640,000 Bosnian refugees are registered to vote in 55 countries around the world on Sept. 14.
The call for the boycott was led by the ruling Muslim nationalist Party of Democratic Action, headed by President Alija Izetbegovic. It was joined by the party headed by Bosnia's popular wartime Prime Minister, Haris Silajdzic, and by a third party headed by a former Bosnian Army commander, Sefer Halilovic.
The parties said the decision on Tuesday to postpone municipal elections sidestepped a larger problem of fraud in voter registration that would affect elections for cantonal assemblies, separate Muslim-Croat and Serb parliaments, a House of Representatives and a collective Presidency. But those elections are still scheduled to be held on Sept. 14.

HEADLINE: The People of Kwangju Recall 1980 Massacre

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: KWANGJU, South Korea, Aug. 28

 BODY:
When paratroopers stormed City Hall here 16 years ago, committing a massacre that has haunted this country ever since, Kim Young Chul was deep inside the building, clutching his gun.
Mr. Kim, who was one of the planners of the pro-democracy demonstrations, never got a chance to use his weapon. Instead, he was shot seven times and suffered head wounds. But he is still alive -- in a mental institution -- apparently still suffering from the trauma of the experience. 
 He was in the institution when he heard the news this week that former President Chun Doo Hwan had been sentenced to death for staging a coup in 1979 and brutally suppressing the Kwangju uprising in May 1980. History suddenly turned on its head: Mr. Chun was for years revered as a national hero and the protesters here were portrayed as Communist rioters. Now the protesters have become martyrs for democracy and Mr. Chun is a convict sentenced to be hanged.
"We should let him go after about a year, for he has already suffered," said Mr. Kim, 48, who has come to dislike all killing. "There should be forgiveness and reconciliation."
Few people in this southern farming region are as magnanimous as Mr. Kim. His wife, who delivered milk, sold vegetables and tired herself out at scores of jobs to raise their three children and pay her husband's medical bills, is deeply embittered. She wants the sentence carried out, and she and others are dismayed that the prison sentence for Mr. Chun's successor, Roh Tae Woo, was 22 1/2 years and not longer.
For people in Kwangju who have fought for 16 years to tell their version of the massacre and to see those responsible be punished, this is a moment to remember. The massacre was a pivotal event in modern Korean history, nurturing radicalism and anti-Americanism among a generation of youth.
An opinion poll at Seoul National University once asked students to name the greatest tragedy in Korean history since 1945. The professor who planned the poll assumed that the students would answer the Korean War, but they overwhelmingly cited the Kwangju massacre.
At Mangwol Dong Cemetery, where many of the victims are buried, visitors come from all over the country to pay tribute to those whose lives helped bring about greater democracy in this country.
"My heart has been grieving so much and so long for these people," said Cho Han Young, a 40-year-old pastor who drove more than six hours to visit the grave of a childhood friend here on a hot summer day. "I regret I was not there with them. I feel guilty for being alive now."
They also come to gain a greater understanding of the event that has been at the center of South Korean politics for the last 16 years. For years after the massacre, those who ordered it served in the top leadership, like Mr. Chun and his aides, while those who had protested for democracy were denounced as Communists or rioters.
Park Soon Lae, 63, a mother whose son was killed by paratroopers as he rode in a minivan with his fellow students, helped start one of the first organizations to fight the Government on behalf of bereaved parents. But for years, every time Mr. Chun paid a visit to this city, more than a dozen police officers would be stationed outside her house to prevent her from staging protests.
"I clapped when I heard it," Miss Park said of Mr. Chun's sentence. "I said: This is one day I feel I am really alive."
But like other people here, Miss Park says that the punishment does not ease the pain and that talk of a pardon is disconcerting.
"I expect the sentence to be implemented," she said, as she pulled some weeds from around her son's flowered grave. "If not, I will fight to the end, until all my strength expires."
In the spring of 1980, protesters in Kwangju broke into armories and stole weapons, which briefly allowed them to gain control of the city. After 10 days of protests, the army invaded with tanks and machine guns.
The Government says that the death toll was 193, but it eventually agreed to compensate the family members of 288 victims of the massacre. Beyond that figure, 23 soldiers were also killed, most apparently by other troops, although some were killed by protesters.
Many relatives have dedicated their lives to fighting for their sons and brothers, daughters and sisters who died in the Kwangju massacre. Hong Soon Baek, a 37-year-old businessman, for instance, gave up his business after a car accident nearly killed him. During his recovery, he had a change in outlook and left his business to become the custodian of the Mangwol Cemetery where his brother, who was killed in the massacre, is buried.
A broader legacy of the Kwangju massacre has been an even more ferocious regionalism in South Korea, and it is still unclear if the conviction of Mr. Chun will ease that. The province that includes Kwangju is economically backward and its people and those from the rest of the country rarely have nice things to say about each other.
So while there is some relief and healing as a result of the sentencing of Mr. Chun, there is also deep skepticism about whether a rapprochment with the rest of the country is really at hand.
"The people of Kwangju have to make more effort to breed more politicians," said Y. H. Chung, 22, a kindergarten teacher who dislikes all the demonstrating around her. "When we have a President from our region, then we will benefit economically."

HEADLINE: Panel Members in Dissension On Hartford School Problems

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Seven weeks after the State Supreme Court ruled that the concentration of poor minority students in Hartford's public schools violated the State Constitution, a state panel began seeking solutions to the problem today.
Panel members were quick to acknowledge the magnitude of the challenge. 
 "We will have to reach a genius level to do in four months what so many have not been able to do in 40 years," said Ben Andrews, president of the Connecticut N.A.A.C.P., who is a panel member.
In fact, the 22-member committee could not agree today on what its focus should be: improving urban education, desegregating the schools or addressing the economic problems that underlie both the racial isolation and the poor quality of many urban schools.
The panel's first meeting also hinted at the divisions that are likely to develop as Republicans and Democrats and urban and suburban residents try to draft recommendations for the Legislature by Jan. 22, the deadline set by the Governor.
For example, State Representative Edna I. Garcia, a Democrat of Bridgeport, said she hoped the panel would address the violations of the State Constitution cited in the Supreme Court decision. But State Senator John A. Kissel, a Republican from Enfield, said the group should take into account the lower court ruling, which the Supreme Court overturned, and the minority Supreme Court opinion, which said there was no constitutional violation.
In its July 9 decision, the court ruled 4 to 3 that the high concentration of black and Latino students in Hartford's schools violated the State Constitution's guarantee of "a substantially equal educational opportunity," and ordered the Governor and the General Assembly to remedy the problem. The Hartford schools are about 95 percent minority.
One possible solution would be integrating the Hartford schools with their suburban neighbors.
Yet the court offered no remedies, and so Gov. John G. Rowland set up the panel to make proposals. Mr. Rowland called on the committee members today to be fair, thoughtful, open to public opinions and creative.
"We need to make sure every child in the state of Connecticut has equal opportunity and equal access," Mr. Rowland said, adding, "We will all be judged by the final outcome."
Afterward, Elizabeth Horton-Sheff, a plaintiff in the desegregation lawsuit, known as Sheff v. O'Neill, said she was upset by the lack of agreement that racial segregation was the most important issue.
"That's playing around with the decision, and it's an affront to the court," Ms. Horton-Sheff said.
Joseph S. Grabarz Jr., executive director of the Connecticut Civil Liberties Union, which helped represent the plaintiffs, said that any solution that did not address segregation would not work. "For the panel not to consider racial isolation as the key to any remedy I think would be fooling themselves," he said.
Theodore S. Sergi, the State Education Commissioner, who is the panel's chairman, said it must adhere to the court decision and the charge of the Governor: to address racial isolation, improve education and involve parents. "I find it hard to separate the three," he said.
The panel is to meet 11 times, and hold two sets of public hearings, one set at the beginning and another when it has some rough recommendations. The first hearings are Sept. 17, 18 and 19 in at least 11 sites.
State Senator Kevin B. Sullivan of West Hartford, a panel member, cautioned that it must produce workable solutions. "This could be a really important step in coming to terms with insufficient equal opportunity and education results for the great bulk of our urban children," he said, "or it's going to be six months of tinkering and thinking we've done something."
