HEADLINE: I.B.M. Helps Lift Blue Chips; Dow Up 1.11, to 5,712.38

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Blue-chip stocks ended with a small gain in quiet trading yesterday, lifted by a rise in shares of I.B.M.
The Dow Jones industrial average closed up 1.11 points at 5,712.38. 
 Advancing issues narrowly led decliners by 11 to 9 on the New York Stock Exchange. Broader market indexes were mixed. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.59 points, to 664.81. The Nasdaq composite index rose 4.86 points, to 1,153.88. Volume was light, with 296.4 million shares traded on the New York Stock Exchange.
The Dow had gained more than 10 points in the morning exclusively on the strength of I.B.M., which ended 3 1/4 points higher at 115.
Salomon Brothers raised its rating on I.B.M. stock, noting that the quarter ending June 30 saw its "best revenue acceleration in the past five quarters," and that the outlook for the second half of 1996 and for 1997 "is very positive."
But stock prices eased in early afternoon as bond prices slipped, pushing up interest rates.
The 30-year Treasury bond, a benchmark for long-term borrowing costs, dipped in price, with its yield nudging closer to 7 percent. The nervousness in the bond market spilled over into stocks.
"Our audience is so limited that any move is going to be exacerbated," said Bill Allyn, director of listed equity trading at Jefferies & Company in Short Hills, N.J.
Mr. Allyn also cited persistent worries, which began last week, that the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates soon. "The Fed, as you know, has been making some rumblings that it is not as serene as we once thought it was," he said.
Higher rates, which raise corporate borrowing costs, reduce business investment, curb consumer spending and cut into corporate profits.
Losses in some Dow components nearly overwhelmed the big gain in I.B.M. shares. The interest-rate-sensitive banking giant J. P. Morgan fell 3/4, to 89 1/8.
Caterpillar lost 7/8, to 70 1/8. The large construction- and farming-equipment maker said it had reached a bargaining impasse with union workers and would put into effect new working conditions, which had previously been rejected by the United Automobile Workers. The U.A.W. denied there was an impasse.
The Dow component Philip Morris ended 1/2 lower at 91 3/8. The stock rose Monday and Tuesday after an Indianapolis jury ruled late last week in favor of the tobacco industry in a smoking liability case. Yesterday, the company said that it would raise its dividend by 20 percent.
Shares of another Dow component, Boeing, rose 7/8, to 92 1/8. The company said on Tuesday that it would increase production of its 777 airliner by 40 percent,
Pepsico led the most-active list on the New York Stock Exchange for the second consecutive session, falling 1, to 30. The soft drink bottler continues to face setbacks in its battle with Coca-Cola for market share, particularly in South America.
H & R Block fell 2, to 28 7/8. The company postponed a shareholder vote on completing the spinoff of the Compuserve Corporation, citing Compuserve's recent poor performance and uncertainty in the on-line industry. Block sold 20 percent of its interest in Compuserve in April in an initial public offering, and shareholders were scheduled to vote Sept. 11 on selling Block's remaining 80 percent interest.
First Mississippi rose 3 1/8, to 27 1/8, after Mississippi Chemical, which gained 1 3/8, to 22 5/8, agreed to acquire First Mississippi's fertilizer operations in a deal valued at $297 million.
 Garden Botanika slumped 4 3/4, to 8 1/2. It reported a loss for the latest quarter and said that its August sales were disappointing.

HEADLINE: Group Seeks Reparations As Detainees

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
They were pulled from schoolhouses in Colombia and Bolivia and from offices and stores around Lima, Peru. Warships carried them to the United States, and trains left them at desolate camps near places like Missoula, Mont., and Crystal City, Tex. Some of them remained there, under guard and behind fences, for years.
Although more than 2,000 of these Latin Americans of Japanese descent were deported to the United States in World War II, and forcibly interned on the orders of President Franklin D. Roosevelt, they have been little more than a footnote in this nation's history. 
 When the Federal Government began issuing apologies and reparations checks to Japanese-Americans held in the camps, most of those who came from Latin America were left out. Today, they began a campaign for redress. In a class-action suit filed in Federal District Court in Los Angeles, hundreds of people uprooted from at least a dozen Latin American countries asked the United States Government for the same consideration it has given to Japanese-Americans under the Civil Liberties Act of 1988.
"Why are we left out?" asked Arthur Shibayama, 66, a retired gas station owner from San Jose, Calif., who was deported from Peru as a boy, held for years in a Texas camp, and was then denied United States citizenship for years even though he had served in Europe as a soldier for the United States.
"We suffered at least as much as the Japanese-Americans, maybe more," said Mr. Shibayama, who finally became a citizen in 1970. "I want the United States Government to take responsibility for the suffering that my family and my friends went through."
In addition to the formal apology and the reparations payments of $20,000 available to the Japanese-Americans interned in the camps, the plaintiffs asked for punitive damages but did not specify any amount.
A spokesman for the Justice Department, Myron Marlin, said the Government would have no comment on the details of the lawsuit. But Mr. Marlin said that in denying benefits to most of the internees who were brought from Latin America, the Clinton Administration was simply applying a law over which it had had no influence.
Little explanation was given for why the Latin Americans were mostly excluded as amends began to be made in 1990. But officials have argued that those brought to the camps from abroad were effectively illegal immigrants and therefore not entitled to the same rights as the roughly 100,000 Japanese-American internees.
Mr. Marlin noted that any Japanese Latin American who was granted citizenship by 1952 was given it retroactively to the time they arrived in this country. About 150 people have qualified for reparations, but more than 300 applications have been denied.
One of the lawyers who filed the suit today, Robin S. Toma, described the Government's position as a perverse catch-22.
"The Government was not telling these people that they could apply for citizenship," said Mr. Toma, the son of Japanese-American internees. "They were telling them that they were being deported."
Of the 2,264 people of Japanese ancestry who were deported from Latin America from 1942 to 1944 and detained until as late as 1952, Mr. Toma said, nearly all of those who met the Congressional test became citizens in 1952 with the help of by a civil-rights lawyer in California.
According to the 1982 Congressional report by a special commission that studied the Government's wartime internment policies in the preparation for the 1988 law, the Japanese Latin Americans were among about 3,000 people from a dozen Latin American nations. They were brought to camps in the United States when their own Governments were unable to comply with Washington's pressure for their internment at home. The remainder were of German and Italian descent.
The arrest and transfer of Latin Americans to United States camps began, the report noted, as "a controlled, closely monitored deportation program to detain potentially dangerous diplomatic and consular officials." But the exercise quickly deteriorated into one in which prominent ethnic Japanese -- teachers, small-businessmen and wealthy farmers, especially -- were rounded up and shipped off with no regard to their national loyalties.
Pressed by the Roosevelt Administration, Brazil, which had the largest Japanese population in Latin America, interned thousands of Japanese. In all, about 8,500 Latin American immigrants and their descendants from the Axis countries were held in 16 Latin American nations. None have paid reparations to the people they held, lawyers for the plaintiffs said. About 200 Latin Americans held in the United States were eventually allowed to go home.
The more than 25,000 Japanese in Peru became special targets. Although many of the country's Japanese immigrants had gone there as low-paid farm laborers at the turn of the century, they were the victims of cultural prejudices like those directed at Peru's Indians, the country's largest ethnic group. The success of ethnic Japanese in business during an era of general economic difficulties deepened the resentment against them.
Libia Yamamoto, the sales manager for a charter bus company in San Francisco, recalled how the local constable in her hometown, Chiclayo, on Peru's northern coast, arrived one night in January 1943, apologizing sympathetically to her father, the well-known owner of a store on the local sugar plantation. Then the constable took her father away.
Nearly eight months later, the family was reunited in Crystal City, where Mrs. Yamamoto's parents were told that they and others would be deported to Japan in exchange for American civilians being held there.
"There was nothing," she recalled of the heavily guarded camp. "It was almost barren."
When her father became ill and was hospitalized, the family avoided joining the more than 800 Japanese Latin Americans who were sent to Japan in December 1945. Over the years, Mrs. Yamamoto said, she entered American-citizenship classes and picked up application forms, only to have her efforts founder on one question: had she ever been deported from any country?
"I always got to that point," she said, "and I didn't have the right answer."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Libia Yamamoto is among the more than 2,000 people of Japanese descent who were deported from Latin America to the United States in World War II and then held in internment camps. Many of them are now seeking a formal apology and reparations from the United States Government. (Jim Wilson/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Target Chain, Citing Costs, to Stop Selling Cigarettes

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER 

 BODY:
The Target chain, the nation's third-largest discount retailer, said yesterday that it would stop selling cigarettes because it was costing too much to keep them out of the hands of minors.
The decision by Target, which has 714 stores in 37 states, is the first time such a large retail chain has dropped the selling of tobacco. 
 While the move is expected to have very little impact on the availability of cigarettes, it comes at a time when retailers have been concerned about the growing burden of regulations intended to curb teen-age smoking. Federal regulations adopted early this year put more pressure on states to enforce laws barring the sale of tobacco products to children under the age of 18. And new regulations issued last week by the Food and Drug Administration will place limits on how cigarettes can be displayed and sold starting next year.
Target, a subsidiary of the Dayton Hudson Corporation, said cigarettes accounted for less than half of 1 percent of its $15.8 billion in sales last year. Tobacco had produced little or no profit in recent years after theft and expenses related to efforts to stop sales to minors were taken into account, Target said.
"It costs lots of money to retrofit stores to put cigarettes behind counters and to service tobacco customers," said Carolyn Brookter, a Target spokeswoman. "It's just too labor intensive."
But the two largest discount chains, Wal-Mart Stores and Kmart, said yesterday that they would continue to sell cigarettes. The Walgreen Company, the nation's largest drugstore chain, also said that it would continue to sell cigarettes.
"Target has simply removed itself from the middle of a controversy in which it really had no stake," said Leo J. Shapiro, a leading retail industry consultant based in Chicago.
Still, he said, "They've done first what a number of other chains are likely to do later."
Drugstore chains are the most likely to follow suit, although not because they are disappointed with the profits from selling tobacco, according to retailing experts.
"Several major chains are considering it in connection with adding medical services in order to position themselves as health care providers," said Eric Sorenson, managing director of the Center for Retail Management at the Northwestern University's Kellogg School of Business.
Tobacco industry analysts, however, were skeptical that Target's decision on tobacco would soon be followed by major rivals.
"There may be some investors who view Target's announcement as another negative for tobacco stocks but it is no reflection on the industry's ability to distribute cigarettes," said Martin Feldman, who follows the tobacco industry for Smith Barney in New York.
Chains like Target represent a small fraction of tobacco sales, analysts noted. Retail outlets like convenience stores are often far more dependent on tobacco sales and will have little choice but to absorb the added cost of complying with new regulations.
"About 42 percent of our customers are smokers, which is way above the national average," said Teri Richmond, spokeswoman for the National Association of Convenience Stores, which represents businesses owning 70,000 retail outlets.
Target executives stressed yesterday that the decision to stop selling cigarettes was an economic one. They said that the company stopped selling tobacco in 50 stores two years ago in a test that helped it conclude that it would not suffer financially by quitting completely.
It quietly began the move to end all sales early this month and did not acknowledge the new policy until it received an inquiry on Tuesday from The St. Paul Pioneer Press. It expects to have all cigarettes off its shelves by the end of September.
Whatever the motive, the move by Target is likely to enhance its image as a family-oriented enterprise. The chain removed Playboy magazine from its shelves in the 1970's and is among the most prominent retailers to have stopped selling toy guns. Its corporate parent, which is based in Minneapolis, has been praised for setting aside 5 percent of its profits annually for charity.
While Target was not known for promoting tobacco with discounts or other promotions, cigarettes had been hurting its image in its home state of Minnesota. Target stores were singled out as among the easiest places for teen-agers to buy or steal cigarettes in Minneapolis by undercover buying operations in 1992. Some Target stores had been fined or had licenses to sell tobacco temporarily suspended in Minnesota towns with tobacco control ordinances.
In Target stores that sold cigarettes, the packs were often at the end of aisle displays where shoppers had access. In many cases, clerks who were trained to be quick at checkout counters were often reluctant to take the time to check a customer's identification.
Target's move was hailed by anti-tobacco groups. "Big stores like Target aren't capable of selling cigarettes in a way that they don't fall into the hands of children," said Jeanne Weigum, head of the Association for Nonsmokers-Minnesota.
But tobacco industry representatives said Target was simply handing a high-volume business to its major competitors.
"I guess they are not very good marketers," said Walker Merryman, a spokesman for the Tobacco Institute in Washington.

Dayton Hudson Sets Sale
MINNEAPOLIS, Aug. 28 (By Bloomberg Business News) -- The Dayton Hudson Corporation said today that it had it agreed to sell its four Marshall Field's stores in Texas to concentrate on its Field's stores in the Midwest.
Saks Holding Inc. will buy three of the stores and Federated Department Stores Inc. will purchase the other, which will be operated by Federated's Macy's West division. The four stores have total annual revenue of about $120 million.
Dayton Hudson said the sale was expected to result in a "slight gain" in the fourth quarter. Financial details were not disclosed.

HEADLINE: Metro Digest

 BODY:

THE CONVENTION
 
Dodd Nominates Clinton
Senator Christopher J. Dodd of Connecticut made the nominating speech at the Democratic National Convention, saying that he would not question the reputation of Bob Dole, the Republican candidate, but rather his "flawed ideas," and stating that "President Clinton has put our economic house in order." [Page A1.]
 
Many delegates, particularly New York City's, say they have been inundated with angry calls about the new welfare law. [B9.]
 
NEW YORK
 
Class Spills Outside Classroom
New York City's public schools are so crowded that thousands of the 1.06 million schoolchildren will report to class next week at such unlikely places as a converted World War II torpedo factory in Brooklyn, a renovated department store in Queens, the Town Hall theater in midtown Manhattan and dozens of temporary aluminum trailers scattered in schoolyards across the city. [A1.]
 
Crazy, No. Facing Trial, Yes.
Vincent Gigante, considered the most powerful Mafia boss in America but perhaps best known for walking through his neighborhood in a bathrobe and pajamas, was declared mentally competent to stand trial on murder and racketeering charges by a judge who called his odd behavior an "elaborate deception." [B1.]
The Genovese crime family has suffered three severe blows, capped by the ruling on Mr. Gigante. [B6.]
The judge in the case, Eugene H. Nickerson, does not shrink from making tough decisions. [B6.]
 
Giuliani's Second-in-Command
Tightening his inner circle of top aides, Mayor Giuliani promoted his chief of staff, Randy M. Mastro, to the position of Deputy Mayor for Operations, effectively making him the second-in-command at City Hall. Mr. Mastro replaces Peter J. Powers but will not receive Mr. Powers's former title of First Deputy Mayor. [B1.]
 
From Depths, Artifacts of Life
To an archeologist and her students, history is an outhouse in SoHo. The group dug into the foundation of the privy and found items either dropped in accidentally or used to fill in the hole when inside plumbing arrived about 120 years ago. What is emerging from those pieces is a picture of middle-class Irish life. [B1.]
 
When a Race Is All-Out War
A battle between Democrats is under way in Manhattan Surrogate's Court, where Judge Renee R. Roth is seeking another term. To her chagrin, the judge finds herself in a primary fight with Karen S. Burstein, the former Family Court judge. The race has riled tempers, split loyalties and turned into something of a morality play for feminists. Metro Matters by Joyce Purnick. [B1.]
 
Rethinking Stadiums and Funds
Months after speculation that the Yankees would abandon their South Bronx home subsided, the stadium debate resurfaced when city officials announced that they had hired a financial consultant to help the city devise ways to pay for new or renovated stadiums for both the Yankees and the Mets. [B3.]
 
Facing More Accusations
Adelphi's former internal auditor told a state panel reviewing charges of mismanagement at the university that Peter Diamandopoulos, Adelphi's president, promoted an employee to a key managerial post even after the employee was found to be stealing on the job. [B5.]
 
Mass Rabies Alert Issued
Since last week, when a goat being exhibited at a county fair in Owego was killed after it was found to have rabies, health officials have been conducting the largest rabies vaccination effort in New York State history. Officials say that as many as 25,000 people visited the fair; one man reported that his 11-year-old daughter's finger was nipped, and some people may have been exposed to the disease by a friendly lick on a hand that had an innocuous cut. [B6.]
 
REGION
 
Crash Search Moves Eastward
Stymied by a string of dead ends and partial hints, the hunt for the cause of the blast that destroyed T.W.A. Flight 800 shifted several miles east, as Navy divers focused on the easternmost patch of wreckage in the Atlantic Ocean. More than a third of the 180-ton jumbo jet still lies on the sea floor in that area. [B5.]
 
CONNECTICUT
 
Panel on Schools Faces Discord
Seven weeks after the State Supreme Court ruled that the concentration of poor minority students in Hartford's public schools violated the State Constitution, a panel began seeking solutions. The first meeting hinted at divisions likely to develop as Republicans and Democrats and urban and suburban residents try to draft recommendations by the Jan. 22 deadline. [B4.]

HEADLINE: I.B.M. Is Starting to Sell Networking Chips

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: ARMONK, N.Y., Aug. 28

 BODY:
The International Business Machines Corporation has started selling its proprietary networking chips for the growing multibillion-dollar market.
I.B.M., which has been selling other computer chips since 1992, said the new products included chips and technologies that distribute sound, data and video through communications networks. 
 The company previously made the networking chips mostly for its own products. It said it now planned to market the chips aggressively to outside customers.
Specifically, I.B.M. will sell chips intended to support technologies called asynchronous transfer mode, or ATM, and token ring. The devices are largely used in communications systems to send data in small packets.
The networking products represent the largest segment of a communications market that is expected to double to about $60 billion in the next four years, according to an analysis by Dataquest Inc. that was cited by I.B.M.
I.B.M. is already a leading source of chips for the broader computer market. Industry observers estimate its computer chip sales reached $2.3 billion in 1995.

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: ENDORSEMENTS;
Former Rival Won't Back Perot's Race For President

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Richard D. Lamm, the former Colorado Governor who failed to take the Reform Party's Presidential nomination from Ross Perot, announced today that he would not endorse Mr. Perot or any other Presidential candidate this year.
Mr. Lamm said he hoped to campaign for Reform Party candidates for other offices, but he refused to explain why he would not publicly support Mr. Perot. 
 "I will endorse Ross Perot as prophet, I will endorse Ross Perot as Paul Revere, I will endorse him as prescient; I will not endorse him as President," Mr. Lamm said in a news conference ostensibly called to discuss the need to change the Medicare system.
When asked why he felt that way, Mr. Lamm said, "For the same reasons everybody in this room knows."
He refused to explain further, saying, "I'm going to keep my reasons private."
He did make the point that "no viable American political party is ever going to be sustained by just one person."
Mr. Lamm waged a bitter six-week campaign against Mr. Perot, who founded and financed the Reform Party and who, after being coy about his intentions, jumped into the race for its Presidential nomination one day after Mr. Lamm announced that he would seek it. Mr. Lamm complained repeatedly that Mr. Perot used the party's organizational machinery to rig the results of the balloting this month.
Mr. Lamm, a former Democrat, made the announcement in Chicago to attract reporters covering the Democratic National Convention here. He commended Mr. Perot for speaking forthrightly about the need to save Medicare from financial collapse, but he said that was not reason enough to endorse him.
"Tom Brokaw is a wonderful anchorman, too," Mr. Lamm said, "but I don't want to endorse him for President. In other words, certain people play certain roles."
Mr. Lamm said he had been deeply disappointed by the failure of the Democrats to seriously deal with what he called the imminent financial collapse of Medicare. Defending Medicare in this form is like defending a snowman from melting in your front yard, Mr. Lamm said. He accused the Democrats of consistently refusing to seek long-term solutions to the Medicare problem.
"This is the party that's most disappointed me," he said. "I expected it from the Republicans."

SECTION: Section B; Page 12; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 2551 words

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS;
Gore Speech: 'America Is Strong. Bill Clinton's Leadership Is Paying Off.'

BYLINE: Reuters 

 BODY:
Following are remarks last night at the Democratic National Convention by Vice President Al Gore:
Four years ago, you gave me your nomination to be Vice President. Tonight, I want to say from the bottom of my heart, thank you for the opportunity to serve our country and for the privilege of working beside a President who has done so much to lift the lives of America's families. 
 Tradition holds that this speech be delivered tomorrow night. But President Clinton asked me to speak tonight. And you can probably guess the reason why: my reputation for excitement.
This is some crowd. I've been watching you doing that macarena on television. And if I could have your silence, I would like to demonstrate for you, the Al Gore version of the macarena.
Would you like to see it again?
Four years ago, America faced a set of problems that our leaders had lost the courage to confront. Our nation was not creating jobs. Our jobs were not increasing pay. Our people were running in place. Our nation was falling behind.
Four years later we meet in this great city of Chicago, the place Carl Sandberg called "the city of the Big Shoulders . . . with lifted head so proud to be alive . . . and strong."
Four years later, Democrats are proud. Our hopes are alive. And America is strong.
Bill Clinton's leadership is paying off.
How can you tell?
By what the American people have achieved themselves. Just look at what all of us have created together these last four years.
Ten million new jobs. A deficit cut in half. A smaller, leaner reinvented government working better and costing less. Unemployment and inflation both down. Record exports. Wages on the rise. An economy moving forward. Empowerment zones bringing neighborhoods back to life. Classrooms being connected to the information superhighway.
Communities given the right to know about environmental dangers. Toxic wastes being cleaned up. Rivers and lakes reclaimed and thriving. An America not just better off, but better.
And our strength at home has led to renewed respect abroad. Nuclear missiles no longer targeted at our cities. Democracy replacing tyranny in Haiti. Peace replacing war in Bosnia. Leadership toward reconciliation in Northern Ireland and the Middle East.
While our nation has made great progress, we have much more to do. And we are here to declare that the man who can help us fashion this better future is President Bill Clinton.
The President's opponent, Senator Bob Dole, is a good and decent man. We honor his service to America and his personal courage in fighting back from injuries sustained in battle.
Though we disagree with his ideas, only the unknowing would deny him the respect he deserves.
But make no mistake: there is a profound difference in outlook between the President and the man who seeks his office. In his speech from San Diego, Senator Dole offered himself as a bridge to the past.
Tonight Bill Clinton and I offer ourselves as a bridge to the future.
Ralph Waldo Emerson once said "Humanity is divided between the past and the future . . . between memory and hope."
It is easy to understand the nostalgic appeal of the party of memory and the men who lead it. But let there be no doubt. The future lies with the party of hope -- and the man from Hope who leads it.
We Americans write our own history. And the chapters of which we're proudest are the ones where we had the courage to change.
Time and again, Americans have seen the need for change and have taken the initiative to bring that change to life, but always with a struggle. Always there were opponents.
Senator Dole was there. We remember. We remember that he voted against the creation of Medicare, against the creation of Medicaid, against the Clean Air Act, against Head Start, against the Peace Corps in the 60's, and Americorps in the 90's.
He even voted against the funds to send a man to the moon.
If he's the most optimistic man in America, I'd hate to see the pessimists.
That pessimistic view of America is very different from ours.
And we saw, in the budget that Senator Dole and Speaker Gingrich tried to slip past the American people last fall. Their budget doubled Medicare premiums while slashing benefits, wiped out nursing home care for seniors, ended the guarantee of medical care to disabled children, rolled back protections for our air and water, increased the cost of college while making students loans harder to get, terminated anti-drug programs for our schools, and raised taxes on the hardest-hit working families.
They passed their reckless plan and then demanded that President Clinton sign it.
They shut the government down, twice, because they thought Bill Clinton would buckle under the pressure, wither in the face of their attacks, cave in to their demands.
But they did not know the true measure of this man.
He never flinched or wavered.
He never stooped to their level. And of course, he never attacked his opponent's wife.
Let me tell you what Bill Clinton did do.
President Clinton took Speaker Newt Gingrich and Senator Bob Dole into the Oval Office. I was there, I remember. And he said, President Clinton said, "As long as I occupy this office, you will never enact this plan because as long as I am President, I won't let you."
That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want someone in that Oval Office who will rubber stamp their plan. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want a President who will appoint the next three justices of the Supreme Court so they can control all three branches of Government and take away a woman's right to choose. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want to give health insurance rip-off artists a license to change Medicare, to let this program for our seniors wither on the vine. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want to outlaw all affirmative action and many other measures to reach out to those who want to reach up. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want to cut education and undermine our schools, put down teachers instead of lifting up students. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
They want to give free reign to lobbyists for the biggest polluters in America to rewrite our environmental laws, allowing more poison in our air and water, and then auction off our natural wonders piece by piece. That's why they want to replace Bill Clinton. But we won't let them.
We will not, we cannot, we must not let them.
And you know what? We can make Bill Clinton's job a lot easier by making Dick Gephardt Speaker of the House and Tom Daschle Senate majority leader.
You can judge a President by the enemies he is willing to make.
You know someone who's been attacked as much as Bill Clinton is doing something right.
American has never changed without a President willing to confront the status quo and take on the forces of greed and indifference. It has changed only when we have had a President with the vision to tackle the real problems that really matter to our families. That's what this President has done, because families don't eat or breath political slogans. They thrive or fail according to how they handle each day's challenges.
When your alarm goes off in the morning, if your family is like mine, everybody starts rushing around getting ready for school and work.
When one of your children reaches for cereal and fruit you shouldn't have to worry about whether the food is safe. That's why just this month President Clinton brought farmers and environmentalists together and signed an historic law to keep dangerous pesticides off our fruits and vegetables.
When you pour a glass of water for each member of the family at the table you shouldn't have to wonder -- should I buy bottled water? We really can't afford it.
That's why President Clinton signed the Safe Drinking Water Act to give families more peace of mind that their water will be pure and safe.
When you notice your child staring at a television set and watching violent and explicit images he or she is not old enough to handle, you shouldn't be forced to choose between throwing the TV out of the house and monitoring every second that child watches. That is why last month the President persuaded the broadcasters to agree to air three hours of quality children's educational programming every single week.
And that's why we're giving parents a new tool, the V-chip, to keep violent and explicit programming out of their homes and away from their children. When our children turn on the TV, let them learn how to read and add and spell and think, not how to kill.
If one of your children has an operation, or some other serious health problem, you shouldn't have to choose between taking care of that child or keeping your job. That is why Bill Clinton fought to pass the Family and Medical Leave Act, so parents can get time off to work to care for a sick child, bond with a newborn, or tend to an aging relative.
When your children do well in school and head toward graduation, they shouldn't have to wonder about whether their family can afford to send them to college. That is why President Clinton expanded scholarships, student loans and Pell Grants.
And that's why he wants to give a tax credit to pay $1,500 per year for tuition to make college more affordable for every single American family.
If the business where you work is changing in ways that cause you to think about getting a different kind of job, you ought to be able to get the training and education you need to learn new skills and plan for the future. That's why President Clinton is proposing a tax credit so if you go to a community college, you can take every single dollar you pay right off your taxes.
If you take responsibility, President Clinton will give you the opportunity to learn.
And if you see an opportunity to move to a better job, you shouldn't feel forced to stay in your old job just because that's the only way you can keep your health insurance.
Even if you have some pre-existing condition, you ought to be able to change jobs and not lose your coverage. That's why President Clinton signed the Kennedy-Kassebaum law.
Americans shouldn't have to feel imprisoned in their homes because of crime. We have a right to streets and neighborhoods that are safe. That's why President Clinton fought for the Brady Bill and the assault weapons ban, and that's why he's putting 100,000 new community police officers on our streets and sidewalks.
These problems are real, and they must be addressed. It's been a long time since we've had a President so in tune with the issues that touch the real lives of America's families. It's been a long time since we've had a President willing to fight the powerful forces that often seem to stand in the way.
Some of the most powerful forces that do the most harm are often hard to see and even harder to understand.
When I was a child, my family was attacked by an invisible force that was then considered harmless. My sister Nancy was older than me. There were only the two of us and I loved her more than life itself.
She started smoking when she was 13 years old.
The connection between smoking and lung cancer had not yet been established, but years later, the cigarettes had taken their toll. It hurt very badly to watch her savaged by that terrible disease.
Her husband, Frank, and all of us who loved her so much tried to get her to stop smoking. Of course, she should have. But she couldn't.
When she was 45, she had a lung removed. A year later, the disease had come back, and she returned to the hospital. We all took turns staying with her.
One day, I was called to come quickly because things had taken a turn for the worse. By then, her pain was nearly unbearable, and as a result, they used very powerful painkillers. Eventually, it got so bad they had to use such heavy doses that she could barely retain consciousness.
We sometimes didn't know if she could hear what we were saying or recognize us.
But when I responded to that call and walked into the hospital room that day, as soon as I turned the corner, someone said, "Al's here."
She looked up and from out of that haze, her eyes focused tensely right at me. She couldn't speak, but I felt clearly I knew she was forming a question.
Do you bring me hope?
All of us have tried to find whatever new treatment or new approach might help. But all I could do was to say back to her, with all the gentleness in my heart, "I love you."
And then I knelt by her bed and held her hand. And in a very short time, her breathing became labored and then she breathed her last breath.
Tomorrow morning, another 13-year-old girl will start smoking. I love her too.
Three thousand young people in America will start smoking tomorrow. One thousand of them will die a death not unlike my sister's. And that is why until I draw my last breath, I will pour my heart and soul into the cause of protecting our children from the dangers of smoking.
And that is also why I was intensely proud last week when President Clinton stood up for American families by standing up to tobacco advertising aimed at getting our children addicted.
He proposed the first-ever comprehensive plan to protect children from smoking, to ban tobacco advertising aimed at our children and to ban it for good.
It took courage for Bill Clinton to take on the tobacco companies. I promise you it is no accident that no President has ever been willing to do it before.
But coming from him, that's no surprise. I've seen him get up day after day and make the toughest decisions, and always by asking what is right for the American people.
As a result, with Bill Clinton's leadership, our nation is moving forward with confidence. Americans don't believe our best days are behind us. We see better days ahead because we have the courage to meet our challenges and protect our values.
And now, once again, in pursuit of the American Dream, we are crossing the bridge to the future.
Thirty-three years ago this very day, one American told us about his dream. Martin Luther King, Jr. stood in the shadow of the Lincoln Memorial on that August afternoon and described the America that he saw in his mind's eye.
He called on our nation "to open the doors of opportunity to all of God's children."
How do we build an America worthy of this dream? Is it with brick and mortar? Is it with second-hand smoke and rear-view mirrors? Or do we build it in the nearest well-raised child?
By shepherding, guiding and protecting our children's souls we build a better America. The American spirit lives within that child. The child grows up to believe in it, to add new vision to it. It's not a vision of a distant future, nor of a remote past, but a constant accumulation of our best instincts and our noblest aspirations.
From the spirit of our Founding Fathers to the courage of today's families it is one vision. It is an American vision. It is the vision of the President Bill Clinton.
Thank you, God bless you, and God bless America.

PUB-DATE: August 29, 1996

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; National Desk 

LENGTH: 2724 words

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: MAN IN THE NEWS;
The Incumbent as a Riddle: William Jefferson Clinton

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
On Thursday night, when he accepts the renomination of a Democratic Party that he has alternately inspired and exasperated, William Jefferson Clinton will be at once the best-known and least-knowable politician in the land.
He is a moderate who cannot suppress his liberal impulses, a liberal who cannot escape his moderating instincts. He confounds his friends, who think he has agreed with them, only to find he has not. He confuses his enemies, who think they can work with him, only to find they cannot. 
 He is a tender man, whose eyes well with tears at the sad tales of strangers, and a heedless man, who in the last four years has left a trail littered with old friends in disgrace or in jail. He is a thoughtful man, with an abiding sense of the sweep of history, and a careless man, with a troubling disregard for the inconvenient details of his own life.
Four years ago, Mr. Clinton's lieutenants called him "Secretariat," in tribute to the thoroughbred daring he exhibited at his best. Some top aides now use another nickname that sums up his less flattering side. He is "The Boy," with unruly appetites that let him drag a meeting past its time, keep a roomful of visitors waiting or tear up a speech at the last minute -- sometimes to brilliant effect, but often at the expense of a clear decision or explanation.
To listen to Mr. Clinton talk for any time at all is to enter a realm of hyperarticulation without apparent limit. He can muster the same enthusiasm for a discussion of post-cold war politics and tax credits for retraining workers as he can for a suddenly shattered golf driver, the college basketball polls or the size of the British Prime Minister's hands. (They are big.)
"Fundamentally, he is a good person who is very altruistic in nature," said Betsey Wright, his longtime chief of staff as Governor of Arkansas who knows him as well as anyone. "Now, on top of that, he complicates it. He has this restless intellectual curiosity. We haven't had many Presidents who read as much as he does. He complicates it. There's an openness I don't think he gets credit for; he gets denigrated for it."
There may be a clue to the contradictions of the man in the ritual he follows at almost every rally and reception he attends: He thanks the band. From the humblest high school ensemble to the resplendent, red-jacketed Marines known as "The President's Own," few players escape his appreciative eye.
He asks about the make of their instruments, as he did a boy in a school gym in New Hampshire this winter. He marvels at their harmony and their heart. And he knows, instinctively, how often they can be overlooked, underestimated and misunderstood.
For Mr. Clinton is, by the social- and class-conventions of the world he grew up in, a band guy -- the in-betweener who did not score the touchdowns but played the horn at halftime, neither jock nor nerd, grind nor goof-off, cool kid nor outcast, but a chameleon, moving ebulliently and effortlessly among worlds.
That quicksilver quality helped him present himself in 1992 as a new kind of Democrat, borrowing ideas and images from everywhere, and it fuels him again as he co-opts Republican issues from crime to welfare in this campaign.
But it is also the quality that makes him a straddler defying easy classification, both within the narrow world of Washington politics and the wider realm of the electorate, with unpredictable effects on his place in each.
 
The Politician
Clear-Cut as a Person But Fuzzy on Policy
Polls show that a majority of the public does not believe Mr. Clinton is telling the full truth about the controversies that continue to embroil him -- from Whitewater to his Administration's gathering of F.B.I. files on Republicans from the Reagan and Bush Administrations -- and that about half of voters do not think he has the honesty and integrity they expect in a President.
But they think he cares about them all the same as 65 percent of Americans said in the most recent New York Times/CBS News poll.
Mr. Clinton never hesitates to exploit that sense. As he put it at the beginning of the train trip that brought him to Chicago tonight, "I want you to know, every day for four years I have gone to that Oval Office and thought about you."
Asked to sum up his boss in a corridor of the convention hall on Monday, his deputy chief of staff and longtime friend, Harold M. Ickes, sighed and shook his head at the implicit complexity of the task.
Then suddenly he whipped out a newspaper clipping, thwacked one underlined paragraph with his forefinger and smiled. In it, Brenda Godsey, a delegate from Kansas, was quoted as saying "it's the first time that I've been voting for President that I felt like I'm voting for a real person."
That Bill Clinton certainly is, and his faults have long been as painfully plain as his strengths. Indeed, when his wife, Hillary, introduced him at his 50th birthday gala at Radio City Music Hall 10 days ago as the best man she had ever known, he was moved to respond, "I'm sure I'm not the best man she has ever known, but I sure have loved her and my wonderful child."
Mr. Clinton ran for President when older, better-known horses in his party thought George Bush looked unbeatable, and he persevered when the experts thought the taint of tales of infidelity and his avoiding the draft had left him dead. In his first two years in office, he scored some victories: a deficit reduction plan, passage of the North American Free Trade Agreement, passage of long-sought gun control laws.
But he also stumbled badly, through botched appointments, the flock of old friends from Arkansas and new ones from Hollywood whom he allowed to have indecorous run of the White House, his clumsy and ultimately failed attempt to permit gays to serve openly in the military, the postponement of middle-class tax cuts and the welfare restructuring that he promised, and finally the total collapse of the health-insurance overhaul that he made the centerpiece of his domestic agenda.
When voters in the midterm elections of 1994 delivered a stunning resounding rebuke of him and the party whose form he has periodically tried to reshape instead of being shaped by it, Mr. Clinton retreated into a self-described sinking spell. He called old friends to wonder what had gone wrong, consulted with historians and even spent a weekend at Camp David with a panel of New Age self-improvement gurus.
From all those dark hours came a determination to reposition himself once more as the man in the middle, just as he had after his bruising defeat as Governor of Arkansas after one term, when voters concluded that he was too young, too liberal and too clever by half. With the help of his politically ambiguous strategist and pollster, Dick Morris, he rebounded with a Presidency so retooled as to seem a premature second term, and he now stands as the acknowledged front-runner to win an actual one.
Just what he stands for is less clear. He promises to lead the nation into "a new progressive era," of smaller, more efficient Government, with modest tax breaks for education and families with children. He preaches a mantra of "opportunity, responsibility, community," pre-empting Republican themes like values and violence in the media.
After twice vetoing Republican welfare bills as too harsh, he finally signed one which he still denounces as full of bad elements and which many members of his own party condemn, because he decided it was the best he could get, and he promises to fix it in the next four years.
For months, he has flooded the public stage with small proposals, from curfews for teen-agers to requiring uniforms in public schools, and bigger ones, like a sharp Government crackdown on teen-age smoking. This week, he has begun unleashing a flurry of perhaps three dozen similar executive actions that would help shape a second term.
Al From, the head of the moderate Democratic Leadership Council, the centrist organization Mr. Clinton helped to found, said "the common thread" of his Presidency has been a desire "to lead the country, and in doing so, his party, beyond a set of orthodoxies that have defined them both into a different and new era."
"He has had to live in a world where he has had opposition both from the opposition party and where he tried to do things that many in his own party really don't support," Mr. From added.
Yet sometimes Mr. Clinton's approach of "one from Column A and two from Column B" has seemed anything but deeply thought out.
In his State of the Union Message this year, Mr. Clinton summoned the nation to join him in an "age of possibility," but his advisers acknowledged that sketchy notion as a space holder until they figured out what it really meant. The President's broad agenda remains sketchy.
"My vision does not seek to promote Government but to perfect it, to make it a better servant of our people," Mr. Clinton writes in his just-released book, "Between Hope and History." "It doesn't seek to demean the free marketplace, but to strengthen it and to take account of what it cannot be expected to do. It does not promise a future of perfection, but it does provide a path of progress."
 
The Person
A Child of Duality Who Often Perplexes
Born to a widowed nurse who barely knew his father, a traveling salesman she married after a whirlwind wartime romance, Mr. Clinton grew up in the dual world he has inhabited ever since.
On the one hand, he was the adored eldest son, indulged and forgiven his frailties, given the biggest bedroom in his boyhood home and a recreation room that saw a constant stream of visiting friends. On the other, he was the premature man of the family, forced to cope with the alcoholic, abusive outbursts of the bourbon-drinking Buick dealer his mother married when he was 4.
"The violence and dysfunction in our home made me a loner, which is contrary to the way people view me, because I'm gregarious, happy, all of that," Mr. Clinton told Good Housekeeping magazine last year in an interview about his mother, Virginia Kelley, who died in 1994. "But I had to construct a whole life inside my own mind, my own space."
Part of the life Mr. Clinton created involved the soulful solitude of the tenor saxophone. Another part involved restless public activity and political networking, a index-card file of friends and contacts that was the stuff of legend by the time he became a Rhodes Scholar. And still another part involved a capacity, learned from his mother, to will away, deny and ignore bad news.
Late in her life, his mother acknowledged that she and he had never talked together about the years of abuse she suffered from her second husband, Roger Clinton, the Buick dealer whose name Bill took, though each by then had talked about it separately on national television as part of the 1992 campaign. The President's aides say he brings the same tendency to bear in the White House.
"Think of some of our recent Presidents, and how they would have been consumed by attacks against them like those he's had, and virtually incapacitated by it," said his press secretary, Michael D. McCurry. "He just doesn't pay any attention to it. He knows enough about himself not to. He will not read a damn thing about any of the subjects that would cause him pain."
In the same Good Housekeeping interview, Mr. Clinton suggested that the skills of coping and compartmentalization he learned as a child were a mixed blessing -- one that friends say he came to understand only much later, in counseling sessions when his younger half-brother, Roger, was convicted on cocaine charges in the 1980's after a sting operation he had authorized as Governor.
"When you grow up in a dysfunctional home," Mr. Clinton said, "inadvertently, you send mixed signals. You learn that other people, in the outside world, didn't live in the same context as you. I see this as President. I don't believe in psychobabble. You can overdo all that, but I think I have to be acutely aware that I grew up as a peacemaker, always trying to minimize the disruption."
"People underestimate your resolve because you go out of your way to accommodate them before you drop the hammer," Mr. Clinton added. "And it's a great mistake, really, because most people -- most good, whole people who have survived these kinds of upbringings -- deep down are pretty steely. They may go that extra mile to avoid conflict, but when it happens, they tend to survive pretty well. You try to avoid it, but you also learn to survive."
No less a firsthand authority on troubled childhoods than Speaker Newt Gingrich misjudged Mr. Clinton's collegial intimations in the budget battles between the White House and Congress last year. Mr. Gingrich was furious when what he thought was the President's willingness to work together turned into a barrage of attacks portraying the Republicans as using slash-and-burn tactics and the ones who shut down Government services and would destroy Medicare.
Indeed, Mr. Clinton is dangerous to his opponents (if not to himself), in part because of his amoebic ability to shift from one self and one stance to the next. To friend and foe alike, the President also has an exasperating propensity for the elisions of truth that Mark Twain called "stretchers."
Mr. McCurry himself scoffed publicly, if affectionately, at the President's recent suggestion that he has lost upward of 15 pounds in recent months, warning that there was "an inflation factor" to the claim.
Asked at the end of an interview with The New York Times in July about a dual-faced watch he was wearing, Mr. Clinton replied: "Let me tell you. This is a watch I wear when Hillary's gone," so as to keep track of what time it is wherever the First Lady may be on the road.
But subsequent questioning revealed that Mr. Clinton had only received the watch the week before, at the summit of major industrial nations in France and that his wearing it was hardly a longstanding practice.
Sometimes, the President simply tries to hard to be liked.
"The foremost thing about this man is that he loves people, he genuinely adores people, and wants that love back," Ms. Wright said. "In fact he goes crazy if he can't have it."
Mr. Clinton's aides know that is part of what drives the public crazy about their President, and part of what makes them understand him, in turn. After all, what mortal has not fudged his weight, or flattered himself for his sensitivity?
At the same time, Mr. Clinton is capable of hewing sternly to a goal when the stakes are high enough. The same man who used to appall aides by wolfing down cold, coagulated pasta from an Olive Garden restaurant on the road has, in fact, slimmed down for his last campaign, and willingly keeps a schedule that exhausts aides 20 years younger.
Having just turned 50 -- a ripe age in a family whose men have died young -- Mr. Clinton at last seems a bit more settled, in life and his job. For example, his spokesman, Mr. McCurry says, he no longer struggles for words when describing a foreign policy problem the way he once did. "He doesn't search now for what he's supposed to say, because he now knows what he needs to say," Mr. McCurry said.
Erskine Bowles, a former deputy chief of staff at the White House, said the President's mind never stops whirring.
"When it's just him and me on the golf course or whatever, he talks about early childhood education or a job training program, so people can have the skills they need in a high-wage marketplace," Mr. Bowles said. "He really talks about that, and you know, he's not putting on any show for old Erskine sitting there."
In The Times interview this summer, groping to synthesize the diverse strands of his agenda, from fighting international terrorism to restricting violence on television, Mr. Clinton summed himself up, too.
"I really try to think every day about how all this looks to someone living in a medium-sized town in Ohio or California or Arkansas or anyplace else," he said. "You know, what it's like for them, and how this can connect to their lives. If I have brought anything to the Presidency I think that is special, I think I do have a feel for how all these issues fit together and why."
Or at least, the President added, "I hope that's true, and I believe it's true."

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS;
Excerpts From Dodd's Remarks at Convention

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the speech last night at the Democratic National Convention by Senator Christopher J. Dodd of Connecticut nominating President Clinton:
Sixteen years ago, a Governor from Arkansas traveled north to Connecticut. He spoke on my behalf at a convention nominating me to serve in the United States Senate. Tonight, I am honored to return the favor. 
 Delegates and fellow Americans, I place in nomination for the Presidency of the United States a man I have called my President for nearly 4 years -- and my friend for more than 20 -- William Jefferson Clinton.
Four more years.
My friends, America remembers that cold and brilliant January day 44 months ago, when a young governor, raised in the small town of Hope, Ark., brought new hope to a nation hungry for renewal and change. Remember what it was like then. Americans were struggling. Men and women needed jobs. Student test scores were falling. Crime rates were rising. Parents looked in on their sleeping children -- and in the quiet of the night, worried that, no matter how hard they worked, those they most loved might inherit a lesser life in a lesser land.
President Clinton identified with the difficulties confronting America's families. Remember, this is a man who never had the joy of knowing his own father. He was raised by a wonderful mother, in a home that had more than its share of problems. So when this President speaks about tough times facing America's families, he knows what he's talking about.
That is why he offered a bold and brave strategy to put America back on track. He invited men and women of both political parties to work together to make America work again. Because he knew that for Americans to do their best, their leaders must do better and appeal to the best in each of us.
But there were those in Congress who decided to seek advantages instead of common ground. They said interest rates would soar, unemployment would increase, a deeper recession would hit, and so the President's plan for recovery had to be passed without a single one of their votes.
Tonight, let me say it plainly: "Mr. President, you did the right thing. You did the right thing for America."
Today, our economy is stronger and more prosperous than it has been in more than three decades. But as we all know, the effort is not finished. People today work long, hard hours to pay their bills, and they have a chance to move ahead because President Clinton took a stand, while his opponents turned away.
It is for you, the American people, that President Clinton has cut the deficit for four straight years. That hasn't happened in more than a century and a half in America.
It is for you, the people of this country, that the President has brought down interest rates, so more Americans can own a home, start a business, and send a child to college. It is for you, the people of this nation, that President Clinton has cut taxes, so 15 million American families can keep more in their pockets.
And now it is for you, the people, that President Clinton has created more than 10 million new jobs. Ten million. That is his most important contribution to our people, who all deserve an opportunity to succeed. And then, my friends, he did something that no other American President has ever dared to do: He took on the gun lobby, and he won.
He signed Jim and Sarah Brady's bill, and stopped more than 60,000 outlaws from buying handguns. He banned deadly assault weapons, because the guns that deal death should not be as easy to buy as the bread that sustains life.
Now our President is taking on tobacco.
On this he heard another chorus of caution: "Don't oppose this special interest, it's a fight you can't win." Some even tried to claim that cigarettes are no more harmful to a child's health than a glass of milk.
But every day in America, 3,000 children start smoking. One thousand of them will die earlier than they should. That will change now -- because this President had the courage to say no more advertising to the young, no more selling to the young, no more "Joe Camel."
Four days ago, he began a train journey across America -- an America that he has helped to change. It is not a perfect America, but it is a nation on the march again, approaching a new century with new confidence. For the next 70 days, our people will listen to a debate about the direction of our country. But this autumn of campaign does not have to be a season of conflict.
On this August evening, and in this hall, I ask each and every one of you to pledge with me that this campaign will be worthy of the people we seek to lead, and of the land we love. Let us do our part to restore civility to America's political discourse. Let us lead by example. Let us not dishonor our democracy. The American people are fed up with relentless assaults on people's reputations.
This has to stop -- and it must stop now. And I say this to our political opponents: Stop attacking the President's family. . . .
Now, at the 11th hour, Senator Dole proposes a flawed economic plan that will either explode the deficit, or lead to withering cuts in Medicare, education and the environment. President Clinton has put our economic house in order, and he offers a plan to lead us into a new era of growth and jobs, while honoring our abiding commitment to the elderly, the young and the earth we inhabit.
Mr. President, you're doing the right thing. You're doing the right thing for America.
Now friends, a few hours ago, this young President arrived here in Chicago. And in a few hours from now, he will accept the nomination of our party. And then he will depart and begin a new journey into the heart of America.
And for that journey, he has the courage, the vision and the commitment to bring all Americans together to the threshold of a new century.
The choice, my fellow citizens, is yours. We're here tonight, proud to offer you our choice. I proudly place in nomination before this convention and this country the name of America's President: William Jefferson Clinton.

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: THE OVERVIEW;
Democrats Send Clinton Into Battle for a 2d Term

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The Democratic Party tonight once again nominated Bill Clinton for President of the United States in a triumphant climax to his sometimes stormy four years in office, capping an evening of dire predictions about what might happen if the Republicans retake the White House.
Though Mr. Clinton's nomination was only a formality for a President who faced no opposition in the primaries, the night was a celebration of solidarity behind a President who just a year ago was down in the polls and blamed by many in his own party for the collapse of his health-care proposal and for delivering Congress to the Republicans in the midterm elections. 
 As the party rambled through the ritual roll-call of states, Mr. Clinton went over the top at 10:59 P.M. with the necessary 2,145 delegates after the battleground state of Ohio cast it votes. The hall rocked with cheers as Mr. Clinton's smiling face appeared on the huge screens in the hall, and, from his hotel suite, he threw his fist in the air.
But before the jubilation and the unanimous vote, the Democratic convention took a bluntly and unabashedly partisan turn as Vice President Al Gore led a line-up of speakers who sought to contrast Mr. Clinton with Bob Dole and to remind voters about the party's favorite adversary, Speaker Newt Gingrich.
"Make no mistake," Mr. Gore said in one of the most aggressively partisan speeches all week. "There is a profound difference in outlook between the President and the man who seeks his office. In his speech from San Diego, Senator Dole offered himself as a bridge to the past. Tonight, Bill Clinton and I offer ourselves as a bridge to the future."
In a well-received address that seems certain to raise his profile as he gears up for an expected run for President himself in the year 2000, Mr. Gore's remarks were salted with stern warnings about what might happen if Mr. Dole reaches the White House. He also chastised Mr. Gingrich and the Republicans for the Government's shutdown last year -- and for attacking the First Lady, Hillary Rodham Clinton.
Saying the Republicans underestimated Mr. Clinton, the Vice President said: "They did not know the true measure of this man. He never flinched or wavered. He never stooped to their level. And, of course, he never attacked his opponent's wife." With that, Mrs. Clinton rose to her feet and applauded heartily.
Mr. Gore brought many delegates to tears with a description of his sister's death from lung cancer but his speech and tonight's program in general sounded more like a traditional political convention after two days of deeply personal moments couched in studied nonpartisan statements. That approach had been intended to provide warm and safe family programming while the President chugged toward Chicago by train through West Virginia, Kentucky, Ohio, Michigan and Indiana.
From the last stop in his train trip in Michigan City, Ind., the 50-year-old Mr. Clinton arrived by helicopter, alighting on a baseball field at the Chicago campus of the University of Illinois, ending a passage through the middle of the country choreographed to build interest and news coverage for a convention that has so far borne neither surprises, controversy nor debate.
Mr. Clinton, who will deliver his acceptance speech on Thursday night, told a small but enthusiastic crowd that welcomed him: "The best is yet to come: the best days of America, the best days of the Clinton-Gore Administration, the best days of our efforts together, to lift up our country and move forward."
Despite the display of partisanship inside the United Center, speakers hewed to a well-orchestrated effort to shun what Democrats asserted were the inappropriately personal attacks at the Republican convention two weeks ago. Both Mr. Gore and Senator Christopher J. Dodd of Connecticut, in his speech nominating the President, went so far as to praise Mr. Dole even as they attacked his policies.
"I begin by thinking of the Republican candidate for President who two weeks ago spoke so movingly of his own past," said Mr. Dodd, the general chairman of the Democratic Party. "So let me say to Senator Robert Dole, on behalf of the thousands here in this United Center: Thank you from a generation of Americans living in freedom because of your sacrifices."
Calling for a restoration of civility in political discourse, Mr. Dodd said: "It is not Bob Dole's reputation that I question. It is his agenda for America. Sometimes a fine person has flawed ideas. This is such a time."
The President's 16-year-old daughter, Chelsea, leapt to her feet when Mr. Dodd implored Republicans to "stop attacking the President's family."
That said, Mr. Dodd plunged into listing Mr. Dole's differences with the President, on issues from education to Medicare to abortion to the economy. On abortion, he accused Mr. Dole of trying "to turn a woman's wrenching personal choice into a constitutional crime."
And the Senator tore into Mr. Dole's signature proposal for an across-the-board, 15-percent tax cut, saying: "Now, at the 11th hour, Senator Dole proposes a flawed economic plan that will either explode the deficit or lead to withering cuts in Medicare, education and the environment. President Clinton has put our economic house in order."
On this festive night, the last differences over welfare, no matter how strongly felt, were swept aside. For the first time since 1964, delegates at a Democratic convention were optimistic, even gleeful, about Election Day -- if not out of personal feelings for Mr. Clinton, then at least because with 10 weeks to go their nominee, a proven and adept campaigner, has a solid lead in the polls.
The circumstances are vastly different from the last time the Democrats renominated their President, Jimmy Carter, in New York in 1980. Mr. Carter clinched the nomination, but only after his rival, Senator Edward M. Kennedy of Massachusetts, ended his quest for the nomination after losing a fight over the rules. This year, Mr. Clinton faced no primary challenger, and Mr. Kennedy, who has long abandoned his designs on the White House, will join in the parade of tributes with an address before the President's acceptance speech.
As the headline speaker of the evening, Mr. Gore sought to underscore the differences between Mr. Dole and Mr. Clinton in an address that aides said was something of a preview of the President's acceptance speech.
Mr. Gore drew respectful applause when he saluted Mr. Dole for his "personal courage in fighting back from injuries sustained in battle." But moments later he attacked his Republican foe: "We remember that he voted against the creation of Medicare. Against the creation of Medicaid. Against the Clean Air Act. Against Head Start. Against the Peace Corps in the '60's and Americorps in the '90's. He even voted against the funds to send a man to the moon."
The hall was hushed during Mr. Gore's emotional description of the death from lung cancer of his sister, who was a smoker. "Until I draw my last breath," Mr. Gore said he would fight to protect children from the dangers of tobacco -- and lauded Mr. Clinton's call for new restrictions on tobacco.
On a lighter note, Mr. Gore used the prime-time opportunity to burnish his staid, even stiff, public image. "Tradition holds that this speech be delivered tomorrow night," Mr. Gore said. "But President Clinton asked me to speak tonight. And you can probably guess the reason. My reputation for excitement." He stood stone faced before breaking into a smile.
While neither Mr. Gore nor the other speakers explicitly noted that Mr. Dole is 73, an underlying theme was the depiction of the Republican nominee as of a past generation. As Mayor Dennis Archer of Detroit said in his speech seconding Mr. Clinton's nomination: "You know, we heard a lot of talk in San Diego about the past -- about turning back the clock. And nostalgia has its place. But their warm memories won't solve the challenges of a new century and a rapidly changing world. Nor will warmed-over "voodoo" economics that spell bigger deficits, recession and crippling new burdens for local governments -- and more pain for the most vulnerable, our seniors and our children."
In the warm-up to the President's arrival and the Vice President's speech, several prominent Democratic Governors and members of Congress took to the rostrum to make the case for the Clinton Administration's accomplishments and warn that a White House and Congress controlled by Republicans would lead to extremist policies. Many appeals tonight were aimed at, and delivered by, working women, an important Democratic constituency. In fact, Mr. Gore's address was preceded by a presentation from the five Democratic Senators who are women.
"Tonight we come before you not just as Senators," said Senator Barbara Boxer of California, "but as grandmothers and moms, as sisters and daughters, as wives and aunts, as women and Americans concerned about the future of our children and the future of our country. And if all Americans put families first and re-elect this President, we will give our children the opportunity they deserve to live up to their potential."
Senator Joseph I. Lieberman of Connecticut was almost alone in raising the subject of foreign policy, an issue that Democratic Party officials say is not on the top of voters' minds when they go to the polls. "The most awesome power of the American Presidency is the power to wage war," he said. "With it comes a profound responsibility to defend America and preserve peace so that war does not occur."
Earlier in the evening, a parade of Congressional candidates addressed the convention -- each with a short, personal talk about themselves and their candidacies.
For the Democrats, the television pictures were as important as the words, explaining the live shots of the train beamed into the darkened hall all week. But White House officials said they had no plans to try to top the theatrics on this night four years ago, when Mr. Clinton marched joyously from Macy's in New York City to a convention hall filled with cheering Democrats.
Before he reached Chicago, Mr. Clinton, for the third day in row, unveiled a new initiative, this time a package of environmental measures costing $1.9 billion over four years. Most of the money, $1.3 billion, would be requested from Congress to nearly double the pace of cleaning up Superfund sites.
 
IN HIS OWN WORDS
 
BILL CLINTON
Arriving in Chicago last night after a whistle-stop tour:
"It's been a wonderful, wonderful trip.
I took that trip for two reasons. As all of you know, when I accept the nomination of our party tomorrow night and start this campaign, it will be the first American campaign for the 21st century and the last campaign for Bill Clinton.
I wanted to be on that track because I wanted to look into the eyes, into the faces, and into the hearts of people who live in the heartland of America, the people I have worked with and fought for the last four years, and I like what I saw.
And I wanted them to see that our train was not only on the right track to Chicago. We're on the right track to the 21st century and that's what this convention and this campaign is all about."
