HEADLINE: Vulture Investors Flocking to Europe;
Real Estate Feeding Frenzy a la U.S.May Be in Store for the Continent

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

 BODY:
The breakfast room at the Grand Hotel Inter-Continental on Rue Scribe in Paris seems an unlikely roost for "vulture" investors.
But on a recent morning there, J. Squire Junger, the partner in charge of Arthur Andersen's real estate portfolio services group, found himself sandwiched at a table between a covey of bankers from Nomura Securities, and a flock headed by Mark Landau from Merrill Lynch & Company. 
 Later, as he entered the offices of Lazard Freres & Company, he ran into a brood led by Keith Breslauer from Lehman Brothers' European real estate group, and on the way out, his friends from Merrill Lynch again.
Having reaped hefty profits from their bailout of the American property business in the early 1990's, vulture investors with an appetite for troubled real estate are now swooping down on Europe, where banks and insurance companies are showing an increased willingness to unload their bad loans and property.
Right now, the carcass these vultures are circling is a portfolio of some $2 billion in bad mortgage loans on office buildings in and around Paris and obscure property-development projects scattered around France. The portfolio, one of the largest collections of distressed real estate ever to come up for grabs, is being auctioned off next month by Lazard Freres on behalf of its client, Credisuez, a subsidiary of Groupe Suez.
If the deal is successful, bankers expect it to kick off the biggest real estate feeding frenzy since 1989, when the United States Government formed the Resolution Trust Corporation to sell off billions of dollars worth of bad property investments made by savings and loans.
Already, Whitehall, the immensely successful real estate fund company managed by Goldman, Sachs & Company, has snapped up two smaller loan portfolios and may have acquired others in private transactions. And all of the other players who made out big buying busted real estate in the United States -- Lehman Brothers, Cargill, the J. E. Robert Companies and Morgan Stanley -- have also shown up.
"You're seeing the birth of a market right now," Mr. Junger said. "They're effectively exporting the bailout business, which has been until now primarily an American phenomenon."
And, at least for the time being, it is still an American phenomenon exported to Europe. European investors have been notably absent, a fact that is interpreted two ways.
The bulls say vulture investing is simply an alien concept to most Europeans, who will enter the fray once an infrastructure is developed to assess distressed property.
Au contraire, say the bears. The Europeans are simply savvier: They figure the downturn in their property market is more structural than economic in nature and -- unlike in the United States -- there is less certainty of a robust recovery.
"They may not be aware of the money that can be made -- or maybe they know something we don't," said Donald Wolf, a principal in J. E. Robert, a privately held real estate company in Alexandria, Va.
Nonetheless, he is confident that investors are on the brink of a gold mine. Last year, J. E. Robert hooked up with a French property management company to form Finestate & Roberts, which aims to help potential investors analyze deals in France and then acts as a manager if a purchase is made.
"We have a conviction that there is an incredible business opportunity over there," Mr. Wolf said.
The deals so far have been limited to France and mainly involve real estate in the Paris metropolitan area. Four years ago, Paris was the first commercial property market on the Continent to run into trouble as demand for office space dropped, rents sank and borrowers started having trouble paying their loans. French banks, unlike some of their counterparts in Spain and Italy, had less of a capital cushion to absorb the losses.
The French Government did move early on to bail out a couple of major institutions, most notably -- and controversially -- the giant Credit Lyonnais, which was all too willing a lender in the late 1980's. But the ability of the Government to ride in on a white horse has been constrained by its own need to reduce its spending and budget deficits in preparation for European monetary union in 1999.
The scope of the troubles in France is difficult to quantify; estimates range from $20 billion to $60 billion. Investors are licking their chops over the prospects of bulk sales by Credit Foncier de France, a real estate and municipal financing company that has a large portfolio of distressed property and loans, and by Consortium de Realisation, the unit set up to handle problem loans of Credit Lyonnais -- although no one is certain they will ever come to market.
"The bottom line is, nobody really knows how big the market is or whether any of these assets will come up for sale," said Mr. Breslauer, executive director at Lehman Brothers. Many banks, he said, "aren't making enough profits to take provisions that would allow them to make significant write-downs." In other words, they cannot afford to absorb the losses from selling their troubled loans.
The first bulk sale of distressed mortgages and property took place last fall, when Barclays Bank France auctioned off about 200 loans with a total book value of 900 million francs, or $180 million, to a group that included Lehman Brothers, Cargill and LaSalle Partners. Shortly thereafter, Credisuez tested the waters with the sale of a package of loans and real property carrying a book value of 745 million francs, or $150 million, which was purchased by a consortium led by Whitehall. Neither purchase price was disclosed.
Those deals were small potatoes by the standards of most vulture investors. But when a Whitehall-led investor group snapped up some 3.2 billion francs, or $630 million, of performing and nonperforming loans from France's largest insurer, Compagnie UAP, in June, their gastric juices started flowing.
"The sellers are finally acknowledging the decline in asset values," said George E. Kountouris, a managing director at Bankers Trust in London who advised the seller in all three of the transactions.
Bankers expect to see similar sales in Spain by early next year and then in Germany, Switzerland, Italy, and Belgium over the next couple of years. Some are even looking to Japan, although admittedly with a great degree of wishfulness.
At first glance, buying distressed real estate in Europe would seem like shooting fish in a barrel. Portfolios are selling at discounts to book value ranging from 50 to 75 percent, and banks and other financial institutions desperately need to clean up their balance sheets.
But the steep discounts reflect huge risks. First and foremost, bankers complain that information most Americans would consider essential for valuing a real estate portfolio either is not available or is available in a form that makes it all but impossible to use. One banker said he was shocked when he received four large boxes filled with a welter of papers about the first Credisuez deal, rather than a computer disk with information presented in a user-friendly format. He pushed them into a corner and used them as a makeshift table for his plants.
John Klopp, a principal at the Victor Capital Group, which advised several American banks on their asset disposals in the early 1990's, said the lack of complete information on properties, their occupancies, their development prospects and their cash flow could cost sellers almost a third of their loans' book value.
"If the French banks and institutions were smart, they would start investing in developing an information infrastructure for buyers," he said.
Also, the recovery of the real estate market in Europe is less certain than it was in the United States, where falling interest rates and improving economic growth combined to create a hothouse environment for real estate. Interest rates in Europe are higher, and economic growth is coming at a snail's pace. France's gross domestic product climbed by a scant nine-tenths of 1 percent in the 12 months that ended in March, while Germany's barely grew at all.
Europe's real estate market is far more troubled than was America's in the late 1980's. "The bulk of the damage has come from the financing of smaller developers and speculators who were building smaller properties," said Jon Zehner, head of real estate investment banking at J. P. Morgan. "So as a rule, what you're looking at in these portfolios are mainly nonperforming mortgage loans with absolutely no cash flow, unlike the U.S. experience."
Additionally, the costs of gaining control of the property tied to a faltering mortgage loan are much higher in Europe. Laws favor the borrower, and transfer taxes can be much higher than expected. And just how an investor realizes a profit is far less clear. Mortgage-backed securities or real estate investment trusts, favorite escape routes for investors who picked up R.T.C. properties, are alien concepts in Europe. The securities effectively turn loans and properties into more liquid bonds and stock that are attractive to pension funds and other institutional investors.
"Because there is no cash flow and because the whole concept of asset securitization is not nearly as well developed in Europe, and particularly in France, there is much greater uncertainty as to exit strategies," said Christophe Tanghe, a vice president in J. P. Morgan's real estate group.
Although next month's Credisuez deal is enormous, most bankers say the only thing that might scotch it is unrealistic price expectations. The bank's first deal attracted relatively few bidders, at least in part because Credisuez wanted what many thought was too rich a price.
Plus, many potential investors still have a bitter taste in their mouths from a deal floated by Argentaria, a large Spanish bank. It pulled the sale of a large property portfolio at the last minute, reportedly because it thought the bids were too low; bidders lost millions of dollars spent on due diligence.
But as big as the risks seem, there is still a steady stream of bidders flowing in and out of the Boulevard Haussman offices of Lazard Freres. No one has forgotten that among the bulk sales of distressed real estate in the United States during the early 1990's, only one deal has lost money for its investors.
"Experience says that no matter how silly it sounds or how opportunistic it appears, they'll probably make a lot of money," said Craig Hatkoff, a principal at Victor Capital. "This is a business where you make a lot of money just for being there."

CORRECTION-DATE: August 30, 1996, Friday

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
Couldn't Help Giving In to Wanderlust

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI 

 BODY:

ANATOMY OF RESTLESSNESS
Selected Writings 1969-1989
By Bruce Chatwin
205 pages. Viking. $22.95.
 
 Writers of books, Bruce Chatwin observed in one of these posthumously published essays, fall into two categories: "the ones who 'dig in' and the ones who move." Among the members of the first category, he counted "Flaubert and Tolstoy laboring in their libraries; Zola with a suit of armor alongside his desk; Poe in his cottage; Proust in the cork-lined room." Among the movers, he named "Melville, who was 'undone' by his gentlemanly establishment in Massachusetts, or Hemingway, Gogol or Dostoyevsky whose lives, whether from choice or necessity, were a headlong round of hotels and rented rooms -- and, in the case of the last, a Siberian prison." 
 Chatwin, of course, belongs firmly in the movers category. Until his death at the age of 48 in 1989 (he had been ailing, he said in an interview, from a rare bone marrow disease contracted during a trip to western China), Chatwin had been an inveterate traveler, a restless, modern-day nomad whose eloquent accounts of his journeys ("In Patagonia," "The Songlines") have already become classics of travel writing.
His latest book, "Anatomy of Restlessness" -- a collection of essays, stories, articles and reviews spanning a 20-year period -- amplifies his thoughts on traveling and the virtues of the nomadic life. Although the volume is more of a hodgepodge than his last collection of articles ("What Am I Doing Here"), it still affords the reader the pleasures of Chatwin's lapidary style, as well as some glittering snapshots from his own peregrinations.
As he recounts in the lead essay, "I Always Wanted to Go to Patagonia" (a piece that originally appeared in The New York Times Book Review in 1983), Chatwin grew up with wanderlust in his genes. His cousin Charley, who became British consul in Punta Arenas in Chile, was shipwrecked at the entrance to the Strait of Magellan in 1898; his Uncle Geoffrey was an Arabist and desert traveler who received a golden headdress from Emir Feisal, and his Uncle Humphrey came to a "sad end in Africa."
With his father away in the Navy, the young Bruce Chatwin spent his childhood drifting about England with his mother, staying with assorted relatives and friends. As a child, he became attached to a wooden camel his father had bought in a Cairo bazaar; as a student, he became "an addict of atlases." Years later he would quit his job as an art assessor at Sotheby's to hit the road himself: he had developed, he recalled, a horror of things, "to feel that things, however beautiful, can also be malign."
He had also begun to believe that human beings possess a migratory instinct that drives them "to walk long distances through the seasons" -- an instinct inherited from their herdsmen ancestors that "when warped in conditions of settlement" found "outlets in violence, greed, status-seeking or a mania for the new."
These ideas reverberate throughout this volume. In excerpts from a book he once planned to write about nomads, Chatwin muses at length about the economic and psychological roots of wanderlust, the emotional impulse "to abandon civilization and seek a simpler life, a life in harmony with 'nature,' unhampered with possessions, free from the grinding bonds of technology." He argues that "the origins of civilization are not all that respectable." He quotes Montaigne and Whitman on the benefits of travel. And he writes that "all the great teachers -- Buddha, Lao-tse, St. Francis -- had set the perpetual pilgrimage at the heart of their message and told their disciples, literally, to follow The Way."
Sounding a lot like a flower child with a smattering of scholarly training, Chatwin is decidedly not at his best in this sort of theoretical writing. He debates the same points over and over again, makes questionable assertions ("The golden-brown babies of the Kalahari Bushmen hunters never cry and are among the most contented babies in the world") and allows his prose to devolve into odd, gnomic pronouncements ("I do not believe in any of the cyclical theories of decline, fall and rebirth"). The four fictional stories included in this volume are also slight -- at once predictable and contrived.
It is when Chatwin turns his hand to describing places and people that his gift for the telling detail really shines. As in earlier books, he introduces us to a motley group of individuals encountered through his travels and through his reading.
There's Nadezhda Mandelstam, dismissing the thrillers he has brought her and sneering: "Romans policiers! Next time, bring me some real trash!" There's the writer Curzio Malaparte, who was exiled to the island of Lipari for making anti-Fascist remarks, and who later built a stupendous villa that reproduced, "on his own grandiose terms, the conditions of his exile on Lipari." And there's Axel Munthe, a celebrated doctor and antiquarian, who worshiped the Roman Emperor Tiberius and decided he had to own 12 houses on the island of Capri, just as Tiberius had.
Chatwin is able, in a few paragraphs, to demonstrate the formative impact that Edinburgh had on the imagination of Robert Louis Stevenson. And he's similarly able to convey the mythic allure of the African town of Timbuktu, an unlovely and "tired caravan city" that lured European visitors for years as a magical source of gold, a place where white egrets roost in the acacias, the desert wind whips up the green waters of the river and the sky is a "thin hard blue."
In this essay, as in so much of his earlier writings, Chatwin conjures both cities: the real and the mythical, the city that exists in the unforgiving quadrants of geography and the city that exists only in the mind.

HEADLINE: Let Dole Cut Taxes and Exporters Do the Rest

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The naysayers have been having a field day bashing Bob Dole's embrace of so-called supply-side economics. Clyde V. Prestowitz Jr., in "Dole's Supply-Side Delusion" (Op-Ed, Aug. 26), gives a cartoonlike description of Mr. Dole's proposed 15 percent tax cut. He hysterically contends that it will depress economic growth; lead to our financial dependence on foreign countries; force indefensible budget cuts that will destroy Federal agencies needed to enforce trade laws and, as a result, cause our exports to plummet to the rapidly growing Pacific Rim.
Such nonsense. 
 Economists like John B. Taylor, Martin Feldstein and George P. Shultz support Mr. Dole's proposals, recognizing that a 15 percent tax cut barely returns taxpayers to where they were before President Clinton's tax increase. They see the need for bold steps to yank our economy out of its sluggish growth.
It is wrong to allege that the Dole proposal will increase our dependence on foreign capital. By allowing Americans to keep more of what they earn, and by cutting inefficient Federal spending, we will substantially reduce the need to borrow from abroad. It is our very low savings rate that forces us to seek foreign financing. If we save more at home, we will not have to borrow abroad.
It is also wrong to claim that Mr. Dole's plan will decimate the agencies that enforce trade laws and will cause exports to plummet.
The problem in government is the overlap of responsibilities that too often destroys efficiency and accountability. For example, the General Accounting Office has warned that the Commerce Department, which has responsibility for enforcing laws prohibiting dumping by foreign companies and the granting of government subsidies to them, shares its missions with at least 71 departments, agencies and offices. Sixty percent of the department's resources go to activities unrelated to its mission. Trimming unnecessary activities from agencies enforcing our trade laws will make them more focused and accountable.
Over the past decade, the private sector has re-engineered itself to be leaner and far more competitive. It is time to carefully examine the public sector. It should be apparent to anyone who has served in government that we can have less redundancy and a better work product at a lower cost.
Mr. Dole's economic proposals will encourage growth, savings and government efficiency. Exporters will have more resources to compete in the global economy.
Remember: It is not Government agencies that make the United States the world's No. 1 exporter. It is the thousands of entrepreneurs -- small, medium and large -- who with 15 percent more of their earnings going to their bottom line can do even better.
 
CARLA A. HILLS
Washington, Aug. 27, 1996
 
The writer was United States trade representative in the Bush Administration.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Strong Data and Franc Rumors Leave Dollar Mostly Unchanged

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar ended little changed yesterday, after rising on unexpectedly strong American economic data that heightened expectations that the Federal Reserve would raise interest rates to prevent inflation.
A falling stock and bond market in the United States offset any help that the dollar received from the reports on sharply rising home sales and upward revisions of overall economic growth. 
 In late trading in New York yesterday, the dollar settled at 108.30 Japanese yen, down from 108.42 yen on Wednesday, and at 1.4764 German marks, off a bit from 1.4765 marks. The British pound declined to $1.5575 from $1.5591.
The dollar rose in the morning on the strength of reports that sales of new homes jumped 7.9 percent in July, reaching the highest level in five months, and that the gross domestic product rose at an annual rate of 4.8 percent in the second quarter, the highest in two years.
But the bounce was over by midday.
"The dollar failed in an attempt to rally off the stronger-than-expected U.S. economic data" that had led to fears of rising interest rates, said Margaret Kudarauskas, senior foreign exchange analyst at Technical Data. "This would normally benefit the dollar, but the steep selloff in U.S. asset markets -- most notably, the stock market -- has the dollar in check."
The dollar received some early support on speculation that the French central bank was intervening in the market to help the franc.
In addition, President Jacques Chirac reasserted France's ability to meet strict guidelines for entry into European monetary union.
As a result, the franc gained and the German mark weakened, indirectly bolstering the dollar.
"The French actions probably set the tone," Mike Malpede, senior foreign exchange analyst at the Refco Group Ltd., told the Reuters news agency. "It was an action to limit the mark's gains."

HEADLINE: Iran Helps Iraq Evade Oil Embargo, U.S. Asserts

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The United States has accused Iran of helping smuggle refined oil products out of Iraq in violation of the United Nations sanctions by forging shipping manifests and allowing barges and small boats to ply its coastal waters.
American officials leveled the accusations in New York on Wednesday at a meeting of the United Nations committee overseeing the sanctions imposed on Iraq after it invaded Kuwait in 1990. The committee asked the United States to put the charges in writing. 
 If Iran is implicated in violating the international embargo against Iraq, it could also face stiff punishment, including sanctions..
John R. Dinger, a State Department spokesman, said today that the United States had "compelling evidence" that Iranians were involved in the smuggling, including members of the Revolutionary Guards, the elite troops closely tied to Iran's Islamic leaders.
Mr. Dinger said that members of the guards provided bogus documents to ship captains, declaring that cargos of Iraqi oil products like gasoline were loaded in Iranian, not Iraqi ports. In exchange, the guards collected large fees.
The vessels, often tugboats pulling barges or other smaller ships, then traveled in Iran's territorial waters along the coast, allowing them to slip past the multinational fleet that patrols the Persian Gulf.
Iran's delegate to the United Nations, Kamal Kharazi, denied that Teheran had circumvented the international sanctions against Iraq, calling the accusations "part of Washington's anti-Iran propaganda," according to Iran's official Islamic Republic News Agency. He said that, on the contrary, Iran had tightened its coastal patrols to enforce the sanctions against Iraq.
Iran's relations with Iraq have been troubled, with new confrontations flaring up periodically since the end of the eight-year war between the two countries in the 1980's. Today, Iraq accused Iran of sending troops into northern Iraq, apparently to strike at Iranian Kurdish guerrillas based there.
But in its desperation to raise money Iraq has offered lucrative incentives, officials said, not only for the Revolutionary Guards, but also for ship captains and other smugglers willing to take the risk of loading oil in Iraqi ports. These officials, who spoke on condition of anonymity, said the Iranians were making a greater profit on each shipment than the Iraqis.
The charges against Iran are unlikely to affect the recent agreement between Iraq and the United Nations to sell $2 billion worth of oil twice a year to buy food and medicine, officials said.
They acknowledged that they did not have a precise estimate of the amount of oil that may have been smuggled out of Iraq, but they insisted it was considerable. From 1992 to 1994, Iraq tried to ship oil in large tankers, but the international naval force largely choked off that route, leaving open only coastal waters.
"It's hard to interdict any of this stuff," one official said.
The United States has accused Iran of complicity in breaking the embargo on Iraq before. In February, it brought similar charges to the United Nations, but the sanctions committee did not act. Mr. Dinger said that the smuggling aboard smaller boats began in 1995 and increased significantly this year.
The new accusations appeared to be an effort to step up pressure and embarrass Iran into stopping the shipments. "We decided to raise it with the committee now," Mr. Dinger said, "because it shows no signs of abating."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Securities Fall in Price

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities tumbled yesterday on the unexpected strength of the latest economic statistics, which heightened investor fears that inflation would accelerate, forcing the Fed to tighten monetary policy soon.
The 30-year bond dropped 22/32 , to a price of 9615/32 ; the yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, rose to 7.03 percent, from 6.98 percent on Wednesday. What perhaps bothered the bulls most yesterday were signs that the expected slowdown for the third quarter may not materialize. 
 The Commerce Department revised the second quarter's gross domestic product gain sharply upward, to 4.8 percent from 4.2 percent. Moreover, surprisingly strong housing sales in July, up 7.9 percent, to an annualized rate of 783,000 units, almost certainly shocked market participants who were looking for signs of moderation in the wake of the strong G.D.P. revision.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said that "the economic statistics released today reaffirmed the economy's bouyancy." He added, "Initial claims for unemployment insurance are still relatively low despite recent modest increases in both the weekly numbers and the four-week averages, second-quarter G.D.P. growth proved to be even stronger than initially reported, and new-home sales noticeably exceeded even the highest expectations."
Mr. Fine said that "the downward revision to business inventories increases the chances that third-quarter G.D.P. growth, while more moderate than the April-to-June period, will also exceed the Federal Reserve's trend rate of maximum, noninflationary growth, which implies a continued bias toward tightening on the part of both the Federal Open Market Committee and the bond market."
The open market committee, the Fed panel that sets interest rate policy, last met on Aug. 20 and chose not to raise rates. But its next meeting is set for Sept. 24, and it is possible, though highly unlikely, that the committee could opt to shift rates even before then if other data are strong enough to alarm the central bank.
 According to Maureen Mooney, a market analyst with Gov Px, a bond pricing service, activity was average after deducting the volume of trading in 14-day cash management bills that were auctioned yesterday.
The Government was selling $30 billion of the short-term bills to bridge a brief shortfall in cash flow expected in September. Dealers said the average rate at auction was 5.24 percent and the bid-to-cover ratio, a low 2 to 1, which reflected the weakness in the market generated by fears that the Fed would raise rates soon.
Selling did quicken in the afternoon, but some traders suspect that investors who held long positions took advantage of some index-fund buying by portfolio managers to get out on an uptick after the recent price declines in the market. And because of the thinness in volume, price movements were exaggerated.
Today, the Treasury market will have an abbreviated session and close early. Thus, with a shortened session and long weekend ahead of next week's employment report, few traders yesterday were willing to be buyers.
Mr. Fine said that perhaps the bigger surprise was the jump in July new-home sales, which nearly matches the high for the year that was seen in February.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of 14-day cash management bills:

HEADLINE: Doris Rosenblum, 71, Borough Historian and Community Advocate

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Doris Rosenblum, an indefatigable combatant in 40 years of struggle over housing, education and development on Manhattan's Upper West Side, died of cancer yesterday at Beth Israel Hospital in Manhattan. She was 71.
Ms. Rosenblum served for the last six years as the borough historian of Manhattan, and her positions of civic authority included her job as district manager of Community Board 7 on the Upper West Side from 1983 to 1990. But the impact of the woman who came to embody neighborhood advocacy in New York City extended far beyond that conferred by official titles. 
 Ruth Messinger, the Manhattan Borough President, said in an interview yesterday that "she was and always will be remembered as the Mayor of the Upper West Side."
Ms. Rosenblum was so deeply involved in the affairs of her neighborhood -- from the decentralization of authority over the public schools in the 1960's to the development battles of the 1980's -- that many people regarded her as an irreplaceable force.
"She is as much a part of the West Side as the No. 2 train," Ronnie Eldridge, the City Council member, said when Ms. Rosenblum retired from Community Board 7 in 1990.
Born June 30, 1925, on Manhattan's Lower East Side, and raised in the South Bronx, she first engaged the political establishment of the Upper West Side when she moved there in 1959 with her husband, Paul, a printer for The New York Times and a scholar in African history.
Within months of her arrival, she began investigating conditions in her children's public school, joined and was elected president of the neighborhood branch of the United Parents Association and led a campaign for smaller schools under greater neighborhood control. That culminated in 1971 when she was part of the group that founded the first alternative high school on the Upper West Side, the West Side High School.
Ms. Rosenblum's most far-reaching impact may have been in her fight to maintain housing for low-income and middle-income people amid the massive urban renewal projects, including Mitchell-Lama apartments and public housing that were built on the Upper West Side during the 1960's and 1970's.
"She always wanted to make sure that urban renewal was not urban removal," her husband said. Ms. Rosenblum's base of influence included the Strycker's Bay Neighborhood Council, where she served as a board member and president, and later Community Board 7, where she became a member in 1971.
After 12 years as a board member, she was appointed district manager of the board in 1983, making her a central mediator, fact finder and advocate at a time when the Upper West Side was awash in huge development plans. Until 1990, when she resigned, she coordinated neighborhood opposition to several projects, including huge developments planned for the site of the New York Coliseum and Donald Trump's Riverside South property.
Ms. Rosenblum was appointed as the official historian of Manhattan -- a voluntary post -- by Ms. Messinger in 1990.
In addition to her husband, Ms. Rosenblum is survived by her mother, Fannie Koppelman of Manhattan; a daughter, Beth, of Ashland, Mass., and a brother, Frank, of Queens. Her son, Peter, was killed in an auto accident in 1981.

HEADLINE: Stocks Post Biggest Loss In Six Weeks

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Stocks tumbled yesterday in their worst loss in six weeks after a surprisingly strong report on new-home sales renewed concern that in a buoyant economy borrowing costs could rise, cutting industry's profits.
The Dow Jones industrial average tumbled 64.73 points, to 5,647.65, tracking bonds lower. Procter & Gamble, General Electric and Walt Disney led the decline. 
 Single-family home sales "is one of the areas people are counting on to see a slowdown, and you're just not seeing it," said Alan Wapnick, a money manager at the Lexington Management Corporation, which oversees $3 billion in assets. The economy "isn't going to be quite as weak as the Fed might want it to be," he said, and that could prompt it to raise interest rates.
Beverage and drug companies paced the slide in the Standard & Poor's 500 Stock Index for a second day; their earnings do not benefit from pickups in the economy as much as industries like chemicals, steel and home building. The S.& P. 500 fell 7.41, to 657.40, and the Nasdaq composite index dropped 8.85, to 1,145.03. The Commerce Department said sales of new homes unexpectedly surged in July to the highest level since February. That came on top of a report that revised second-quarter economic growth to a 4.8 percent annual rate, its strongest showing in two years.
Short-term interest rates, a gauge of the market's expectations about Fed policy changes, jumped. The yield on the two-year Treasury bill rose almost 7 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, to 6.23 percent. Higher rates hurt stocks by raising borrowing costs and making bonds relatively more attractive as an investment. The yield on the 30-year bond rose 5 basis points, to 7.03 percent.
Particularly hard-hit yesterday were soft drink and pharmaceutical shares that had moved up lately, trading at high price/earnings ratios.
Coca-Cola slid 1/2, to 50 3/4. Its shares trade at 36.4 times estimated earnings for 1996, compared with 18.9 times profits for the average S.& P. 500 stock.
"I have to take a second look when Coke is selling at a higher multiple than Microsoft," said Edward Laux, director of trading at the Chicago Corporation in New York.
Microsoft, which grew at twice the rate of Coke in the last 12 months, trades at 30.8 times earnings for the fiscal year that began in June. It gave up 1 1/2 yesterday, closing at 124 1/8.
Pfizer, which trades at 24.4 times estimated earnings, retreated 7/8, to 72 5/8. Merck & Company sank 7/8, to 66 7/8.
Procter & Gamble dropped 1 7/8, to 89 7/8, General Electric slid 1 7/8, to 84 1/2 and Disney fell 1 3/8, to 57.
Investors made Pepsico Inc. the second-most actively traded stock after Goldman, Sachs & Co. removed the drink, snack and restaurateur from its "recommended list." Two days earlier Merrill Lynch & Company cut the company's earnings forecast for 1996 and 1997. It fell 1 1/8, to 28 7/8, bringing its loss this week to 7.9 percent.
Financial shares declined, with J. P. Morgan & Company falling 3/4, to 88 3/8, and the Chase Manhattan Corporation slipping 1 1/4, to 75 7/8.
Novell Inc., the faltering computer-network builder, dropped 3/8, to 10 5/8, following the resignation of R. J. Frankenberg, the company's chief executive, chairman and president. John Young, a board member and former chief executive at the Hewlett-Packard Company, was named chairman, and Joseph Marengi was named president. Novell said it would begin searching for a chief executive.
Housecall Medical Resources Inc. sank by more than half after the home health-care provider said it would report a loss in the fiscal fourth quarter. Its shares slid 7 3/8, to 7 1/8.
Among companies that rose, Sierra Semiconductor jumped 2 1/8, to 11 3/8, after the company said it would sell its modem chip line, a sign of a shakeout in that industry.
Structural Dynamics gained 2 3/4, to 25 3/8, after the Ford Motor Company said that it would use the company's software around the globe to test its cars on the computer. The order could be worth $200 million over five years.
In perhaps another sign of a bouyant economy, the Starbucks Corporation, the specialty coffee retailer that sells $3 Frappachinos, said same-store sales rose 9 percent in August.

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Giuliani, Playing to the Bleacher Seats, Boycotts Tennis Open

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani is an ardent fan of the New York Yankees who often uses his bully pulpit to give the kind of workaday analysis on the team's games that might be heard over beers at a dusky corner bar. But lately, the Mayor has voiced little love for another local sporting institution, the United States Open in Queens, portraying its sponsors as haughty and meddlesome.
Yesterday, he even announced that he was boycotting the Open, which has long been one of the city's premier summer attractions. 
 The Mayor insisted that he liked tennis (though perhaps not as much as his beloved baseball), and he said he was basing his criticism on what he contended were flaws in the contract that his predecessor, David N. Dinkins, negotiated with the United States Tennis Association, which runs the Open.
Yet his comments also seemed directed at working-class neighborhoods that have long complained about the traffic and crowds that clog Queens during the tournament, people who may feel that the city cares more about an event that draws an affluent audience than their peace and quiet.
These are people who tended to back Mr. Giuliani in his narrow victory in 1993 and whose support he will need for his re-election campaign next year. They are the stickball vote -- like the Mayor himself, they are probably more comfortable wielding a bat (or a broomstick) than a tennis racket.
On Wednesday night, Mr. Giuliani brought up the United States Open even before he was asked about it during a meeting of the First New York Conservative Democratic Club in Forest Hills, Queens.
As he has repeatedly done this week, he assailed a provision in the contract that requires the city to limit noise at the tournament by insuring that the Federal Aviation Administration shifts airplane flights away from the airspace above the National Tennis Center, which is in Flushing Meadows-Corona Park. The provision also requires the city to pay a fine if too many planes broach the airspace.
The Mayor said those rules, negotiated by Mr. Dinkins, usurped the city's authority, endangered air safety and caused more noise over residential neighborhoods. "Airplanes should not be rerouted because tennis players and tennis spectators don't want to be bothered," he said.
The line garnered hearty applause -- even though the Federal Aviation Administration has said the flight patterns during the tournament are not risky.
Arlene Salac, a spokeswoman for the agency, said it had used the alternate routes for 30 years without major problems. She said that during the Open last year, the F.A.A. shifted about 2,800 out of 11,000 flights in and out of La Guardia Airport.
Mr. Giuliani no doubt remembers that his erstwhile rival, Mr. Dinkins, who was not particularly popular in those same neighborhoods, was a fervent tennis aficionado. When he was Mayor, Mr. Dinkins took vacation in August to attend the Open.
Mr. Giuliani, on the other hand, appears eager to cultivate an image of a man who would wear all-white clothing only if it had pinstripes. "I might not be able to afford the new prices," he said at his news conference yesterday when asked about why he was not attending the tournament.
Tournament officials said ticket prices had not increased this year, but they would rise next year, particularly for luxury boxes.
Reached yesterday at the Democratic National Convention in Chicago, Mr. Dinkins sounded exasperated by Mr. Giuliani's attacks. He defended the contract with the tennis association, saying that the Open would provide the city with more than $100 million in economic activity this year.
Mr. Dinkins also questioned why Mr. Giuliani had been largely silent about the tournament in Mr. Giuliani's first two years in office. Mr. Giuliani did make an issue of the contract during the mayoral campaign in 1993.
"It has to be illogical for him to have this tremendous bias against tennis," Mr. Dinkins said. "We don't find him reacting against baseball, basketball, football or hockey in the same way that he reacts against tennis. He seems not to understand and appreciate its value."
Mr. Giuliani surely realizes that his criticism has little chance of prompting the Open to leave the city because the tennis association signed a 25-year lease in 1993. For their part, tennis association officials did not seem interested in doing battle.
"We have a big tournament to run, the biggest tennis tournament in the world, so we really are not going to respond to the Mayor's comments," said Page D. Crosland, a spokeswoman for the association.
Mr. Giuliani said yesterday that he would not attend the tournament until the tennis association agreed to remove the provision on airspace from its contract with the city.
He and his aides said he had been to the tournament many times, but not since he took office.
And then, as he has so often during his tenure, he ended his news conference with a brief analysis of the Yankees' recent performance -- or in this case, failures.
"I was very upset last night about the Yankees losing the second game in Seattle," he said. "I am starting to get worried, but they have such good starting pitchers, and I think that they will start to kick in."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
I.M.F. May Sell Gold to Cut Debt Load of Poor

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

 BODY:
The International Monetary Fund has indicated for the first time that it may be willing to sell some of its $40 billion supply of gold and use the proceeds as an outright gift to reduce the foreign debts of the poorest developing countries, allowing them to improve economic growth and attract more foreign investment.
This is one of several concessions that the I.M.F. and the World Bank have made to critics, who regard their March debt reduction plan to make concessional loans as still being too onerous for the poor. The new plan was sent in confidential, outline form to member governments this week; it will be discussed by the executive boards of both institutions next month. 
 Officials say it is still unclear whether this debt-reduction initiative -- first called for by the Group of Seven leading industrial nations at a summit meeting in Halifax, Nova Scotia, in 1995 -- will be approved, as had been hoped, at this year's ministerial meetings of the World Bank and the I.M.F. in Washington at the end of September. It is expected to cost between $5.6 billion and $7.7 billion over six years.
Although the United States favors gold sales to finance debt relief, other Western industrial countries, which control both institutions, remain divided over the wisdom of allowing the I.M.F. to sell any of its gold when it has some $30 billion in outstanding loan commitments to Russia and Mexico. Germany leads the opposition, and is supported by Italy, Switzerland, Norway and Sweden.
In addition, officials say the so-called Paris Club of Western creditor nations is proving slow to heed a call in June by the Group of Seven at a summit meeting in Lyons, France, to raise the level of forgiveness offered qualifying poor countries to 90 percent of their direct debts from 67 percent.
World Bank officials who returned this week from talks with the Paris Club secretariat say the creditors have done little so far to prepare the groundwork for a more generous treatment of debtors, despite the personal commitment of President Jacques Chirac of France.
"This whole debt initiative is in danger of ending up as an empty charade if there is not a rapid agreement on I.M.F. gold sales and more generous Paris Club terms," said Justin Forsyth, Washington director of Oxfam International, a privately financed aid agency lobbying for debt relief for the poorest nations.
The World Bank and the I.M.F. have proposed that about 20 of the poorest countries, whose debts exceed a range of 200 to 250 percent of their annual export earnings, should qualify for relief under the plan, provided they take steps to strengthen their economies. Among these countries are Zaire, Mozambique, Bolivia, Nicaragua and Uganda.
As an illustration of the hardship imposed by high debt burdens, Oxfam International has calculated that Uganda spends $17 per person on debt repayment annually but only $3 on health care. Such heavy obligations crush economic growth and deter foreign companies from investing.
In the latest version of the debt-relief plan, I.M.F. documents say that the agency is ready to use the proceeds of a gold sale to make "loans or grants" to eligible developing countries so they can pay off some of the money they owe it, signifying that the institution is no longer insisting that poor nations repay the help it gives them.
Previously the I.M.F., which all along has taken a more cautious approach to debt relief than the World Bank, wanted to extend low-interest 10-year loans to eligible countries. But critics complained that this would merely add to the developing world's debt burden instead of reducing it.
The World Bank has already put aside $500 million from last year's profit as its first annual contribution to a trust fund it plans to create to relieve eligible countries of debt. Both the World Bank and the I.M.F. insist, in effect, on paying themselves back the money the poor nations owe them, instead of writing it off, because as a matter of principle, they refuse to forgive debts.
To qualify for relief, developing countries would still generally be required to undertake three years of economic liberalization intended to make them more competitive, after which they would be eligible for forgiveness of up to 67 percent of their debts to the Paris Club countries, followed by a further three years of economic liberalization.
After that, the I.M.F. and the World Bank would offer relief and the Paris Club would raise its forgiveness level to 90 percent.
But in answer to critics who complained that six years was too long for these poor nations to wait for help, the revised plan provides for countries like Uganda, which are already making good progress in improving their economies, to move straight into the second stage of the process -- thus becoming eligible for full debt relief by the year 2000.
The new proposal is also more generous toward countries on the borderline of eligibility for debt relief.
And the World Bank and the I.M.F. spell out clearly that developing nations seeking to make their economies more efficient, under their supervision, will also be expected to meet social targets in areas like health care, the reduction of poverty, and education.

HEADLINE: Bulldozers In Camden Make Room for Affordable Homes

BYLINE: By JENNIFER PRESTON 

DATELINE: CAMDEN, N.J., Aug. 29

 BODY:
Workers began demolishing an entire block of abandoned, dilapidated brick town houses today as part of the Whitman administration's first major effort to help build affordable housing in this impoverished city on the Delaware River.
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman climbed into a front loader to help knock down the first of 35 city-owned buildings on Washington Street that have been empty for more than a decade. Mrs. Whitman said the demolition would not only pave the way for construction of new row houses, but also help rid the neighborhood of drug dealers who had been operating out of the abandoned buildings. 
 "By sending these machines into action, we are clearing the way for a rebirth of this historic city," Mrs. Whitman said. "Where there are crack houses, there will be family homes. Where there are dangerous streets, there will be safe communities."
Governor Whitman, who has been accused of paying too little attention to the state's cities, said she had done more to spur economic development in urban areas than previous governors. She also dismissed criticism by affordable-housing advocates that she has not provided additional state money to create more such housing statewide. The Governor earlier this year rejected a legislative proposal for a $290 million bond act to help finance new and rehabilitated housing. Mrs. Whitman said the state had enough resources to do the job.
State and local officials estimate that there are more than 3,000 vacant houses in Camden, where the unemployment rate exceeds 20 percent and more than half of the city's 87,000 residents receive some form of government assistance. State, city and business leaders have been struggling over different plans to help revitalize the city.
While Mayor Arnold W. Webster has had his differences with the Whitman administration, he praised the project and the administration's plans to help finance the construction of 30 new row houses on the site. "I have to take a positive approach to this," Mr. Webster said. "It is going to be a shot in the arm for us."
Jane M. Kenny, the State Commissioner of Community Affairs, said the town houses, built in 1880, were in such disrepair that it was cheaper to demolish than to renovate them. She said they would be replaced with new three-bedroom row houses that are to be completed next September and then sold to low- and moderate-income families for between $50,000 and $70,000. The state intends to demolish an additional 20 buildings in the coming year in Camden, and replace them with new construction. The total cost of the project is about $6 million. The state has provided financing to help create approximately 90 new housing units in Camden in the last two years, state officials said.
The Rev. Paul J. Breslin, pastor of a nearby Roman Catholic church, St. Anthony of Padua, and a founder of Camden Churches Organized for the People, has been urging the Whitman administration to do something about vacant buildings here. He praised the demolition project, but he said he hoped the state would help renovate vacant houses on blocks where families are now living. "That is what we are looking for and they seem supportive of that," Father Breslin said.
Esther Lopez, 26, who stood on a corner with her five small children watching the demolition begin, said she was glad that the buildings were being taken down. "A lot of drug dealers have been going in and out of there," she said. "They find a lot of dead bodies in there."
But Ms. Lopez added that her calls to city officials to do something about the four vacant houses on her nearby block have gone unanswered. "It is really bad," she said. "There are drug dealers in there and then they sit on the stoop all night. I can't let the kids out to play because they find needles and things."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. Christine Todd Whitman helped tear down one of dozens of empty houses in Camden, N.J., yesterday as part of New Jersey's first big effort to build affordable housing. "By sending these machines into action, we are clearing the way for a rebirth of this historic city," she said. (Laura Pedrick for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Robert Reinhold, Ex-Reporter For The Times, Is Dead at 54

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME 

 BODY:
Robert Reinhold, an editorial writer at The Los Angeles Times and before that a longtime reporter for The New York Times, died on Wednesday at his home in Los Angeles. He was 54.
The cause was complications of a brain tumor, said Dr. Timothy Cloughsey, a neuro-oncologist at the University of California at Los Angeles Medical Center. 
 Mr. Reinhold left The New York Times in January 1994 after a 30-year career that included stints as a science writer and bureau assignments in Boston, Washington, Houston and Los Angeles. He covered the world of ideas from Boston and urban affairs and demographics in Washington; he was bureau chief in Houston and Los Angeles.
Along the way he developed a reputation as a fast, meticulous and insightful reporter with a cool, stylish presence, who became a role model for a generation of national correspondents.
Robert Reinhold was born on Dec. 18, 1941, in Brooklyn. He grew up in the Flatbush section near Brooklyn College where his father, Meyer Reinhold, taught classics.
After graduating from Midwood High School in Brooklyn, he won a scholarship to John Hopkins University, where he studied biology, chemistry and physics and became the editor in chief of the campus newspaper. While working on a master's degree in journalism at Columbia University, he was hired as a part-time copy boy at The New York Times in 1964.
He became a science reporter three years later and was one of the paper's most prolific writers from 1967 until 1994.
Mr. Reinhold's first out-of-town assignment set the tone for his career. Sent to the South Pole to cover scientific developments there, he soon tired of the V.I.P. treatment at the United States Navy operations center at McMurdo Station.
Equipped with tent, C-rations, ice ax, gas stove, fuel, two sleeping bags and a foam-rubber mattress, he ventured to a remote area he described as "one of Antarctica's three eerie, other-worldly valleys whose glaciers had melted away millenia ago, leaving jagged rocks and sand in their path." There he joined two puzzled American geologists from Wyoming studying mountain rock formations who never could figure out why a Times reporter wanted to join them in their frigid desolation.
In addition to his science articles, he filed an article on spending Christmas Day eating powdered mashed potatoes in the Antarctic and stopped off at Waikiki Beach on his way back home to New York.
Mr. Reinhold arrived in Texas near the peak of the state's economic boom of the 1970's and 80's and was ahead of virtually all his peers in foreseeing the bust when oil prices collapsed in 1985.
Typical of his reporting was a 4,000-word piece in The Times in September 1986 that was the first to report on the impending financial downfall of former Gov. John B. Connally of Texas, detailing everything down to the $899 queen-size rattan sleeper-sofa and $86 lamps for which Mr. Connally was overdue on payments to the Texana National Bank of Belton.
"Connally is the story of Texas, a state that bet too heavily on itself, believing it could not lose," he wrote.
Similarly, Mr. Reinhold went to California just as the sun-kissed California myth was giving way to a harsher reality of urban unrest and natural disaster. His articles there went from the San Francisco earthquake in 1989 to issues of immigration, water policy and the economic complexities of the state's conversion from the military-based economy of the cold war to one based on peacetime spending.
With characteristic precision, a year after the riots that followed the state acquittal of the police officers who beat Rodney G. King, he surveyed records and buildings for 1,118 structures damaged in the riots and found that 513 had been repaired, with the lowest rate of repairs in the largely black and Hispanic South-Central area.
And while Mr. Reinhold was best known for tough, analytical news reporting, a ferocious work schedule and an uncanny knack for getting to disasters sooner than anyone else, he was also known for his style. Indeed, with his Armani suits and Gucci shoes, his measured subdued style and air of perpetual calm, Mr. Reinhold was a stark contrast to most of his rumpled, harried peers running around with their coffee-stained ties askew.
He often betrayed a softer side of his personality in articles for The Times's Travel section.
Writing of Hilo, Hawaii, where he said, it rains 280 days a year, roughly 130 inches, he blended his usual precision with a wry eye, reporting, "Hilo seems stuck in time, a throwback to Hawaii's plantation days, when the islands were merely a quiet territory of the United States and when the economy was based more on growing sugar cane and pineapple than on catering to Japanese tourists in search of such typical Hawaiian handicrafts as Gucci luggage and Rolex watches."
And reminiscing in 1993 about hitchhiking through Europe as a young man 30 years earlier, he concluded: "Now I stay in comfortable hotels and eat in good restaurants when I travel. But I often long for those days when riding through Sicily under fleecy clouds in the open bed of a cheese truck was a sublime experience and the small pleasures were enough."
In addition to his father, he is survived by a sister, Helen Barrett, of Nashville, and his companion, Michael R. Mueller, of Los Angeles.

HEADLINE: Democrats Bring a Fleeting Sense of Peace to a Neighborhood

BYLINE: By DON TERRY 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Now that James Jackson has tasted life for a couple of weeks without drive-by shootings and brazen drug dealers on every corner, he never wants the Democratic National Convention to leave town.
Mr. Jackson, a lanky 17-year-old, lives in the Henry Horner Homes, part of the other America just two blocks from where the Democrats are holding their convention on Chicago's West Side. 
 "It's been real peaceful around here," Mr. Jackson said today, the last day of the four-day convention. "All the drug dealers got scared off by all the police. But they'll be back, and all the killing will start up again. Probably by Saturday."
Horner is a place where people do not have much beyond their pride and poverty, where the Boys and Girls Club has to close down sometimes because of gunfire. It is the kind of place that the party of Kennedy and Johnson is supposed to care about. And indeed, the city's Democratic administration has been pouring millions into the neighborhood for new roads, lights and other improvements, and has ambitious plans to redevelop Horner as a model of mixed-income and public housing. Private developers have also been rushing in with their money and dreams.
In the weeks leading up to the convention, the city's interest in the area accelerated. Old buildings were replaced with new playgrounds. Grass was planted, fences painted and hopes raised. Parts of the development went from dust bowl to green valley.
"We haven't had grass around here in 15 years," said Kenneth Butler, director of the Boys and Girls Club and a former Horner resident.
Although Mr. Butler, like many people here, is firmly committed to re-electing President Clinton, he says many are skeptical about whether the Democratic Party really cares about them.
"The joke around here," Mr. Butler said, "is that after the D.N.C. leaves, the city will take back the playground and roll up the grass."
Walking past the new playground, Kevin Johnson, 28 and unemployed, said his trust in Mr. Clinton had been badly shaken after the President signed the welfare overhaul bill. But he will vote for Mr. Clinton anyway because he said the Republicans were more hostile to poor black people like himself and his neighbors.
"It's a catch-22," Mr. Johnson said, shrugging his shoulders. "I'm voting for the lesser of two evils. I guess anyway it goes, we're going to lose."
A few blocks away, sitting in the sunshine with a group of friends, Jacqueline Williams, 44, said she has had voted Democratic her entire adult life but would sit this election out because she had heard too many promises but seen little improvement.
"I don't see any reason to vote," Ms. Williams said, adding that she was looking for work. "I don't see any reason to leave my house. We fought hard to get the vote, but it doesn't matter anymore. Politicians don't care about us."
Poor people on the city's West Side are used to losing their dreams. After Martin Luther King Jr. was assassinated in April 1968, much of the neighborhood was destroyed in the flames of rage and grief. Stores and apartment buildings were turned into vacant lots that remained vacant for more than two decades.
It was an isolated neighborhood, cut off from the rest of the city, except on the nights the Chicago Bulls or the Black Hawks played their games in the old Chicago Stadium, the Madhouse on Madison.
Now, new town houses, and restaurants are going up and abandoned buildings are being converted into expensive lofts. The stadium has been replaced by the shiny new United Center, the site of the Democratic convention.
"This was skid row for years and now it has really been turned around," said George Lemperis, owner of the Palace Grill, a longtime restaurant a few blocks from the United Center and Horner. The Palace seats 19 customers on stools and this week is featuring a Bill Clinton All You Can Eat Special for $9.99 -- steak, bacon, ham, eggs, sweet rolls, coffee, hash browns, onions, grits, pancakes and French toast.
The convention accelerated some cosmetic improvements to the area, but the neighborhood has been changing for years. In 1993, Bridget and Jim Davis, both 32, moved into a nearby loft. When they moved in, the area was quiet. Now it is hard to park.
Mrs. Davis said she would vote for Mr. Clinton. "He is the lesser of two evils," she said, sounding like her Horner neighbors.
How long the people of Horner will remain in the area is uncertain, many of them say. They say they are worried that they will be priced out just when the neighborhood is making a comeback and the Democrats are two streets away promising a better America for everyone "but us," said Rubin McClellan, 30.
"Before Michael Jordan came along and built that stadium over there, the city never thought about us," Mr. McClellan said, pointing to the United Center. "Now, they want to chase the black man out of here."
Residents have been told they can stay after the mixed-income housing is built, if they want to, which is exactly what Mamie Bone, president of the development's tenants council intends to do.
Mrs. Bone has lived in Horner for nearly 40 years and said she is thankful for all the improvements brought about by the convention.
"The place is clearer than it has been for years," she said. "The streets are swept every day. The police are all around. But I don't think it has made a big difference in the real conditions. People still have plumbing problems. We still need more security."
Misty Lenon, 31, the secretary at the Boys and Girls Club, has lived in the area all her life. The windows in her office have bullet holes. She, too, is happy the convention has come. She has not heard gunfire in weeks.
"I don't think all these improvements will continue after the Democrats leave," Ms. Lenon said. "It took for the convention to come for them to do anything. That hardens you. Just because we will live in the quote unquote ghetto, doesn't mean we shouldn't be treated like American citizens every single day of the year."

GRAPHIC: Photos: The Democratic convention has brought some peace to a high-crime neighborhood but residents wonder how long it will last. Misty Lenon, left, said she had not heard gunfire in weeks. Jacqueline Williams, center, said "politicians don't care about us." Mamie Bone, right, is happy about improvements made in preparation for the convention but said more was needed. HEADLINE: Economy's Pace Gained Speed In 2d Quarter

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The Commerce Department reported today that the economy sprinted to an even bigger gain in the spring quarter than it had earlier estimated. The report, along with other data released today, suggested that an expected slowing of activity was eluding policy makers, rekindling financial markets' fears of higher interest rates.
Growth in total output of goods and services for the April-June period was calculated at at an annual rate of 4.8, six-tenths of a percentage point above the estimate published at the beginning of the month and the strongest since the spring of 1994. Moreover, the upward revision showed less production going into inventories and more of it to meet demand. 
 In a second report, the Government said sales of new single-family homes surged 7.8 percent in July, the most robust showing since February. This advance came despite mortgage rates that remain above 8 percent and, like the output figures, also surprised analysts, most of whom had expected a small decline in home sales.
"The economy still has a good deal of momentum," said Brian M. Jones, an economist at Salomon Brothers.
The unexpected strength rattled the bond market. Bond prices tumbled on revived fears that the Federal Reserve would not be able to avoid a decision to tighten monetary policy to prevent a flare-up of inflation. The next scheduled meeting of the Fed's main policy body is Sept. 24; minutes of its July meeting published last Friday showed it had decided to lean in the direction of raising short-term rates.
The slump in prices drove up the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond -- a benchmark for mortgages and other consumer and corporate interest rates -- to 7.03 percent, from 6.98 percent on Wednesday. Bond prices and their yields move in opposite directions.
Stocks followed bond prices lowers on fears that higher rates would raise borrowing costs and cut into profits. The Dow Jones industrial average fell 64.73 points, or 1.13 percent, to 5,647.65, while the Nasdaq composite index fell 8.85 points, or nearly eight-tenths of 1 percent, to 1,145.03.
A third report, covering last week, showed today that first-time claims for unemployment insurance edged up 4,000, to a total of 331,000. This continued a recent pattern of job-market strength but closely matched expectations. Some analysts are predicting that the Labor Department's monthly gleaning of labor-market data, to be published Sept. 6, will show that the nation's unemployment fell in August to 5.2 percent, which would be the lowest in more than six years.
The Clinton Administration, facing a Presidential challenge from Bob Dole, who has centered his campaign on calls for faster economic growth, was quick to hail the surprising, though not unusually large, upward revision in gross domestic product.
Joseph E. Stiglitz, the President's chief economic adviser, declared the G.D.P. report "strengthens our view that the United States economy is achieving the kind of strong economic growth that can raise America's living standards." He added that the downward revision of inventories combined with upward revisions for final sales and business investment in plant and equipment "provide additional signs that the prospects for further strong economic growth in the future are excellent."
By this he was pointing out that when shelves and warehouses are thinly stocked -- and much of the second-quarter increase was concentrated in motor vehicles -- demand is quickly transmitted to new production.
The biggest single factor in the revision was international trade, which was less of a drag because import growth was lower than initially assumed.
But the slowing of imports, as Merrill Lynch analysts noted today, is not a sign of economic strength. Nor is inventory buildup, however moderate. And the second-quarter growth rate also benefited, by about a percentage point, from the way the Government chose to account for last winter's partial shutdown of the Government, for which its workers were paid.
For these and other reasons analysts seemed to be sticking today with forecasts for marked slowing in the third quarter, with Merrill Lynch holding to a 2 to 2 1/2 percent rate of increase. This is in line with assessments, including the Federal Reserve's, that the economy can grow that much without accelerating inflation.
Still, after a string of figures pointing to a cooling off, perhaps partly because the Olympic Games kept many shoppers at home, some of the most recent figures cast doubt on this hope. One was last week's report of very strong orders for long-lived factory goods in July. Another was today's report from the Departments of Commerce and Housing and Urban Development that builders sold single-family homes at an annual rate of 783,000 last month, up from 726,000 in June.
"The housing market is stubbornly strong," said Alan D. Levenson, an economist at UBS Securities in New York. He nevertheless predicted that the economy would throttle down to a 2 percent pace in the current quarter and that the Fed would not need to move rates higher.
The rise in home sales included sizable gains in all four regions of the country other than the Northeast, where activity remained at the best level of the year while showing no further rise.
At month's end, builders had 365,000 unsold houses, 2,000 more than in June but only a 5.8-month supply at the latest selling rate instead of 6.1 months.
Sales have now surpassed 700,000 for seven consecutive months, the first time this has happened in 10 years, said Ed Yardeni, economist at Deutsche Morgan Grenfell/C. J. Lawrence. Sales for April, May and June, however, were revised downward by a combined 70,000, the report also showed.
The median sales price of new houses sold last month was $143,000, up from $140,000 in June and up from $131,000 in July 1995.
The G.D.P. report disclosed that inflation, as gauged by the price index for gross domestic purchases, ran at a 2.1 percent pace in the spring quarter, one-tenth of a point higher than previously estimated.
Corporate profits, estimated by the department for the first time, slackened during the quarter. Profits from current production advanced 1.3 percent following a 5.4 percent first-quarter increase, while current-production cash flow rose 1.1 percent, compared with a 2.5 percent advance in the winter quarter.

HEADLINE: Sorry, We're All Out Of Great Merchants;
Changes in Retailing Are Shrinking the Pool Of Trend Setters

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
When Sally Frame Kasaks, the chief executive of beleaguered Ann Taylor Stores, resigned last week, what was most surprising was not so much that she was pushed out after more than a year of slow sales. Rather, it was just how few names came up as her possible replacement.
Instead of the dozens of merchants -- those executives charged with choosing the store's goods -- who would have quickly emerged as strong candidates for the job only a few years ago, experts in retailing say that today the most frequent answer to the question, "Who's next?" is "Who knows?" 
 "I have not come up with a single person who could replace Sally," Walter Loeb, a retail consultant, said. "It has to be a person who has the excitement and the leadership and the fire in the belly, and a lot of people fail to meet the test of a great merchant today."
Ms. Kasaks, who just two years ago was considered one of the best merchants in women's mainstream apparel, will be succeeded as chief executive by J. Patrick Spainhour, her former chief operating officer. His background is financial -- not creative -- and his appointment still leaves Ms. Kasaks's chief merchant's spot unfilled.
The reasons for the decline of great merchants, analysts say, are many -- ranging from the blossoming role of computer spreadsheets in decision making to an increase in off-shore apparel purchasing to the decline of the family store. And perhaps most important, they say, is the consolidation of the department store business, which historically provided the training grounds for the great merchants, a role that has all but evaporated.
The consequence is that there are precious few executives, say many in the industry, who can carry the torch of the great founders of American merchandising -- people like Adam Gimbel, Stanley Marcus and Mary Ann Magnin, whose names were over the front door. Nor are there any big stars on the horizon like those who emerged in recent decades from department store training schools and now head their own retail concerns -- people like Allen I. Questrom, chairman of Federated Department Stores; Leslie H. Wexner, who now runs the Limited; Millard S. Drexler, chief executive of Gap Inc., and Rose Marie Bravo, president of Saks Fifth Avenue.
"In my mind, this is a huge issue in our industry that none of us are addressing," said Kirk Palmer, who runs his own retail-executive search firm. "There is not a pipeline to develop talent anymore, and when companies go looking for a chief merchant, there is no one there to replace them."
As a result, the excitement in retailing has shifted away from the traditional stores. Mr. Drexler of the Gap breathed new life into the chain by offering colorful basic fashions to all ages. Gordon Segal brought buying smarts to the housewares business at Crate and Barrel, a unit of Euromarket Designs. Phil Knight, the chairman of Nike, has moved from selling running shoes to creating new lines of athletic wear to opening his huge Nike Town emporiums. And clothing designers like Calvin Klein, Giorgio Armani, and Donna Karan have gone into the business of opening their own stores.
Among retailers, special wrath is held for Wall Street, where the desire for little more than a rich bottom line and expansive growth plans has made creativity take a back seat to ledgers. Wall Street executives who have been content to wear the same three colors for the last century, do not want to hear complicated explanations of why ostrich-skin suits failed to fly out of the stores.
"You have to satisfy your owners, your numbers crunchers," Mr. Loeb, the consultant, said, adding that financial professionals and merchants rarely share the same temperament or objectives. "And merchants are by their nature optimistic and emotional."
For instance, retailers now tend to treat deals with suppliers as pure transactions -- bargaining over, say, who will take the hit on unanticipated losses or how to split the advertising budget -- rather than as opportunities to develop a standout line of clothing.
All too many merchants, critics say, are no longer creators focused on ferreting out the next Armani but nerdy bean counters who have replaced hype with Hewlett-Packard calculators. Fiscal conservatism in retail, they argue, has all but eliminated the kind of merchant who not only knew in his heart that fur could sell in August but who could also make it happen.
Consider Stanley Marcus, the model of the old school merchant who is chairman emeritus and son of the founder of Neiman Marcus, now a unit of Harcourt General. Early in his career, Mr. Marcus recalled in a recent interview, he was admiring a magnolia tree on a street in Dallas. "I thought: 'It would be great to create a series of clothes that looked like that tree,' " he said. "Clothes that gave you the green of the leaf and the warm brown of the underside of that leaf and the vanilla colored blossom."'
Inspired, Mr. Marcus enlisted designers to made silk dresses and jackets, then managed to sell them with great fanfare at his Dallas department store.
Jump to 1991, during an exchange between Lisa Holland-Nelson, then an executive at the clothier Evan Picone, and a room full of retail merchants who had come to see her spring line.
"They came in, sat down, and never looked up," said Ms. Holland-Nelson, now a principal at Apparel Resource Group, a consulting firm. Instead, she said, the buyers were feverishly punching away on their laptop computers. "I realized that my business was in trouble in that moment," Ms. Holland-Nelson said, "because merchandising had moved from the gut to the numbers. Units and dollars had taken over."
Indeed, clear cultural and economic shifts have whittled away at some of the glamour of merchandising.
Many of the earliest American merchant stars were immigrants from Europe, often Jews who only had limited opportunities in most types of business. They started with pushcarts or a single family store, which, through sheer discipline and touches of kookiness, sometimes became an empire.
In 1888, Mary Ann Magnin turned her home embroidery business into an I. Magnin store on Market Street in San Francisco, where she trained her sons. In his book, "Merchant Princes" (Kondansha America, 1979), Leon Harris describes how Mrs. Magnin forced her son Grover to stand with his hands behind his back to learn to identify different types of lace by touch alone.
P. G. Winnett, who ran Bullocks Wilshire in Los Angeles, came up with the bright idea in 1928 of employing highly specialized merchants. He hired a merchant just to select men's robes, and when that succeeded, hired another to focus just on silk robes.
"He figured that a buyer who had nothing else to do but dream about robes," Mr. Marcus said, "would develop a better business then one who bought socks and shirts and something else."
Even during the Depression, the merchants who created the great stores that later dominated the postwar era were encouraged not to think about the bottom line. Extra money was squirreled away so that harebrained schemes could be tried without worrying about the risk of failure. Innovative offerings drew traffic, the thinking went, and traffic eventually drove sales.
Such freewheeling ways withered away in the late 1980's, as the wave of department store mergers left the survivors with huge debt obligations, not just shelves, to fill. The consolidation also led to much greater centralization of buying.
All this, industry insiders say, helps explain why so many department stores seem so dull and interchangeable. "A lot of department stores have similar merchandise today," Peter Rizzo, the executive vice president and general merchandise manager for Barneys New York, said, "because the senior executives do not take aesthetic control of the product the way they take financial control."
As the local department stores with individual character, like Abraham & Straus in Brooklyn and Marshall Field's in Chicago, died or were absorbed into national operations, they no longer were in a position to provide the long, specialized training that encouraged future executives to learn every facet of the business.
"Executive development got a bad name," said Henry Jackson, a retail investment banker at the Peter J. Solomon Company in New York. "It was a 50's kind of concept. People didn't want to go from picking up pins off the floor to moving into the executive suite."
A.& S. raised a whole group of important current retail executives. "You had a great sense of stability at A.& S.," said Michael Gould, chief executive of Bloomingdale's, who like Mr. Drexler of the Gap and Mr. Questrom of Federated is one of the A.& S. alumni. "People didn't go to other jobs back then. You got great responsibility at an early age."
The spread of overseas buying, while adding enormous economic benefits to retailers, has also contributed to the erosion of the intimate relationship between merchants and their vendors. Just a few decades ago, it was common for suppliers to drop in on buyers once a week to show their goods, and a buyer would often test a few samples by putting them on the rack.
"Small acorns grew into big oak trees," said Bud Konheim, owner of the design firm of Nicole Miller. "You would know what was selling, and you could get it right away because it wasn't from Indonesia."
These types of pressures leave little room for the inevitable goofs that all merchants who take chances sometimes make -- and which led to Ms. Kasaks's demise. She gambled one year on youthful fashions for her working-woman customer and lost. When her stores failed to recover after a year, Wall Street grew impatient for results. Next time, experts say, creativity is likely to be replaced by predictability.
"If you can't screw up," Mr. Konheim said, "then you may as well buy Mao suits and just put those on the floor. He sold billions of them."
