SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 5; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1643 words

HEADLINE: THE DEMOCRATS: THE OVERVIEW;
CLINTON, DECLARING 'HOPE IS BACK,' DEFENDS HIS FIRST TERM AND LISTS PLANS FOR SECOND

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Declaring that "hope is back in America," President Clinton accepted his party's nomination for a second term tonight by vowing to protect Government programs for children, the elderly and the environment in a "strong, united American community."
In a speech that blended outsized optimism with small-scale proposals, Mr. Clinton summed up his efforts of the last four years and sketched out his plans for the next, claiming credit for a new kind of politics mixing ideas without regard to their partisan origin. 
 "On issues that once tore us apart, we have changed the old politics of Washington," Mr. Clinton said in a veiled dig at the sharply partisan tone of the Republican Convention in San Diego, his voice husky from four days of campaigning by rail. "For too long, leaders in Washington have asked who's to blame. But we ask, 'What are we going to do?' " [Transcript of speech, page A20.]
The President answered himself by repeating his vigorous defense of existing Government programs like Medicare and Medicaid -- a stand that has scored highly in public opinion polls in recent months -- and by reciting a list of modest proposals for new ones, from tax credits for college education and families with children to tax breaks for homeownership and job creation. Over and over again, he pledged to "build a bridge to the 21st century."
But the speech, lasting one hour and seven minutes, was not so much a sustained exploration of either the man himself or any overarching vision as it was something of a State of the Union laundry list of all the multi-facted things he has done, tried to do or vows yet to do.
He took on critics of both the welfare system -- and of the bill he just signed overhauling it -- challenging every American "to try to hire somebody off welfare and try hard," and declaring:
"There is no more 'Who's to blame on welfare.' Now the only question is what to do, and we all have a responsibility -- especially those who have criticized what was passed and who have asked for a change and who have the ability -- to give poor people a chance to grow and support their families."
Mr. Clinton set an ebullient tone for the campaign he hopes will make him the first Democratic President elected to a second full term since Franklin D. Roosevelt 60 years ago -- and only the third in this century. But for all the flags and cheering in the hall, his speech tonight made it plainer than ever that he presides over a vastly different party, its old coalitions splintered, its majority status shaky and its ambitions limited by the President's own repeated pronouncements that the era of Big Government is over.
And what he had planned as a moment of meticulously staged triumph was marred by a cinematic sideshow: the sudden resignation of Dick Morris, the political strategist responsible for many of the themes in the President's speech, and for helping to reposition Mr. Clinton over the last 18 months as a man of the middle road.
Mr. Morris stepped down today after a tabloid newspaper, Star, reported that he had conducted a yearlong affair with a Washington prostitute and had discussed confidential White House business with her in private moments.
The news echoed Mr. Clinton's own unhappy encounter with Star in 1992, when it published Gennifer Flowers's accusation of a long extramarital relationship with Mr. Clinton when he was the Governor of Arkansas. The situation today sent the traveling White House staff, holed up with the President in the Sheraton Hotel here, into a frantic tailspin of damage control.
In contrast to his first acceptance speech four years ago in Madison Square Garden, in Manhattan, when his challenge was to introduce and humanize himself to a public that knew relatively little about him -- and to Bob Dole's self-consciously personal speech in San Diego -- Mr. Clinton faced the nation tonight as an all too known and human commodity, striving to show how the disparate pieces of his Presidency would fit together in a second term.
The President did so in a speech studded with the sort of modest, moderate proposals for executive and legislative action he has made his stock in trade in recent months, including two new ones: a new capital-gains tax break for homeowners and tax incentives for businesses that create jobs to employ welfare recipients.
Mr. Clinton mades nods to the politics of personality, recalling his younger brother's recovery from cocaine addiction in his speech and beginning the brief film, produced by his friend Linda Bloodworth Thomason, that introduced him in the hall with an account of the deaths of friends and family he has suffered since taking office.
It featured testimony from his wife, Hillary, that he is "a person who combines the best of his heart and his head," and his own assertion that his parents would have punished him if he made personal attacks of the sort he implied the Republicans rely on.
"I will not attack them personally or permit others in this party to do it if I can prevent it," Mr. Clinton said in his speech. "This must be a campaign of ideas, not of insults. The American people deserve it."
The President vowed to "build a bridge to help parents raise their children, to help young people and adults get the education and training they need, to make citizens feel safer on our streets, to help Americans succeed at home and work, to break the cycle of poverty and dependence, to protect our environment for generations to come, and to maintain our world leadership in the face of new threats and new opportunities."
Mr. Clinton summed up his goals in the terms of children and families that have become his unceasing refrain.
"If we do these things," he said, "every 8-year-old will be able to read, every 12-year-old will be able to log on to the Internet, every 18-year-old will be able to go to college, and Americans will have the knowledge they need to cross that bridge to the 21st Century."
Mr. Clinton drew his sharpest contrast with Mr. Dole at the very end, saying the real choice was "whether we will build a bridge to the future, or a bridge to the past, about whether we believe our best days are still out there or our best days are behind us, about whether we want a country of people working together or one where you're on your own."
And, as he has repeatedly, Mr. Clinton vowed to protect the most popular, mainstream Government programs from any who would diminish them.
"I could never allow cuts that devastate education for our children, that pollute our environment, that end the guarantee of health care for those served under Medicaid, that end our duty or violate our duty to our parents through Medicare," he said. "I just couldn't do that. As long as I'm President I'll never let it happen."
He continued: "Of course, there is a better answer to this dilemma. We could have the right kind of balanced budget with a new Congress -- a Democratic Congress."
Senator Edward M. Kennedy, the scarred, silver-maned war horse of the party's liberal past who briefly flirted with making a bid for the Presidency at the last Democratic convention in Chicago, in 1968, and who scored victories in Congress this summer on the minimum wage and health insurance legislation, started the evening on a full-throated, partisan note. Senator Kennedy mocked Mr. Dole's warning against intolerance to the Republicans in San Diego two weeks ago.
"We are proud of who we are, proud of where we stand and proud of who leads us," Mr. Kennedy told the delegates. "No one in this convention hall has to point to the exit signs to tell the prejudiced, the extremists and the intolerant that they are not welcome here."
And with mirror-image mimicry of one of the most pointed speeches of the Republican Convention, Mr. Kennedy drew raucous cheers and whoops of laughter by turning Senator Kay Bailey Hutchinson's hyphenated attacks back against her, denouncing the Republicans' platform as a Trojan Horse of hidden hazards.
Interrupting himself at one point to accommodate the applause, Senator Kennedy declared, "It is the radical wish-list of the education-cutting, environmental-trashing, Medicare-slashing, choice-denying, tolerance-repudiating, gay-bashing, Social-Security-threatening, assault-rifle-coddling, Government-closing, tax-loophole-granting -- there's more, there's more to come -- minimum-wage-opposing Republican majority than dominated the delegations in San Diego."
For weeks, Mr. Clinton's aides have billed his acceptance speech tonight as his first specific effort to sketch out the details of a second term, and the aides and advisers most charged with drafting it -- from Mr. Morris on down -- are squarely in the moderate mold.
Mr. Clinton made two new specific policy promises: a modest proposed tax break for homeowners and tax cuts for businesses providing jobs in depressed areas. The first amounted to a nod to longstanding Republican proposals for cutting capital gains taxes and, the second, a way to assuage the concerns of liberal Democrats that the Republican-sponsored welfare bill Mr. Clinton signed last week would cut off benefits for poor people without providing adequate job opportunities.
Mr. Dole has already endorsed a nearly identical capital gains proposal, and his running mate, Jack Kemp, has long supported the concept of tax incentives to spur economic development in urban "empowerment zones."
White House aides said Mr. Clinton spent virtually the entire day going over drafts of the speech at the dining room table of his 31st-floor hotel suite, reworking triple-spaced computer printouts with a black felt-tipped pen. The aides said President Clinton was so intent that his daughter, Chelsea, joked that he was sitting in exactly the same spot when she returned from a day out on the town at 5 P.M. as he had been at 10 A.M.

HEADLINE: New York, Cradle Of Labor History

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
IT may be hard to imagine, but not far from Manhattan in-spots like the Union Square Cafe and CBGB's, not far from the Bowery and Orchard Street, many of the nation's epic labor battles took place.
In New York City's streets, figures who were titans of their times -- Samuel Gompers, David Dubinsky, Emma Goldman -- led thousands of workers in struggles to end the 60-hour workweek and the treacherous conditions that made the 1911 fire at the Triangle Shirtwaist factory possible. Although New York does not pulse with union struggles as it once did, a tour of its labor sites teaches much about the working conditions New Yorkers once faced and about the people who braved arrests, beatings and wintry weeks picketing to build a better tomorrow. 
 Sixty, 80 and 100 years ago, workers' battles often dominated the news. On the Lower East Side, strikers were set upon by thugs. They were thrown into jail on West 10th Street. They thronged into Union Square for rallies and filled the Cooper Union Great Hall for meetings. In such showdowns, Irish, Italian, German and Jewish immigrants fought to secure the basics that many Americans now take for granted: the 40-hour workweek, a safe workplace and wages high enough to support a family. Many immigrants to New York felt tossed into a sea of brutal capitalism, and they banded together to struggle for the better life they had sought when they left Europe.
Immigrants led some landmark strikes in New York, like the Uprising of the 20,000 in 1909, a three-month walkout by apparel workers that was the first mass strike by women in American history. In a far cry from today's climate, labor's cause was embraced by high society, so much so that Mrs. August Belmont, wife of the renowned millionaire, joined Miss Anne Morgan, J. P.'s daughter, to sponsor a Carnegie Hall benefit to support the 20,000 strikers.
Gompers, a Lower East Side resident who became the American Federation of Labor's first president, called New York City "the cradle of the American labor movement." With all due respect to Detroit and Pittsburgh, there is more labor history per square inch between Chambers Street and Union Square than anywhere else in the nation. In taverns off the Bowery, Gompers helped establish the cigar workers' union, while at the Garden Cafeteria on East Broadway, Socialists, Communists and anarchists debated, over their borscht, whose ideology would create a workers' utopia.
New York may be notorious for bulldozing over its history, but somehow the wrecking ball has spared many sites that served as crucibles for labor.
A walking tour of all of the New York sites would be too ambitious. But a localized labor history tour of Greenwich Village and the Lower East Side is feasible because these neighborhoods are filled with so many squares, meeting halls and tenements that shaped the history of the American worker. This tour does, however, omit some pivotal Manhattan sites, like the Park Row building where Susan B. Anthony founded the Working Woman's Association in 1868 and the Harlem buildings where A. Philip Randolph lived and worked while building the first black-led union, the Brotherhood of Sleeping Car Porters.
Probably the best place to start a tour is the site of the TRIANGLE SHIRTWAIST FACTORY, east of Washington Square at the northwest corner of Washington Place and Greene Street. Here 146 garment workers, almost all of them Jewish or Italian immigrant women, died on March 25, 1911, when a fire swept through the top three floors of the 10-story building. Most were trapped because many exits were locked and there was only one fire escape. At the time, neither fire ladders nor hoses reached above the seventh floor. Rather than be burned alive, many women jumped to their deaths, shocking onlookers who rushed to watch.
New York University now uses the stately building for biology and chemistry studies. The only hint of the place's gruesome history comes from two plaques, one from the International Ladies' Garment Workers Union reading: "Out of their martyrdom came new concepts of social responsibility and labor legislation."
Frances Perkins, a member of the state committee that investigated the fire who became President Franklin D. Roosevelt's Secretary of Labor, called the incident "the torch that lit up the whole industrial scene." As a result, the State Legislature passed 36 laws tightening regulations on job safety.
From here, it is a short walk to WASHINGTON SQUARE, where there is a statue of Giuseppe Garibaldi, who united Italy and once worked as a carpenter while living in exile in Staten Island. In 1834, stonemasons clashed with the police at this site while protesting New York University's use of prison labor to construct its early buildings.
Head next to the JEFFERSON MARKET COURTHOUSE, now a public library, at the Avenue of the Americas and West 10th Street. This remarkable 1877 structure, modeled after Ludwig II's castle in Bavaria, has pinnacles, gables and a delicate clock tower. Here the police dragged hundreds of female strikers arrested during the Uprising of the 20,000. The workers went on strike to protest low wages, the requirement that they pay for their needles and thread, and favoritism toward compliant workers. Mrs. Belmont once sat up all night in this courthouse and put up her palatial Madison Avenue mansion as collateral to get several strikers out of jail pending trial.
One judge condemned the women for striking against God, "whose firm law is that man shall earn his bread from the sweat of his brow." George Bernard Shaw, commenting upon the judge's ruling, replied: "Delightful. Medieval America always in the intimate personal confidence of the Almighty."
Nearby are two picturesque cul-de-sacs, PATCHIN PLACE (off West 10th Street, between Greenwich Avenue and the Avenue of the Americas) and MILLIGAN PLACE (on the Avenue of the Americas between West 10th and 11th Streets). Here, rooming houses were built around 1850 for the Basque waiters at the Brevoort Hotel on Fifth Avenue. Patchin Place's tenants included E. E. Cummings, Theodore Dreiser and John Reed, the Harvard dropout turned radical who chronicled the Russian Revolution in the book "Ten Days That Shook the World."
A School for Organizing
Head over to 7 East 15th Street, a building that once housed the nation's first major workers' school, the RAND SCHOOL OF SOCIAL SCIENCE. Founded in 1906, it taught thousands of workers over 50 years about history, economics and union organizing. Its teachers included John Dewey, Bertrand Russell, Charles Beard and Stephen Vincent Benet.
Next is UNION SQUARE, which contrary to popular belief is not named after labor unions. In 1811, it was already known as Union Place, because two major streets, Bloomingdale (later Broadway) and the Bowery (later Fourth Avenue), intersected here. New York's first Labor Day Parade was held here in 1882, when 25,000 people under a Knights of Labor banner marched for an eight-hour day and a ban on child labor. In 1930, 35,000 demonstrators protested the Depression and clashed with the police here, leaving 100 people injured.
Union Square has been the home of the Amalgamated Clothing and Textile Workers Union and the Amalgamated Bank, the nation's first labor-owned bank, the Communist Party and its newspaper, The Daily Worker. Many artists, including Reginald Marsh and Raphael Soyer, had studios around 14th Street in the 1920's and 1930's and painted working people at this square.
Emma Goldman's Home
Next, walk to 208-210 East 13th Street, an orange-brick tenement where Emma Goldman, the anarchist and advocate of free love, lived from 1903 to 1913. She was born in Lithuania and moved to Rochester, where she worked as a seamstress. Soon after moving to New York City, she earned a reputation as an incendiary leftist orator. Her lover Alexander Berkman tried to assassinate Henry Clay Frick, the steel magnate, during the 1892 Homestead steel strike near Pittsburgh. She was sentenced to a year in jail for incitement to riot after a speech she gave at a Union Square hunger demonstration in 1893 in which she told the poor to steal bread if they could not afford to buy it. She was deported to Russia during the Red Scare of 1919, but she soon condemned that nation's Communist repression.
Nearby, at 12th Street and Fourth Avenue, wealthy women who had joined the Women's Trade Union League supported the ascendant working woman by climbing a ladder each afternoon to beseech apparel workers to join a union.
Head to WEBSTER HALL, at 119 East 11th Street, where Woody Guthrie sang on behalf of unions in the 1930's and where earlier the Socialist magazine The Masses held fund-raising balls. As part of a public-relations effort during the 1910 textile strike in Lawrence, Mass., Margaret Sanger, the Socialist who became famous for promoting birth control, led more than 100 Lawrence children from Grand Central Terminal to Webster Hall for dinner. Afterward, New Yorkers lodged and fed Lawrence children.
Walk next to TOMPKINS SQUARE PARK, at 10th Street and Avenue A. In the recession of 1874, 6,000 protesters from the Workingmen's Party gathered to hear New York's Mayor, William F. Havemeyer, speak about helping the unemployed. When he failed to appear and the police declared the rally illegal, the protesters clashed with the police, injuring hundreds of officers. Gompers cowered in a doorway nearby, and it is said that the riot turned him away from radical unionism.
Then walk along St. Marks Place to COOPER UNION, founded in 1859 by Peter Cooper, the philanthropist who made the first rolled steel railroad tracks and helped Samuel Morse lay the first Atlantic telegraph cable. Cooper created this school to provide free art and technology education to working people. In 1860, Abraham Lincoln gave a major address in the school's Great Hall. In 1909, Gompers led an emotional meeting of shirtwaist workers that authorized the Uprising of the 20,000. "There comes a time," he said, "when not to strike is but to rivet the chains of slavery upon our wrists."
At 64 East Fourth Street was the LABOR LYCEUM, a center for workers' classes, lectures, rallies and social gatherings. This boarded-up building once housed The Volkszeitung, a German-language workers' newspaper, and it was where the International Ladies' Garment Workers Union was founded in 1900.
Just south (at 55 East Third Street and 36 East First Street) are two buildings used by the Catholic Worker movement and its weekly newspaper (which still costs one cent), founded in 1933 by Dorothy Day and Peter Maurin. Day, who died in 1980, tirelessly backed pacifism, unions, helping the poor and reconciling Roman Catholicism with radicalism. She inspired many imitators when she opened a soup kitchen in 1933. The two buildings, MARYHOUSE and ST. JOSEPH HOUSE, today shelter several dozen homeless people and provide meals to about 300 people daily.
Commerce and Spirit
Across Houston Street is the Lower East Side, where many of the 1.2 million Jews who immigrated from 1880 to 1920 first settled. Many thronged into the garment trade, often working in sweatshops or sewing at home in their tiny, overcrowded apartments and even on their fire escapes.
Off the Bowery, at 10 and 80 Stanton Street, once stood the taverns where Gompers and other cigar workers met to organize a union. Gompers said the tavern owners complained that the unionists did too much talking and not enough drinking.
Just south of Rivington Street is UNIVERSITY SETTLEMENT, a handsome, dark-brick building at 184 Eldridge Street. Founded in 1886, it is the nation's oldest settlement house, and it provided immigrants with citizenship lessons and English instruction. It also inspired innovations like kindergartens and public baths. Eleanor Roosevelt used to teach dance here.
In a white-painted tenement at 174 Eldridge Street, some early meetings of the Workmen's Circle, a combined immigrants' mutual aid society and Socialist political group, took place in HYMAN LEVY'S APARTMENT.
Raucous Orchard Street, with its bustling shops, remains the neighborhood's commercial and spiritual heart. At 90 Orchard Street is the LOWER EAST SIDE TENEMENT MUSEUM, which uses a 132-year-old tenement to show the dark, sardine can-like conditions in which struggling immigrants once lived. As many as 15 people often inhabited a three-room apartment, and the neighborhood had the world's highest tuberculosis rate.
Throughout the neighborhood, the ghosts of labor remain. At Rivington Street and the Bowery, 30,000 people rallied during a 1912 garment workers' strike. At 127 Rivington was EISLER'S GOLDEN RULE HALL, where, during an 1882 longshoremen's strike, union leaders led 500 Jewish immigrants fresh off the boat to urge them not to work as scabs. Each morning, at the corner of Ludlow and Hester Streets, hundreds of immigrants gathered, hoping to be picked by hiring agents.
From a Polish Village
A surprisingly stylish building stands at 5 Ludlow Street, just above Canal Street. Constructed in 1892, it housed the INDEPENDENT KLETZKER BROTHERLY AID SOCIETY, a mutual aid society set up by workers from Kletzk, a Polish village. Nearby, at 54-58 Canal Street, is the JARMULOVSKY BANK BUILDING. Thousands of immigrants deposited their pennies here, but a panic developed before World War I when many depositors withdrew their money to help relatives flee Europe. On Aug. 4, 1914, Black Tuesday, the bank was ordered closed and riots broke out as thousands of immigrants lost their savings.
Head east to STRAUS SQUARE, at East Broadway and Rutgers Street, once the center of Jewish immigrant life. At the southeast corner, 165 East Broadway, was the Garden Cafeteria, founded in 1911, where Leon Trotsky, Isaac Bashevis Singer and other immigrants schmoozed and debated. It was closed in 1983, and replaced by Wing Shoon, a Chinese restaurant.
THE JEWISH DAILY NEWS was once published at 185 East Broadway. It hired a young apparel worker from Cleveland, Rose Pastor Stokes, to write an advice column. She became known as the Cinderella of the Sweatshops because after she interviewed James Graham Phelps Stokes, a millionaire who helped run University Settlement, he asked her to marry him. She became famous as an advocate of Socialism, Communism and birth control.
At 197 East Broadway is the EDUCATIONAL ALLIANCE, which German Jews who had immigrated four decades earlier founded in 1889 to educate Eastern European Jews, especially in English. Its tranquil rooftop, used for reading and relaxing, was dubbed "tar beach." Sholom Aleichem, the Yiddish writer, once spoke here and was called the Yiddish Mark Twain; later, when Mark Twain spoke here, Aleichem said he was happy to meet the English-language Sholom Aleichem.
The 12-story building at 173-175 East Broadway long dominated the Lower East Side, architecturally and intellectually. It housed THE JEWISH DAILY FORWARD, which was founded in 1897 by Abraham Cahan, a charismatic intellectual and labor advocate. To support Franklin D. Roosevelt's re-election in 1936, Cahan helped found the American Labor Party with David Dubinsky, the garment workers' union president, and Sidney Hillman, president of the Amalgamated Clothing Workers. On the building's facade is a frieze with busts of Marx and Engels, but they have been covered over by the building's current owner, the New York Ling-Liang Church. Still, the Yiddish lettering for Forward remains on the roof parapet.
The Forward's most popular feature was its Bintel Brief, or "bundle of letters," which was a colorful mirror of immigrant life and attitudes.
One letter said, "Dear Editor: I am a Socialist and my boss is a fine man. I know he's a capitalist but I like him. Am I doing something wrong?"
Another letter: "Esteemed Editor: My son is already 26 years old and doesn't want to get married. He says he is a Socialist and he is too busy. Socialism is Socialism, but getting married is important, too."
 
Five Tours

 For those preferring an organized walking tour of labor history sites in Manhattan, here is a sampling of what is scheduled this weekend and next: This Weekend
SALUTE TO LABOR DAY, Adventure on a Shoestring. A walking tour of the Union Square vicinity, an area rich in labor history. Monday at 1 P.M. Fee, $5. Meeting place and other information: (212) 265-2663.
"IMMIGRANT LABOR, IMMIGRANT TRIUMPH," Big Onion Walking Tours. A walking tour of the East Village and the Lower East Side, focusing on the role of immigrants and the labor movement in the growth of New York City. With stops at the sites of the Labor Lyceum and of the International Ladies' Garment Workers Union's founding, both on East Fourth Street, and the building that housed the Triangle Shirtwaist factory, on Washington Place. Meets on Monday at 1 P.M. at the arch in Washington Square. Fee: $9; $7 for students and the elderly. Information: (212) 439-1090.
LABOR HISTORY WALK, Manhattan Memories. A tour of the Union Square and Greenwich Village areas that includes the site of the Triangle Shirtwaist factory, the Jefferson Market Courthouse and the Women's Trade Union League site. Meets on Sunday at 1:30 P.M. at the corner of 14th Street and Union Square East, at Fourth Avenue. Fee: $15. Information: (212) 628-9517.
RADICAL WALKING TOUR. A tour of Chelsea Ladies Mile, including the site of the old Madison Square Garden, where the Paterson Pageant of 1913 was staged by striking silk workers from that New Jersey city to dramatize their plight. Meets on Sunday at 1 P.M. in front of the Chelsea Hotel, 23d Street between Seventh and Eighth Avenues. Fee: $6. Information: (718) 492-0069.
Next Weekend
EAST VILLAGE AND NOHO WALKING TOUR, a tour of radical history and architecture in New York City. Includes stops at the sites of the Civil War draft riots, the Labor Lyceum, May Day celebrations and Emma Goldman's house. Meets on Sept. 7 at 2:30 P.M. at 339 Lafayette Street, at Bleecker Street, East Village. Contributions accepted. Information: (718) 264-3021.

 CORRECTION:
An article in Weekend on Aug. 30 about Manhattan's labor history misidentified the woman who used her Madison Avenue mansion in 1909 as collateral to raise bail for striking garment workers. It was Mrs. O. H. P. Belmont, not Mrs. August Belmont.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Shortages and Labor Problems Send Copper Prices Up Sharply

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Copper prices surged in New York yesterday as labor problems at a number of mines and smelters led to fears of shortages.
On other commodities markets, heating oil and unleaded gasoline retreated. 
 Copper prices rose after an unexpectedly small increase in London Metal Exchange stockpiles. The exchange did not release a warehouse inventory report on Tuesday, and said yesterday that its stocks had increased 3,968 tons, to 303,439 tons. Expectations were for an increase of nearly 10,000 tons.
Announcements of labor problems added to uncertainty about supply.
The state-owned Chilean copper producer Codelco announced that a strike was planned at one of its divisions, and a strike in Namibia also supported prices.
Both operations contribute a relatively small amount of copper to world stocks, but many smelters have not been running at full capacity because of production problems.
"It's just not a bad time to buy some copper," said Wayne Atwell, an analyst with Morgan Stanley. "The economy is strong here, it's picking up in Europe and consumer demand is rising."
Copper for December delivery rose 4.55 cents, to 95.25 cents a pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the highest price for an active contract since June 26.
The expiring September gasoline and heating oil contracts retreated on the New York Mercantile Exchange because of profit taking after several days of strong gains.
The losses, however, were limited. Tight nearby supplies are likely to support prices in the short term, said John Saucer, an analyst with Smith Barney in Houston.
Reserves of heating oil and other distillates are about 17 percent below levels of a year earlier, while gasoline stockpiles are 3 percent below normal.
September unleaded gasoline fell 0.90 cent, to 62.82 cents a gallon, and September heating oil fell 0.85 cent, to 62.82 cents a gallon. October crude oil rose 60 cents, to $22.25 a barrel.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Rises in Choppy Session As Higher U.S. Rates Are Seen

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar rose against most leading currencies yesterday, holding onto gains made from a second day of strong economic data that pointed to higher interest rates in the United States.
"It's been pretty volatile," Chris Iggo, an international economist with Barclays Bank P.L.C., said of the choppy moves by the dollar before it ended higher in light, pre-holiday trading that saw many traders taking off by early afternoon. 
 In late trading in New York, the dollar stood at 108.92 Japanese yen, up from 108.30 yen on Thursday, and 1.4808 German marks, up from 1.4764 marks. But the dollar slipped against the British pound, which rose to $1.5620 from $1.5575.
The dollar was bolstered in European trading after Hans Tietmeyer, president of the Bundesbank, was quoted by the German Press Agency (D.P.A.) as saying the economic fundamentals of the United States "show that the dollar could be somewhat higher."
"Initially, the dollar was just up on these Tietmeyer comments overnight," said Richard Vullo, a vice president at Hypo Bank. "We came in with a stronger dollar."
But the dollar fell in early trading in New York, following the stock and bond markets lower on reports of rising consumer confidence and higher orders received by factories in the United States.
These were among other indications that the United States had a more robust economy than analysts had thought and than the markets had wanted.
Yesterday was the second consecutive decline in American stock and bond markets. Investors fear that signs of strong growth will soon prompt the Federal Reserve to push short-term interest rates higher.
But higher rates can help make the dollar more attractive by increasing returns on new investments in dollar-denominated assets.
A Federal Reserve governor, Lawrence B. Lindsey, helped the dollar yesterday with comments that traders interpreted as indicating that the Fed might be ready to raise interest rates.
The central bank's policy-making Federal Open Market Committee next meets on Sept. 24.
"The main feature here is that the dollar gave back early gains following stocks and bonds lower but is well supported by the notion the Fed will have to move as early as the September F.O.M.C. meeting," said David Gilmore, a partner at Foreign Exchange Analytics in Essex, Conn.
In other currency trading, the dollar rose to 5.0685 French francs, from 5.0580 French francs on Thursday and to 1.2024 Swiss francs, from 1.1944 francs.

HEADLINE: Journal;
Sex, Drugs And Dole

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH 

 BODY:
Is it true that Dick Morris shared the First Lady's and the Vice President's convention speeches with a prostitute days before they were delivered to the public? If so, let history note that this is the first recorded instance of the official oratory of either Hillary Clinton or Al Gore being used as an aphrodisiac.
The Democrats were certainly asking for this mess. Those who live by the infomercial die by the infomercial. There were 15,000 journalists gathered at Chicago's United Center with nothing but Harry Thomason's scripted show to cover, and 15,000 journalists abhor a news blackout as surely as nature does a vacuum. Mr. Morris, by choosing to play a scene from the wrong Thomason script ("Designing Women"), rode valiantly to the press's rescue, and, with impeccable showmanship, did so in the final reel. 
 But before Republicans get too giddy, they should remember that Mr. Morris, a bipartisan cad, may have spilled secrets about such other clients as Trent Lott and Jesse Helms at Washington's Jefferson Hotel. And they should remember that their candidate, as prone to repeating self-destructive behavior as is Mr. Clinton, remains remarkably inept. If Mr. Morris's alleged misadventures were the week's most profusely covered political story, the least covered, no less significant, was the Dole campaign's continued haplessness.
As the preponderance of the nation's political press partied on in Chicago, the Republican standard-bearer was on a "working vacation" in California. Heaven help the G.O.P. once Mr. Dole resumes stumping full time. In Santa Barbara on Tuesday, the same day that brought the first of the week's several positive economic indicators (a rising consumer confidence rating), he flatly declared that "the economy's in the tank." On Wednesday in Ventura, he started flip-flopping yet again about tobacco. On Wednesday night he responded to Al Gore's anti-tobacco speech in Chicago by calling the Vice President a "hatchet man" -- a bizarre choice of epithet, given its association with the low points of Mr. Dole's own career that he'd presumably like voters to forget.
Still, these self-immolating asides are nothing next to the overarching theme of Mr. Dole's week -- his repeated speeches pinning the rise of drug use among America's young entirely on the Clinton Administration. So shrill was the attack and so far-fetched was Mr. Dole's own proposed drug-prevention policy, which stopped just short of calling for the carpet-bombing of Latin America, that anyone could see that the candidate was once again playing the generational card. By attributing all drug criminality to the White House, Mr. Dole was reinforcing the combative thrust of his San Diego acceptance speech, in which he assaulted all the President's men and women as an "elite" who "never grew up, never did anything real, never sacrificed," and offered himself as "a bridge" to a far more moral generation, his own.
The political wisdom of such generational warfare has always been cloudy. Why risk alienating some 76 million baby boomers, all potential voters? Perhaps this week Mr. Dole was betting that the Democrats, once back at the scene of their '68 blowout, would prove counterculture recidivists. Maybe a Clinton aide or two would get busted for drugs by a new Mayor Daley and conveniently provide lurid TV imagery to support boomer-bashing rhetoric.
This was not to be. Though Crosby, Stills & Nash and a revival of "Hair" were in proximity, Chicago '96 was more tourist trap than acid trip. When the surviving members of the Chicago Seven reunited, it was only for the purpose of waxing nostalgic on "Larry King Live."
Mr. Gore and Mr. Clinton, walking though the door opened for them by their opponent's speech in San Diego, impaled Mr. Dole on the generational issue by recasting it as the age issue in their own acceptance speeches. With the determination of brainwashers, they repeatedly inverted his metaphorical use of the word "bridge," wielding the phrase "bridge to the 21st century" as a battering ram to portray him as an out-of-touch relic of a distant past.
So far Mr. Dole and his handlers seem more than willing to cooperate. With enemies like these, Mr. Clinton will have no trouble surviving friends like Dick Morris.

HEADLINE: Cracks Appear Yet Again In Transit Authority Buses

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

 BODY:
A problem that has plagued the Transit Authority repeatedly over the years is back: at least 50 relatively new buses, battered by New York City's washboard streets, have developed cracks, officials said yesterday.
The buses were built in 1993 by Bus Industries of America, a company that was based in Oriskany, N.Y., but that no longer exists. They have fractures in the beams and plates that hold up their rear floors, and in external panels above the rear wheel wells. Some of the cracks are as much as four inches long, and are visible to the naked eye. 
 "At this point, it's probably something that we can very easily fix, and keep it from becoming a structural problem," said Millard L. Seay, the Transit Authority's senior vice president in charge of buses. "It's one of those things that clearly, if you let it go three or four more years, it could be a serious issue."
In the bus industry, the Transit Authority is widely regarded as the toughest customer in the country. Largely because of the demands on city buses -- heavy passenger loads, pothole-scarred streets, frequent stops and starts and 24-hour operation -- the authority has more stringent specifications for construction than any other transit agency. Only two manufacturers' buses have met its standards in recent years, and most bus makers refuse even to try.
"We think some of their requirements are unreasonable," an official of one bus company that has not bid on a New York contract in many years said on condition of anonymity. "But to some degree, they just have to insist on higher tolerances than anyone else because of the punishment the buses have to take."
Several batches of buses have developed cracks over the last couple of decades, most notably those built in the early 1980's by Flxible, a Grumman Corporation subsidiary. The authority eventually decided that the problems with the Flxible buses were so intractable that it got rid of all 850 of them when they were still relatively new. Yet many of those buses had a long second incarnation with New Jersey Transit, running on more forgiving roads.
Mr. Seay said that conditions in New York may well have something to do with the latest spate of cracks. "Clearly, New York is a different environment than just about any other place," he said. "We run the buses longer and harder than anyone else, and we have tougher streets than most cities."
Since the cracks began to turn up a few months ago, the authority has checked 350 buses of similar vintage made by Bus Industries of America -- about 10 percent of the total fleet -- and found that 50 had cracks. "More than likely, it will show up in others as we go along," he said.
The buses are still under warranty, and transit officials said they were sure that the authority would not have to pay for the repairs.
Bus Industries of America, which had chronic financial troubles, was a subsidiary of Ontario Bus Industries, which was owned by the province of Ontario, Canada. Ontario Bus was later bought out by a private company, Orion Bus Industries, but New York City transit officials said they believed that as a condition of the deal, the province of Ontario, and not Orion, was responsible for making good on the warranty.
Officials at Ontario's Ministry of Economic Development in Toronto did not return phone calls yesterday.
The cracks were detected as buses were taken in for a routine three-year overhaul, and will be fixed as part of that overhaul without significantly delaying their return to service, Mr. Seay said. He said it may just be a matter of welding the fractures and welding new supports into place under the rear floors. How difficult and time-consuming that will be will depend, he said, on whether the repair can be made without removing the floors.
The Transit Authority has had trouble not just keeping its buses running, but buying enough of them. Its main supplier over the last 15 years, the Transportation Manufacturing Corporation of New Mexico, has had recurring financial troubles, and it has at times fallen far behind on its orders, as did Bus Industries of America. As a result, the authority has kept more than 1,000 buses in service past their scheduled 12-year retirement age.

HEADLINE: Mixed Signs in Flurry of Economic Data

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
The United States economy is giving mixed signals, but financial markets reacted today to two nongovernmental indicators that depicted the economy as picking up speed quickly after a midyear lull.
The Purchasing Management Association of Chicago said its overall index soared to 60 in August from 51.2 in July. An index reading higher than 50 means manufacturers that reported improved business outnumbered those that reported deteriorating conditions. And a report from the University of Michigan showed that its final index of consumer sentiment for August rose to 95.3 from 94.7 in July -- the highest reading since January 1995. 
 For many investors, those signs of an economy powering ahead overshadowed an early morning Commerce Department report that showed consumer spending rose only two-tenths of 1 percent last month.
That was an improvement from June's decline of four-tenths of 1 percent but only half the four-tenths of 1 percent average monthly increase for the first seven months of the year. Weak spending on autos and other big-ticket goods restrained the July spending increase.
Also, personal income inched up just a tenth of 1 percent in July -- the weakest showing since January and a noticeable reduction from June's nine-tenths of 1 percent increase and the five-tenths of 1 percent average for the first seven months of the year.
In response to the signs of the economy's heating up, stock and bond prices plunged for a second consecutive day. The concern is that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates to try to cool the economy.
"It's the best of both worlds if you're a manufacturer, but it's the worst of all worlds for the Federal Reserve," Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at the First Albany Bank, said.
Signals of quick growth also came from the Government. Orders placed with American factories rose in July to the highest level on record, led by demand for electronics and industrial machinery, Commerce Department figures showed. The 1.8 percent increase for the month was double what analysts had expected.
The president of the National Association of Manufacturers, Jerry J. Jasinowski, who for months has been arguing against a rate increase by the Fed, said July's small rise in spending led him to conclude investors were overreacting.
The benchmark 30-year Treasury bond fell 11/32 points, to 9516/32 , after the day's second wave of economic reports was released at 10 A.M. The long bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose 9 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, to 7.12 percent. Stocks also fell. The Dow Jones industrial average, after slumping almost 60 points, closed down 31.44 at 5,616.21.
The Commerce Department also said today that disposable income increased a tenth of 1 percent in July, while the savings rate was unchanged from a month earlier at 5.3 percent.
Because it accounts for two-thirds of economic activity, consumer spending provides a key indicator of where the economy is going. Based on the findings of the last two months -- June's consumer spending decline turned out to be twice as large as the two-tenths of 1 percent loss originally estimated a month ago -- the message from today's Commerce Department spending report was mixed.
Still, retail sales have increased since a stall in July, which analysts linked to people's staying home to watch television coverage of the Olympic Games.

HEADLINE: Unbridled Wanderlust;
Vacationers Are Hitting the Road in Record Numbers

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
Vacationers have taken to the roads and the air in record numbers this summer, undaunted by fires in national parks, power failures in the West, a hurricane threat that forced widespread evacuations in the Southeast and unseasonably cold, rainy weather from New England to the Carolinas. Not even the crash of T.W.A. Flight 800 curbed their wanderlust.
"From every report we've gotten, we feel confident it was another record summer," the third in a row, Shawn Flaherty, a spokeswoman for the nonprofit Washington-based Travel Industry Association of America, said this week of the travel season that officially ends this weekend. She said that the number of individual trips of at least 100 miles taken by Americans during June, July and August would apparently rise 2 percent from last year's record. 
 As another example, the number of miles flown by paying passengers increased 6.5 percent in July over a year earlier, and the percentage of seats occupied by paying passengers on airline flights rose to 74.3 percent in July from 71.7 percent a year earlier, according to the Air Transport Association. In fact, the seat occupancy, or "load factor," for the entire industry so far this year is 71.7 percent, far higher than 1995's record 67 percent, which was the highest since World War II.
To the surprise of some travel officials, the crash of Paris-bound T.W.A. Flight 800 on July 17 appeared to have had no measurable impact on international travel. "There was some talk at the time of that happening, but the people I talk to say there's been no impact," said Jon F. Ash, managing director of Global Aviation Associates, a consulting firm in Washington. "Instead, demand for Europe is probably up 4 percent this summer, and load factors almost everywhere are very high." AMR's American Airlines, Delta Air Lines and UAL's United Airlines also say their advance bookings are well ahead of last year's.
Indeed, Americans apparently fulfilled European Travel Commission predictions of a record 4.9 million American visitors this summer. The commission expects a record 9 million for all of 1996.
United Airlines averaged more than 80 percent of its seats filled this summer on its routes to Europe. And its nine daily flights to Hawaii averaged 93.9 percent two weeks ago. With practically every flight full, the lead item in United's employee newsletter said: "Going to Hawaii? Make backup travel plans."
Backup plans were not a bad idea on the ground either. Marriott International said its occupancy rate this summer averaged as much as 12 percent above the national average, which would put Marriott occupancies around 80 percent. Occupancy at Manor Care's Choice International hotels -- Comfort, Quality, Clarion, Sleep, Econo Lodge and Rodeway -- averaged 9.2 percent higher this summer than last summer. Both the increases are exceptionally strong for the hotel industry, and come on top of a strong summer last year.
The reasons for the disaster-defying summer, tourist officials agree, are a relatively strong American economy, the dollar's gathering strength against European currencies and a decline in gasoline prices from their peak of $1.38 a gallon on May 24 (although still almost 8 cents a gallon higher than in August 1995). Also, "You had more air fare sales than I can ever remember, although some were from a higher base than last year," said Jerry Cheske, a spokesman for the American Automobile Association in Heathrow, Fla.
In late June, Continental Airlines slashed domestic fares as much as 40 percent for travel through September, and other carriers basically matched the sale prices on directly competitive routes.
Yet while air fares, gasoline prices and the cost of meals and lodging obviously have an effect on travel, attitudes may be even more decisive. Statistical evidence suggests that Americans increasingly regard travel and tourism as an important part of their life style.
Total trips taken by Americans increased 45 percent from 1985 to 1995, even as the population grew only 10 percent, according to a survey of 18,000 households by the Travel Industry Association of America. And the percentage of American adults who took at least one trip of 100 miles or more away from home in 1995 rose to 76 percent from 63 percent a decade earlier.
The automobile association predicted last spring that summer vacationers would spend an average of 8.5 nights away from home this summer, compared with 7.9 nights last year, but that a family of four would spend the same $1,076 this year as last, and Mr. Cheske said the figures still looked to be on target.
Jim Sortino, owner of the Sleep Inn in Sandusky, Ohio, the site of Cedar Point Amusement Park, said that even though bad weather kept people away in May and June, July and August resulted in one of Sandusky's biggest seasons ever.
California has had an especially strong summer tourist season. Although attendance was down at Sequoia National Park, whose giant, ancient trees for a time were threatened by wildfires, Universal Studios and other theme parks chalked up record attendance, and Los Angeles and San Diego enjoyed their highest hotel occupancies in years. San Diego tourism officials said the Republican convention, which drew thousands of delegates and journalists, was an added bonus for the city's hotels and restaurants.
Orlando, Fla., and surrounding towns also had good summers, as did even steamy New Orleans, which was host to this summer's Junior Olympics and increased its convention business with lower summer rates for hotels.
The national parks appear to be "booming as usual," Elaine Sevy, a National Parks Service spokeswoman, said, despite the problems at Sequoia and at the Cape Hatteras, N.C., National Seashore, where visitors fled Hurricane Bertha's fury.
Attendance at Great Smoky Mountains National Park in Tennessee and North Carolina has been up slightly over last summer, Bill Miller, a spokesman there, said, adding that it might have been even higher, were it not for the Summer Olympics in Atlanta.
While the Games proved to be a bonanza for Atlanta and nearby cities -- with almost two million visitors spending an estimated $4 billion, according to the Georgia Department of Industry, Trade and Tourism -- tourist industry officials from Wilmington, N.C., with its miles of ocean-front beaches, to the Florida state line said they had received little spillover.
A sampling of some smaller attractions turned up a mixed record. The Lawrence Welk homestead in Strasburg, N.D., had a "very good" summer, according to Rosemary Schaefbauer, the president of Pioneer Heritage, Inc., which maintains the site in memory of the musician who died in 1992. Overall attendance is up only about 4 percent from the usual summer average of 5,000 visitors, but souvenir sales have risen, she said.
The National Cowboy Hall of Fame in Oklahoma City is off about 7 percent this summer, according to M. J. Van Deventer, the director of publications, perhaps because of a $36 million expansion program in advance of the Sept. 22 opening of the hall's yearlong Frederick Remington exhibition -- the biggest Remington exhibition ever assembled. But, Ms. Van Deventer added, hundreds of cars with out-of-state license plates come to Oklahoma City each weekend to view the site of the Alfred P. Murrah Federal building, where 168 people were killed in an April 1995 bombing.
Across the country, vacationers with advance reservations still flocked to the beaches from the New Jersey shore to as far south as Myrtle Beach, S.C. -- even though it sometimes meant wearing sweaters and sleeping beneath blankets.
While beach concessionaires suffered from the cold, rainy weather, tourists found other places to spend their dollars.
"If they didn't go to the beach, they went shopping, or ate out more often," said Linda Conlin, the director of tourism for New Jersey. "So many of our retailers still benefited." More than half of New Jersey's $22.9 billion in tourist revenue last year came from the shore communities, including Atlantic City, whose casinos appear to have profited from the forgettable weather.
Las Vegas, Nev., the other major gambling mecca, had a big summer, as usual, with hotel occupancy up about 3 percent. When the second blackout that hit the West this summer knocked out power several hours for millions of people in at least nine states in August, Las Vegas casinos simply switched to backup generators, and the good times continued to roll.
"The blackout affected air-conditioners and electricity in private houses," said Myram Borders, a spokeswoman for Las Vegas. "So a lot of local residents went to the casinos to eat in order to stay cool."
Adversity had its compensations elsewhere, as well. When the approach of Hurricane Bertha forced Bill and Gloria Gillette of Cleveland to vacate the Outer Banks of North Carolina a day before their vacation was to end, they and their two children drove inland to Virginia's Skyline Drive and made an unscheduled stop at Monticello, the home and burial place of Thomas Jefferson, who designed it.
"The evacuation turned out to be a bonus," said Mr. Gillette, the executive editor of Hotel & Motel Management magazine, "because we got to see another part of the country."

HEADLINE: Bond Prices Fall Again on Fears of Fed Move

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities continued Thursday's retreat yesterday as more economic evidence suggested that the economy was not slowing as much as many investors had hoped, making a Federal Reserve move to push up interest rates increasingly likely.
In an abbreviated session, ahead of Monday's Labor Day holiday, the 30-year bond tumbled 11/32 , to a price of 9516/32 to yield 7.12 percent, up from 7.03 percent on Thursday. The yield is the highest since July 9, when it was 7.13 percent. 
 Investor concern was fed by data on July factory orders, which rose a larger-than-expected 1.8 percent, double what the market had expected. Furthermore, the Chicago Purchasing Managers Index, increased to 60 in August from 51.2 in July, well above the expected 53.1 reading, and the final number on the University of Michigan's consumer sentiment index for August rose to 95.3, from July's reading of 94.7. The latest figure was the highest since January 1995.
Jack Ablin, director of fixed-income securities and senior fund manager at the Private Bank of the Bank of Boston, said the market saved its strongest reaction for the purchasing managers' report. "The fact is, it was a good predictor of the National Association of Purchasing Management report the last time around, so people obviously took notice today," he said. The national report for August is due for release at 10 A.M. on Tuesday.
Mr. Ablin said, however, that the wild card in the mix of economic data is labor. The question is whether there is a tight labor market, and consequent price acceleration. "The only other unanswered question is the Fed," he said. "We don't believe they will tighten in September, but there is certainly more evidence for those in the September camp who expect a rate rise."
Discussing the personal income and spending data, Mr. Ablin said the rise of one-tenth of 1 percent in income was only a bit above the expectation for a drop of one-tenth of 1 percent, while spending came in right on expectations, with a gain of two-tenths of 1 percent.
Ken McCarthy, founder of the Economic Intelligence Company, a research firm, said, "Confirming earlier releases, the income and spending report shows that the economy was growing more slowly as it entered the second half of 1996."
He said the small increase in income was centered on nonwage components like dividends, interest, transfer payments and proprietor's income, all of which rose enough in July to offset a drop of one-tenth of 1percent in wages and salaries.
"Although the markets have suddenly turned jittery over the possbility that the economy is not slowing," Mr. McCarthy said, "the data we have seen so far certainly suggest that the economy continues to grow at a moderate pace."
In his opinion, the economy is not strong enough to create inflationary pressures, and he, therefore, does not expect the Fed to move between now and the end of the year.
Jack Regan, chief derivative strategist at Josephthal, Lyon & Ross, took another view. Yesterday's numbers, he said, are compounding the feeling within the market that the economy has more strength and is moving ahead faster than previously thought. Mr. Regan said he saw a change in the market's perception, and that is "that the Fed is now behind the curve and that on or if not before the next F.O.M.C. meeting on Sept. 24, the Fed may find it necessary to tighten."
"Some talk of a 50-basis-point increase in interest rates," Mr. Regan said, adding, "I think the Fed will wait to see next week's data."
The statistics due next week include the leading indicators, the national purchasing managers' report, auto sales and, on Friday, the all-important employment data.
"Today the market probably priced in about a 25-basis-point move by the Fed," Mr. Regan said, "next week we could see another 25 priced in if the data continue to show stronger-than-expected strength."

HEADLINE: The Easy One In Tax Breaks: Who's Against Homeowners?

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL 

 BODY:
It's not hard to guess what homeowners will think of the very similar plans offered by Bob Dole and Bill Clinton to expand tax breaks on home sales. Both are intended to eliminate taxes on capital gains on all but the most profitable homes.
Nor is it hard to see why both candidates were quick to embrace the idea. The beneficiaries will mostly be affluent suburbanites in states with high housing costs whose votes and campaign donations could spell the difference between victory and defeat in November. Moreover, the cost will be minimal since few home sellers today pay capital gains taxes anyway. 
 "This cat is 99 percent out of the bag," explained Alan Auerbach, an economist at the University of California at Berkeley. Freeing it entirely, he suggests, might actually help the economy by encouraging homeowners to "trade down," taking their profits out of their houses and investing them elsewhere.
Owner-occupied housing is already king of the very large hill of deductions, credits, deferrals and exemptions that riddle the Federal tax system. Among the most prized of these preferences: taxes on capital gains from the sale of a principal residence are deferred indefinitely if the seller buys an equally valuable house within two years. Sellers age 55 or over can also take a one-time, tax-free capital gain of $125,000 with no strings attached.
Bob Dole would retain the tax deferral for sellers who soon buy another house. But his plan would also let sellers of any age have the option of realizing up to $250,000 in capital gains tax free for houses owned for five years, with an extra $25,000-a-year exemption for longer tenure. Hence a family that bought a house for $125,000 and sold it 30 years later for $1,000,000 -- an $875,000 capital gain -- would owe no tax. Nor would there be a limit to the number of times the exemption could be claimed.
President Clinton's alternative is less generous in some ways and more in others. Unlike Mr. Dole, he would end the capital gains deferral on house sales. But a seller could take a maximum of $500,000 in tax-free capital gains on the sale of a principal residence every two years.
Both plans, then, would eliminate Federal tax liability on all but a narrow sliver of home sales. And both would be selective in awarding the goodies, favoring the rich over the middle-income homeowners who generally have smaller capital gains and lower tax rates on the gains they do realize. "The benefits would be distributed regressively," Joel Slemrod, an economist at the University of Michigan Business School, said.
The generational and regional distribution of the benefits within income groups would also be capricious. Winners would mostly be people who bought houses before the run-up in prices from 1975 to 1990, not to mention residents in a few urban areas on the East and West Coasts -- Los Angeles, the San Francisco Bay area, Seattle, New York City, Boston -- where housing prices rose into the stratosphere.
But most economists, for all their complaints about the politically inspired tax advantages enjoyed by homeowners in general, do not seem disturbed by the latest proposals. And in large part, their attitude follows from the fact that the latest proposals would cost the Treasury very little.
Congress's Joint Committee on Taxation estimates that the existing combination of deferral of capital gains on rollovers and the one-time $125,000 credit at age 55 will reduce tax revenues by a bit more than $20 billion this year. But decisions to sell houses are now so carefully tuned to tax consequences that the additional breaks would probably add only a few hundred million dollars to the annual revenue loss.
"Hardly anybody pays the capital gains tax on housing, so eliminating it wouldn't make much difference," said Harvey Rosen, an economist at Princeton University who was a deputy assistant secretary of the Treasury for tax analysis in the Bush Administration.
That, of course, makes broadening the capital gains exemption a deficit-conscious Presidential candidate's dream. Here, after all, is a tax break with great popular appeal that requires only modest offsetting revenues. And here, miracle of miracles, is a tax break that even passes muster with policy wonks -- and for many of the same reasons that it would please homeowners.
For one thing, the tax break would eliminate the "lock in" effect for people inclined to move to smaller quarters or to become renters -- say, when their children leave home -- but who are loath to pay taxes on what are often the profits of a lifetime. Those most affected within this group are people in their late 40's and early 50's who can now take a $125,000 gain tax free only if they wait until age 55.
It is even conceivable, Mr. Auerbach says, that the tax change would reduce the overall demand for large houses, partly offsetting existing tax-induced distortions that virtually all economists agree funnel too much of Americans' savings into housing at the expense of investment in productivity-enhancing industrial capital. This inclination to downsize, incidentally, might also gradually bring down the price of large houses.
Note, too, that the change would eliminate a source of friction between taxpayers and the Internal Revenue Service. Under current law, homeowners must keep records of housing purchases and improvements indefinitely, carefully distinguishing outlays for capital improvements from spending on maintenance. A lot of home sellers never even file the proper forms. Under the Dole and Clinton plans, the record-keeping would be greatly simplified.
The only real downside, Mr. Slemrod suggests, is the erosion of the spirit that animates calls for a fundamental overhaul of the tax system. All the major tax plans floated over the last two years -- everything from the Forbes flat tax to the Lugar national sales tax -- envisioned a broadening of the tax base and a winnowing of special preferences. But the enthusiasm with which both the Dole and Clinton campaigns have embraced these latest targeted tax breaks demonstrates just how hard it is to take the politics out of tax policy.

HEADLINE: Arafat's Call for Prayer Protest Gets Modest Response

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN 

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, Aug. 30

 BODY:
A rigid Israeli police cordon and an apparent shortage of enthusiasm among Palestinians made for a modest and peaceful turnout today at Al Aksa Mosque, where Yasir Arafat had called on Palestinians to converge for protest prayers.
Palestinian officials said about 15,000 worshipers gathered at the mosque here, and the Israeli police put the number at 8,000. On a normal Friday, the holy day for Muslims, the sacred site draws as many as 50,000. 
 Palestinian spokesmen blamed a taut Israeli security operation, in which thousands of policemen and soldiers blocked roads from the West Bank and checked identity papers in Jerusalem to prevent Palestinians without permits from entering the city.
"They are fighting people economically and socially, and even banning them from practicing their worship," declared Hassan Tahboub, the minister of Islamic affairs in the Palestinian Authority, who had predicted that more than 100,000 would heed Mr. Arafat's call.
But a tour of the checkpoints showed relatively few Palestinians trying to pass, and many Jerusalem residents, who had no trouble reaching the mosque, stayed away. There was also no sign that Israeli Arabs had come in any large numbers.
Palestinians gave varying explanations for the evident shortage of interest. Chief among them was a sense that a four-hour strike called by Mr. Arafat for Thursday had already produced results, reducing the sense of urgency.
"The turnout was not a failure for Arafat," said Mofid Abu Rabbo, a member of the Palestinian Legislative Council from Tulkarm. "Yesterday's strike was successful, so today there was no seriousness in dealing with this. The strike achieved its aims and had results and there were contacts between the two sides."
The contacts, prominently reported on evening television programs in Arabic and Hebrew, included one between a lawyer close to Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu, Yitzhak Molho, and Mr. Arafat; another between Mr. Netanyahu's foreign affairs adviser, Dore Gold, and Mr. Arafat's deputy, Mahmoud Abbas, and a third between the chief Israeli and Palestinian negotiators, Dan Shomron and Saeb Erekat.
There were also reports that Mr. Netanyahu had a long telephone conversation about the Palestinian situation with King Hussein of Jordan and that the Israeli leader received an urgent message from President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt. Another report said the United States Ambassador, Martin Indyk, held a secret meeting with Mr. Arafat in Ramallah on Tuesday to deliver a message from President Clinton.
Though no details of the discussions were disclosed, the meetings were followed by speculation that a major development was imminent in the stalled Israeli-Palestinian relations, and that a meeting between Mr. Netanyahu and Mr. Arafat could be announced soon. Mr. Netanyahu's reluctance to meet with Mr. Arafat has been viewed as one of the obstacles to resuming peace talks.
These reports evidently lowered the sense of urgency that Mr. Arafat had generated when he declared on Wednesday that Israeli policies on settlements and on Jerusalem were effectively a "declaration of war against the Palestinian people."
Palestinian officials further noted that Mr. Arafat did not press the call for prayers too energetically. His original appeal on Wednesday was less forceful than his call for the general strike, and Palestinians said no efforts were made to spread the summons through neighborhoods. Mr. Arafat himself went to Nablus today for prayers and meetings with local officials.
Some Palestinians suggested that Mr. Arafat's call for prayers in Jerusalem was intended more as a signal to the Israelis than as an actual summons to mass protest -- that it was meant to show Israelis that Mr. Arafat was not abandoning his claim to the city, which Mr. Netanyahu has declared to be solely and eternally Israeli.
Abdul Malik Dahamshe, an Arab member of the Israeli Parliament, said Mr. Arafat's call came as a surprise, and left no time for leaders to organize. "There was no coordination with us, it was a spontaneous call," he said.
Hatem Abdul Kader, a member of the Palestinian Legislative Council, said "the call was not organized in a way that would allow a flood of worshipers to pray in Al Aksa."
"In my opinion," he added, "the call for prayer was more for the media than for the people in the streets. If Arafat wanted it, it would have had another shape. He wanted to send a signal to Israel and the world."
Mr. Kader also said the lukewarm reaction of Palestinians from Jerusalem reflected their dissatisfaction with the Palestinian Authority and Mr. Arafat. "The citizens are not satisfied with the way the Authority is acting," he said. "There's no transparency, no accountability, and Jerusalemites feel the Authority has a lack of interest in Jerusalem."
The mounting pressures on Mr. Netanyahu to start dealing with the Palestinians were countered by new pressures from his right-wing Government. The evening news program reported that Ariel Sharon, the Minister of Infrastructures and one of the staunchest hard-liners in the Government, angrily protested at a Cabinet meeting his exclusion from the team negotiating with the Palestinians. Among Cabinet members, only Mr. Netanyahu and Foreign Minister David Levy are on the negotiating committee.
"I will not be a consultant to the Government," Mr. Sharon stormed. "The subject of Hebron was one of the main reasons I joined the Government."

HEADLINE: Tokyo Stocks at 5-Month Low, Reflecting Worries Over Growth

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Japan's benchmark stock index plunged to its lowest level in five months today, led by declines in shares of car and steel makers, reflecting concern that Japan's economic growth has stalled.
"The recovery is much weaker than anyone expected," said Yoshio Inamura, who manages 50 billion yen at Tokyo-Mitsubishi Asset Management Ltd. "There's nothing positive for the market. It can only be flat or sink." 
 The benchmark Nikkei 225 stock index fell 386.26, or 1.88 percent, to 20,166.90, the lowest since it fell to 19,923.7 on March 14. It was the index's biggest one-day decline since July 24. For the week the index fell 5.6 percent.
The announcement on Wednesday of an unexpected decline in the Bank of Japan's closely watched "tankan" survey of business sentiment led to concern Japan's economic recovery has yet to take hold, and the market fell Wednesday, Thursday and today.
Shares of steelmakers and other companies that tend to track the performance of the overall economy, known as cyclicals, led the decliners.
The NKK Corporation fell 4 yen, to 287, while the Kawasaki Steel Corporation shares slipped 6 yen, to 365.
Among the auto makers. Toyota fell 30 yen, to 2,620; Nissan lost 17 yen, to 813; Suzuki was down 20 yen, to 1,240, and Isuzu Motors plunged 22 yen, to 548.
The Nippon Metal Industry Company, which makes stainless steel, fell 27 yen, to 427, after forecasting a pretax loss of 1.8 billion yen ($16.6 million) for the half-year ending in September.
Reports that the Government may be reluctant to continue pumping up the economy with public spending also hurt shares, said Hiroshi Masuda, equity trading manager at the Yamaichi Securities Company.
Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto told reporters in Hawaii Thursday that it was too early to discuss whether the Government should compile a supplementary budget for this fiscal year.
