HEADLINE: Drums That Beat Out the Sounds of Home;
A Corner of Central Park Has Become a Gathering Spot for Sunday Rumba

BYLINE: By MIRTA OJITO 

 BODY:
Directions to a Sunday party: enter Central Park near the Dakota on West 72d Street and walk through Strawberry Fields past the John Lennon memorial. Right about there, if the wind is blowing a certain way, you'll hear the insistent call of the drums. Tum-taca-taca-tum.
Follow it. 
 There, by the side of the Lake, you will find a piece of Cuba, or of its music, which for many of the drummers and listeners who gather here every Sunday is the same.
"This, and nothing but this, is the essence of being Cuban," said Arturo Cuenca, 40, a painter who left Cuba six years ago and now lives in Manhattan. "I didn't know that in Cuba. I learned it from a distance. Here, with these drums."
These drums have lured Cubans, Puerto Ricans, Dominicans -- and just about anybody who cares about rumba -- to Central Park for a long time. They offer free weekly entertainment for New Yorkers of a certain sensibility. And more importantly for some, they offer what many immigrants are perpetually searching for: a whiff of home, a space where people ask not what country you come from, but what town.
It is here, perhaps more than in any other place in New York, where many Cubans feel at home. This is the one corner of the city where they encounter old friends, enjoy homemade Cuban food, and effortlessly revert to rapid-fire Spanish.
"I have found people here that I hadn't seen for years," said Rosa Nieses-Torres two Sundays ago. "I come here as much as I can. I was born and raised in the old part of Havana, where this sort of thing was an everyday event.
"Coming here is spiritual for me," said Ms. Nieses-Torres, a college literature professor who came to New York from Cuba in 1979. "It's an act of remembrance; a ritual."
It starts with the music.
The drummers begin arriving shortly after noon.
Eddie Bobe is one of them.
Mr. Bobe was born 36 years ago in Puerto Rico to a Cuban father and a Puerto Rican mother, and the beat of the drums "is in his blood."
"I'm following a tradition, that's all," he said, wiping the sweat from his brow with an open palm. "For me, it's a cultural expression, a way of defining who I am, because rumba means people. The instruments alone can't do it. You need to have people to have rumba."
Before long, a crowd has gathered.
Mothers with babies in strollers sit on the green benches. Young couples share an old blanket on the lawn. Old men respectfully stand by the drums, waiting for the first cue to join in the chorus. Food vendors peddle their dishes: pasteles and tamales from Puerto Rico; cod fritters and rice with chicken from Cuba. A man sells beer out of a brown plastic bag packed with ice.
The drums attract a mixed crowd: the die-hard rumba aficionados, the tourists who stop to take yet another picture of New York City life (some make awkward attempts to follow the rhythm with their hips) and the nostalgic types who come not just for the music but also for a slice of Caribbean warmth and camaraderie in the land of McDonald's and rock-and-roll.
And then there are the curious passers-by who stay for so long that eventually someone hands them an instrument. There seems to be a silent command behind the friendly gesture: here, you make music, too.
It happened to Iwao Sado, 48, a sushi chef who came to New York from California 11 years ago.
As Mr. Sado tells it, he was walking in the park one day when he heard the sound of drums. He followed it and came upon a group of people singing in Spanish, a language he is now trying to learn. He became fascinated by the music's rhythm and timing. An electric bass player in Japan, Mr. Sado had never been as deeply touched by any other music.
"Something happened inside of me," he said. "It just totally transformed me."
He started taking lessons from Mr. Bobe and others and soon got a chance to play for the Central Park crowd. The music led him to Santeria, an Afro-Cuban religion practiced on the island and increasingly abroad. Mr. Sado recently made a trip to Cuba, where he officially joined the religion.
Nowadays, "El Chino" -- a term that Cubans and Puerto Ricans use to describe anyone with Asian features -- plays the drums next to some of the most experienced musicians in the park.
Nobody here seems sure how these rumba Sundays started. Some say it started spontaneously 80 years ago with a group of friends who wanted to play drums outdoors. Others recall a day in May 14 years ago when a group of Latinos, looking for a place to continue to party after a big parade, settled on this specific spot in Central Park and started a tradition.
Morton Sanders has another version. His, he insists, is the true story.
Mr. Sanders, a retired architectural designer who is 75, said he became immersed in Cuban culture after a series of trips to the island in the 1940's and 50's. He had gone to Cuba for the first time on his honeymoon and was immediately smitten with the island's sensuality and contagious joie de vivre.
For $35, he bought a drum and hired a private instructor. He returned to his Manhattan apartment and practiced until his fingers were calloused and his palms no longer hurt.
Eventually, accompanied by two friends, Mr. Sanders took his drum to a fountain in Central Park where Latinos, mostly Puerto Ricans born in New York, used to gather. That was 30 years ago.
With his blond hair and blue eyes, Mr. Sanders wasn't immediately accepted, he said. Undaunted, he took his music someplace else. He found a bench he liked next to the boat lake under the shade of a big old tree. And there, with his drum and his friends, Mr. Sanders began to play for fun.
Intrigued by the music, the Latinos who had shunned him soon followed. Since then, Mr. Sanders said, musicians and fans have got together every Sunday, from spring to fall, in the same spot.
In 1980, the gatherings received an unexpected jolt when Cubans who had arrived in the Mariel-Key West boat lift began appearing.
The Cubans, most of them blacks from economically poor but culturally rich Havana neighborhoods, brought new energy and authenticity to the gatherings.
Rumba, which is not a rhythm, but rather a concept that means to party, was born in Cuba more than a century ago. It is regarded as the first musical expression of Cuba's nationhood, said Rene Lopez, a Puerto Rican record producer who lives in New York.
It began with the black Cubans who had moved to the cities from rural areas. Influenced by Spanish music, but emotionally tied to their traditional African rhythms, they took elements of both and created a new sound.
Although the sound is distinctly Cuban, it has traveled the world.
It was popularized in the 1940's in New York's biggest nightclubs by performers like Chano Pozo and Miguelito Valdes, who used trumpets and other musical instruments to make it more palatable to American dancers. Yet rumba can be played on just about any surface, from a wooden box to an upside-down dresser drawer. Most often, it is played with at least three drums, a set of claves (sticks), a lead singer and a chorus.
In the early 60's, Mr. Lopez said, Puerto Ricans played drums on the rooftops of El Barrio. Eventually, they took their music to local parks, but the police used to chase them away and confiscate the drums.
"They said it bothered the neighbors, but nobody believed them," Mr. Lopez said. "They just weren't ready for us, didn't understand us."
Today, Mr. Sanders said, the police do not bother the Central Park crowd, though it can get rowdy at times toward the end of the day. Sometimes, there are too many people, maybe even one or two who have had too much to drink. There have been fights, often over who gets to play the drums.
But most Sundays, by early evening, the party has become a huge family picnic. Budding friendships are cemented with a drunken hug and a promise to call. Old friends agree, once again, to keep in touch. A shirtless man gently touches a baby's forehead and asks the gods, his gods, to bless him.
The drummers, perspiring from hours of intense pounding, get up to leave, already making plans for the next weekend.
Mr. Sanders is one of the last to go. "I have had such good times here," he said. "I don't want to stay away. I have told my wife that when I die I want her to throw my ashes in this lake. That way, I'll always be close to the music."

HEADLINE: A Union Gives an Old Refinery a New Lease on Life

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS 

DATELINE: MARCUS HOOK, Pa., Aug. 30

 BODY:
In an agonizing choice but one that is often faced by unions these days, members of the Oil, Chemical and Atomic Workers Union approved a new contract today under which about one-third of the workers will lose their jobs when the Tosco Corporation reopens a refinery here by next July.
Denis J. Stephano, the president of union local 8-234 in Linwood, Pa., said tonight that 93 percent of the 230 members who voted were in favor of the contract. Two hundred workers will get new jobs when the refinery is reopened, but about 100 will lose their jobs. 
 The workers had voted down a previous company offer and have been out of work since Tosco shut down the refinery at the end of January. For many, the vote today was a grudging acceptance of economic reality -- it was necessary to retain a job, get off the unemployment rolls and begin paying off bills when the refinery reopens.
"It's been bad for everybody," said Ed Creely, a 52-year-old worker who has spent 18 years in maintenance at the refinery. His unemployment benefits ran out months ago, and his bills have been mounting. Sitting in the union hall today, he said he had been seeking work but had had no offers. "Jobs are very hard to get around here," he added, since many other plants have closed in the area.
But he considers himself one of the lucky ones because he was offered a job by Tosco and hopes that he may even begin working in the next few months as the company upgrades the refinery.
For Tosco management the new contract provides an opportunity to extend the company's already powerful position as an important Northeastern refiner. It plans to invest about $110 million to upgrade the refinery, which it acquired from British Petroleum last November. It will be able to turn out 150,000 barrels daily of various products including 100,000 barrels of gasoline or about 10 percent of the region's production.
Combined with the huge 280,000-barrel Bayway refinery in Linden, N.J., which the company took over from the Exxon Corporation in 1993, the Pennsylvania refinery will bring Tosco close to the capacity of the region's largest refiner, the Sun Oil Company, based in Philadelphia.
Tosco has also begun to build up its retail end, having bought the rights to sell gasolines produced at Tosco refineries through about 500 B.P. stations in the Northeast. It also supplies gasoline to stations of other major oil companies in the region, including Exxon.
"We're making a fresh start," Thomas J. Nimbley, a senior vice president of Tosco who helped forge the contract, said in an interview at his office at the Bayway refinery. Tosco has poured $120 million in improvements into that refinery.
The reopening of the Marcus Hook refinery, which will be renamed Trainer after the town where most of it is situated, is expected to help stabilize gasoline supplies and prices in the Northeast during times of peak demand, notably the summer.
This summer, gasoline spot prices often jumped when refiners were operating at full capacity, and there were concerns that supplies might be interrupted should Caribbean refineries, major suppliers to the region, be shut down by tropical storms or other unforeseen events.
The nation, which a decade ago had a large surplus refining capacity, has seen that surplus shrink because of numerous closings. In recent years, capacity has remained level, while demand for gasoline has been going up.
Tosco shut down the Marcus Hook refinery just before gasoline prices began to soar last spring, enabling the local union leaders here to get the attention of some Congressmen and Senators in their battle with Tosco management.
They asked whether Tosco had deliberately shut down the refinery to force up gas prices in the region. The company denied it had done so, noting that when supplies became tight and prices rose in the Northeast, refiners in Europe began to ship in gasoline.
Although the union was able to get wide publicity and political attention, it was short-lived. Gasoline prices turned back down, and union members had to sit down with Tosco managers and make concessions to persuade the company to invest in reopening the refinery, which sprawls along the Delaware River.
"They were holding all the big cards," Mr. Stephano, the local president, said, referring to Tosco's bargaining position in the last few months.
Nevertheless, he and other union leaders were able to make some important gains. For example, the local contract will incorporate any gains made by the union in the next nationwide bargaining in February 1999 for a new contract that would run until July 2002.
Also, the workers who will be hired will retain their vacation and some other benefits that they built up during their previous employment.
John Saracina, who has worked in the refinery for 17 years as a machinist, came in grim-faced and cast his ballot in the basement. He said that he had not been offered his job and that it had been a big blow to his self-esteem. "I can't figure out why I wasn't picked," he said. "It just doesn't make sense. That's the way it goes."
Some other workers also said that there seemed to be little sense in how the company made its decision.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Stocks Slump on Fears Fed Will Raise Rates Soon
Stocks slumped in the slowest trading session of the year -- 258 million shares on the New York Stock Exchange -- after unexpectedly strong economic data heightened fears that the Federal Reserve would soon raise interest rates to counter inflation.
The Dow Jones industrial average lost 31.44 points, closing at 5,616.21, after being down nearly 60 points early in the day. For the week, the Dow lost 106.53 points, its worst decline since the last week of May. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 5.41 points, to 651.99. The Nasdaq Composite index dropped 3.53 points, to 1,141.50. [Page 37.]
 
Yield on 30-Year Bond at 7.12%
Prices of Treasury securities continued Thursday's retreat on fear of Fed action. The 30-year bond tumbled 11/32 , to a price of 9516/32 to yield 7.12 percent, up from 7.03 percent on Thursday. The yield is the highest since July 9, when it was 7.13 percent. [43.]
 
Economic Indicators Show Strength
Two nongovernmental indicators showed the economy picking up speed quickly. The Purchasing Management Association of Chicago said its overall index soared to 60 in August from 51.2 in July. And the University of Michigan's final index of consumer sentiment for August rose to 95.3 from 94.7 in July -- the highest reading since January 1995. Less worrisome to investors was a Commerce Department report that consumer spending rose only two-tenths of 1 percent last month, after dropping four-tenths of 1 percent in June. Also, personal income inched up just a tenth of 1 percent in July. [34.]
 
Tobacco Executives Subpoenaed
Philip Morris executives have been subpoenaed by the Justice Department to testify before a Federal grand jury investigating whether tobacco companies and their executives knowingly provided false information about nicotine's addictiveness, a Government official said. [34.]
 
British Brokerage to Scale Back
Winchester Brokerage, a British metals brokerage firm, said that it would scale down operations and cut a number of jobs because it had lost business after adverse publicity from the copper scandal involving the Sumitomo Corporation of Japan, which was a client. [35.]
 
UST to Buy Walden Bancorp
The UST Corporation said that it would buy Walden Bancorp of Acton, Mass., for $155.9 million in stock and take a $13.5 million pretax charge. UST, which is seeking to increase its presence in eastern Massachusetts, said in June that it planned to acquire 20 Baybanks and Bank of Boston branches. [34.]
 
Stock Prices Plunge in Tokyo
Japan's benchmark stock index plunged to its lowest level in five months, led by declines in shares of car and steel manufacturers, reflecting concern that Japan's economic growth has stalled. The benchmark Nikkei 225 stock index fell 386.26, or 1.88 percent, to 20,166.90, the lowest since it fell to 19,923.7 on March 14. For the week the index fell 5.6 percent. [37.]
 
Dollar Up Against Yen and Mark
The dollar rose against most leading currencies, holding onto gains made from a second day of strong economic data that pointed to higher interest rates in the United States. In late trading in New York, the dollar stood at 108.92 Japanese yen, up from 108.30 yen on Thursday, and 1.4808 German marks, up from 1.4764 marks. [36.]
 
Copper Futures Prices Surge
Copper futures prices surged as labor problems led to fears of shortages. The contract for December delivery rose 4.55 cents, to 95.25 cents a pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the highest for an active contract since June 26. Futures prices for heating oil and unleaded gasoline retreated. [36.]

HEADLINE: Bilingual Parents Dismayed By English's Pull on Children

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO 

DATELINE: MIAMI, Aug. 30

 BODY:
In Marcel A. Apple's bedroom, the book "The Three Little Pigs" rests on a shelf next to "Mi primer libro de palabras en Espanol" ("My First Book of Spanish Words"). The 3-year-old can sing along to both a Sesame Street song and "La Bamba," or have as much fun watching "Barney," a purple, English-speaking dinosaur, as he does "Tito," a blue, Spanish-speaking shark.
But when Marcel tired of pounding on a piano one recent afternoon and sat on the lap of his Nicaraguan nanny, it was English that he spoke. 
 "Estan descansando?" ("Are they resting?") Josefina Avendano asked him, pointing at a picture of a countryside in a children's book.
"No, they're eating," he answered.
"I try to put him in an environment where there's as much Spanish spoken as possible but it never seems to be enough," said Marcel's mother, Esther Perez-Apple, a Cuban-American who herself is trying to recapture the Spanish she heard while growing up in her native New York. "He knows he can speak in English and people could understand him."
Last month Congress entered into a heated debate over English-only legislation, with proponents insisting that the very survival of American culture and civilization, as well as the language, was at stake. On a more down-to-earth level, the English-only drive has also been fueled by resentment against bilingual rules and other accommodation of immigrants, both old and young, who have not learned English.
But what new immigrant families across the nation are learning, as their predecessors did before them, is that the power of American culture, and particularly, the lure of television, is so strong that it is a challenge to raise a child who can speak a foreign language fluently.
Parents send their children to foreign countries for summer vacations, hire bilingual nannies and read bedtime stories in a cacophony of tongues, all in an effort to pass on the family's language, give the children a linguistic advantage for the future or simply enrich them culturally. Still, the languages, parents say, often lose out to television, schools and peer pressure.
Even in Miami, one of the country's most Hispanic areas, parents say it is hard to get their children to learn Spanish. The challenge to raise a child to be bilingual can be even greater for families speaking less-prevalent languages like German, Swedish or Japanese, parents say.
This experience runs counter to concerns that English is in danger of being diluted. And some bilingual educators argue that anti-immigration sentiments and the English-only laws, which generally require government business to be conducted in English, only help foster a climate that plays down the importance of other languages. They say that for a child to become bilingual it is essential that he places value on speaking more than English.
"You can have a bilingual child a lot of different ways," said Barbara Z. Pearson, a linguist who is part of a University of Miami research team on bilingualism. "But what you have to make sure is that the kid hears both languages, values both languages and wants to speak to the people who speak those languages."
But Mauro E. Mujica, chairman of U.S. English, a group in Washington that lobbies for English-only laws, says the language restrictions, which have been enacted in 23 states and are under consideration in Congress, are not meant to discourage other languages or dictate what people choose to speak or learn. A native of Chile who came to this country in 1964 and married an American, Mr. Mujica said he reared his three children speaking English and Spanish.
"We are in no way forcing people to speak anything; they can speak whatever they want," Mr. Mujica said. "But when dealing with the government, they need to speak English."
In Miami, however, a center of Latin American commerce that is heavily dependent on tourism, the problem is not getting people to learn English but getting them to retain their Spanish. Sandra H. Fradd, a University of Miami professor who conducted a study for the Greater Miami Chamber of Commerce released this year, concluded that businesses worried that a shortage of bilingual workers would hamper the city's economic growth. Many employers, she said, said they relied on foreign citizens or first-generation immigrants for jobs that require Spanish because they have found that many Hispanics are not fluent enough to conduct business.
Often, when efforts to raise a child to be bilingual fall short, he ends up with what is known as "kitchen Spanish," the kind that is good enough only to order at a restaurant or talk to grandparents. Dade County Public Schools officials say only about 2 percent of high school students graduate fully fluent in Spanish -- which means able to read, write and converse in it -- in a school system where half the students are of Hispanic descent.
"We have enough Spanish courses offered that students can graduate with the fluency of Cervantes," said Lourdes Rovira, director of foreign-language skills for Dade County schools. But, she added, "the youngsters are not taking them."
Census figures show Miami to be the nation's most bilingual area, with 57 percent of the residents speaking a language other than English at home, the overwhelming majority of them Spanish. But linguists here say that is largely because of the constant influx of immigrants, not necessarily the language skills of the natives.
In the Apple-Perez home in the Coconut Grove section of Miami, Marcel hears Spanish from Ms. Avendano, his mother and tapes, videos and books. He hears English from both his parents (his father, Larry, speaks English only), his paternal grandparents and television, as well as at his preschool.
On any given day, it all adds up to about one-third Spanish, not enough for him to say "zanahoria" when he sees the picture of a carrot but plenty to carry on a 15-minute conversation with a Costa Rican during a recent vacation in which he was asked questions in Spanish and he answered them in English.
"He understands everything," Ms. Avendano noted.
But Lourdes Alvarez, whose 11-year-old daughter Cristina spoke only Spanish before Kindergarten, saw English quickly take over after the girl started school. Mrs. Alvarez says she now has to force Cristina to speak Spanish at home.
"She fights it," Mrs. Alvarez says.
Cristina, who insists she wants to be bilingual, says English just comes more easily to her. Despite taking Spanish as a subject in school, she cannot write it and barely reads it.
"Spanish is really hard," she says.
University of Miami linguists and psychologists who study the development of languages in elementary-school children have found that students are more proficient in English than in Spanish as early as second grade, even at schools that emphasize both languages. But they say that can only be expected when English is society's primary language and that the children would still be considered bilingual if they can speak and write in both languages.
In Miami, where Spanish is ubiquitous, spoken on the streets and in corporate offices and heard on numerous radio and television channels, many children fail to learn the language well because parents assume it will happen automatically. But parents who have successfully promoted Spanish skills in their children say that to become literate in the language the child needs not only to hear it but to study it.
Many immigrant families see English as so fundamental to a better life that they allow their children to even shun their first language, researchers who have studied the subject say. Bilingual educators say English usually becomes the first and sometimes only language by the first American-born generation.
Census figures show that of the country's 230 million residents 5 years old and older in 1990, 198 million spoke English only at home. Among the remaining 32 million, a majority, 17 million, said they spoke Spanish at home. Nearly 13 million of those said they also spoke English "well" or "very well."
"Many teen-agers say they never spoke Spanish and their parents can't speak English," said Professor Fradd, who conducted the study for the Greater Miami Chamber of Commerce. "Something happened there -- trying to pass, to assimilate, not being seen as different."
English, indeed, is so powerful it has become in effect the international language in most fields. But the need to compete for jobs in an increasingly global economy is one of the reasons for a resurgence of language study at universities and two-year colleges nationwide in the 1990's, says the Modern Language Association, an organization of college language teachers.
In some schools, like Miami's Florida International University, the Spanish classes are filled with students who were native speakers before English took over. But even families, like that of Barry Brown, a credit union accounting clerk with no language other than English in his background, have chosen to make language skills a priority in their children's education. Mr. Brown's 8-year-old daughter, Megan, who attends a school here whose goal is to turn out bilingual graduates in English and Spanish, now can translate at the market and with neighbors for her mother, Mary, a presser at a dry cleaner who gets teased by her daughter for knowing only two Spanish words: "si" and "no.".
"Any type of education is a real asset," Mr. Brown said. "You feel your kids will have a better shot at the jobs that are opening."
The Congressional bill to formally make English the official language of the United States, which awaits a vote in the Senate, is the latest effort by English-only proponents to curtail bilingual government documents, ballots and other programs. This fall the Supreme Court will weigh in by reviewing Arizona's law.
Supporters of the laws say they promote unity, cut government costs and encourage new immigrants to learn English. But often they portray other languages as a threat to nationalism and point north at the conflict between French-speaking Quebec and the rest of Canada, which is predominantly English-speaking.
Canada scholars question the Quebec analogy, noting there is no region in the United States where a language other than English predominates and where a local majority wants to preserve that. And detractors of the laws, including President Clinton, who has promised to veto the bill if it is passed, say the laws only harm the minority of elderly and newly arrived immigrants who cannot communicate in English.
Florida is among the states where voters approved a constitutional amendment to declare English the official language. But legislation needed to enforce the eight-year-old law has never been passed.
Parents who want their children to speak other languages say the laws are unnecessary.
"There's no question that the nation's language is English, but we have to broaden ourselves," said Ligia Esteva, a Miami area resident who sends her 9-year-old son, Eric, to a school where instruction is given in Spanish and English. "You go to Israel and the cabdriver speaks seven languages. I'd like my son to take French."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Even in bilingual areas like Miami, immigrants find their children speak only English. Larry Apple and his wife, Esther Perez-Apple, a Cuban-American, say their son, Marcel, understands when his nanny, Josefina Avendano, reads to him in Spanish, but he often responds in English. (Kevin Moloney for The New York Times)(pg. 7)

HEADLINE: Pataki Panel Says Casinos Could Bring In $2.6 Billion

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Aug. 30

 BODY:
A commission created by Gov. George E. Pataki issued a report today that said casino gambling could generate as much as $2.6 billion in business for New York State annually and create 38,000 jobs.
The findings will probably be the focus of debate early next year as the State Legislature takes up a measure to legalize casino gambling. The Legislature already passed such a bill once, but a second vote is required before the measure can be put before voters in the form of a Constitutional amendment. 
 Legalizing casinos would put the Catskills and the Niagara Falls region, where casinos would be permitted under the amendment, in direct competition with Atlantic City and the Indian casinos of Connecticut. The report projects that New York casinos would recapture tens of millions of dollars now flowing into neighboring states, as well as draw 19 million visitors a year from outside New York.
The report makes no recommendation on whether to legalize casinos, and it warns that casinos could reduce lottery revenue, as well as attendance at horse races.
Mr. Pataki said he would not comment on the report because he had not reviewed its findings. But it clearly bolsters his contention that casino gambling could help the state's tourism industry.
"I think the most important thing is to be able to stop losing billions of dollars to surrounding states and surrounding communities," Mr. Pataki said at a news conference in the Capitol this afternoon.
"I think we want to see the Catskills and other historic tourism areas able to successfully compete," he said.
The report also provides powerful ammunition to hotel owners, resort companies and casino operators as they prepare for what is sure to be an intense lobbying campaign by opponents of legalized gambling.
The supporters of casino gambling praised the report as the most comprehensive study to date on the potential benefits of casinos.
"I'm pleased because the timing is perfect," said Assemblyman Ronald C. Tocci, a Democrat from Westchester who is a member of the Assembly's Racing and Wagering Committee.
"It comes just as we're slated to vote on whether to place this issue on the ballot," Mr. Tocci said.
But opponents of casinos sharply questioned the report, saying it failed to adequately take into account the many hidden costs of legalizing casinos, like the need to expand police forces and courts in order to deal with gambling-related crimes.
They also charged that the report was compiled merely to provide Mr. Pataki, a Republican, the political cover he needs to campaign on behalf of legalizing casinos at the same time many of his more conservative supporters voice opposition to the idea.
"The report sounds like it's virtually irresponsible," said Lee Karr, chairman of the Coalition Against Casino Gambling. "It seems to be making the case for casino gambling based on insufficient data."
The measure that lawmakers will vote on would allow casino gambling in four places: the Catskills, the cities of Niagara Falls and Buffalo and either Saratoga or Warren counties near Lake George.
Each area would be allowed one casino, except for the three counties of the Catskills -- Greene, Ulster and Sullivan -- where a larger but undetermined number of casinos would be authorized.
Last year, the measure passed 34 to 21 in the Republican-controlled Senate, in almost a straight party-line vote.
It passed 88 to 62 in the Democratic-controlled Assembly, where 27 Republicans joined 61 Democrats in supporting the measure.
The report based its estimates on the number of casinos permitted under the resolution. For example, it said that a resort casino in western New York could attract as many as 10.5 million visitors a year and create 4,000 jobs.
But the report went beyond the scope of the resolution, noting that Long Island and New York City would be suitable spots for casinos. It also found that resort casinos would pump about $278 million in taxes into state coffers alone.
In its report, the commission estimated that New Yorkers, largely New York City residents, spend $2.35 billion a year at casinos in Atlantic City and Las Vegas, Nev., and at the Foxwoods casino in Ledyard, Conn.
But some of the report's estimates were questioned. Dennis Tompkins, a spokesman for State Comptroller H. Carl McCall, said the amount of money the report estimated New York casinos would get by reclaiming New York gamblers from casinos in neighboring states was based on the premise that those states would not expand casino gambling.
"That's an optimistic assumption that probably won't hold up," he said. "You certainly can't expect that New Jersey and Connecticut are going to say, 'O.K., New York is going to have gambling.'
"They're going to fight to keep their business."
In fact, more casinos are already planned for Atlantic City, and a new Indian casino is to open in Connecticut this fall.
The eight-member task force was composed largely of the Governor's top aides.
They included Michael H. Urbach, the Commissioner of Taxation; Alexander F. Treadwell, the Secretary of State; Bernadette Castro, the Commissioner of Parks; Paul S. Schectman, the director of criminal justice; Edward Martin, Executive Deputy Commissioner of the State Department of Economic Development; Jerry Belinski, a former chairman of the State Racing and Wagering Board, and Thomas Griffen, the executive director of the state's Office of Real Property Services.
The other member is Robert J. Sise, former New York State chief administrative judge.

HEADLINE: STUNG BY ATTACKS, MEXICO INTENSIFIES SEARCH FOR REBELS

BYLINE: By JULIA PRESTON 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Aug. 30

 BODY:
The Mexican Government, stung by widespread attacks this week by a new leftist guerrilla organization, condemned the rebels as terrorists and stepped up the hunt for them today in seven states.
The Government of President Ernesto Zedillo was caught off guard by the coordinated assaults on military and police posts across southern Mexico on Wednesday night by the shadowy new rebel organization, the Popular Revolutionary Army. 
 Struggling to recover, Mr. Zedillo said late Thursday that he had concluded that the group does not enjoy any widespread popular following and ordered the army and police to pursue the rebels as criminals.
Officials ruled out any possibility of political discussions with the group, whose surprise assaults in faraway villages and sleepy army barracks left 13 people dead.
The new insurgency battered the image Mr. Zedillo has worked hard to build of a country climbing out of a painful recession, moving toward a more open political system and reaching peace with the Zapatista rebels, an apparently unrelated group that rose up in the southern state of Chiapas three years ago.
The Popular Revolutionary Army appears to present a much more formidable security challenge to the Government than the Zapatistas did when they briefly occupied a city in Chiapas in January 1994 and made their plea for relief from the abuses suffered by Indian peoples.
But the new rebels' hard-core revolutionary message seems unlikely to attract anything like the following the Zapatistas enjoy among the Mexican public, which has shown time and again its fear of instability and radical change.
"In this country at this time, the method of armed struggle is inappropriate and it also encourages the most repressive and violent elements in the Government," said Pablo Gomez, a leading member of the leftist Party of the Democratic Revolution. "There is no possibility of a generalized rebellion."
But Mr. Zedillo's economic strategy has kept wages at rock bottom and has left hundreds of thousands of Mexicans in debt and out of work. The rebels' message of despair with the Government could begin to resonate over time, especially in rural states like Oaxaca and Guerrero.
"This Government has forgotten to respond to the needs of everday people," said Alfredo Castillo, a human rights activist who has worked closely with grass roots organizations around the country.
Based on the events this week, it appears that Mexico may face a period of sporadic guerrilla attacks on its police, military and infrastructure, possibly rattling the military and frightening tourists and investors.
Prices on the Mexican stock market took a dive this morning but rallied to close only 0.88 percent down. Likewise the peso slipped and then recovered somewhat, indicating that the news has not spread any panic in Mexico's markets.
Politicians, analysts and human rights observers across the political spectrum said today that the new insurgents do not pose a real threat to Mexico's long-stable political institutions.
Government officials have not tried to conceal the extent of the attacks on Wednesday night, but they dismiss the new rebels' political relevance. New reports issued late Thursday disclosed that there had been assaults or propaganda actions in six states on Wednesday night, including three small attacks in the state of Mexico, on the edge of Mexico City.
Arturo Nunez, the Under Secretary of the Interior Ministry, called the Popular Revolutionary Army "a mini-group left over from the 1970's." He said Government intelligence indicated that the core militants of the group number only about 50, and that no more than 200 guerrillas took part in Wednesday's actions.
"We can definitely affirm that they have no popular base," Mr. Nunez said. "What they may have are some accomplices in their crimes."
Based on their actions this week and glimpses of their organization they have given in clandestine interviews, the Popular Revolutionary Army seems to be much better financed and more heavily armed than the Zapatistas.
They demonstrated their ability to move surreptitiously throughout much of southern Mexico in both cities and mountains, to pop up, strike and vanish. Their fighters seem to be trained in the ideology and tactics of classic guerrilla warfare, and their political beliefs are forged by Marxist doctrine.
The Zapatistas said they took up arms to dramatize an essentially peaceful cause and did not aspire to state power, but the Popular Revolutionary Army, according to its manifestoes, regards armed struggle as the way to force out the Government.
Sergio Bautista Martinez, a former high-school teacher turned guerrilla who was arrested Wednesday by the police in the state of Mexico, told the police that "the final objective of the group is to destabilize the country," the policeman who questioned him, Gilberto Vargas Munoz, said today.
According to Mr. Vargas, Mr. Bautista said that "since they hadn't been able to bring the Government down peacefully, they hope to do it with arms."
President Zedillo sought to make a clear distinction between his approach to the Zapatistas and the treatment he has ordered for the Popular Revolutionary Army. Out of deference to the Zapatistas' strong roots among the Indians of Chiapas, the Government has agreed to a cease-fire and carried on peace talks with them since last year.
The search for the new rebels by the army and police will be based on arrest warrants on charges ranging from illegal weapons possession to murder and sedition, officials said. Four men were arrested on Thursday in the southwestern state of Guerrero on suspicion of being members of the group. The Interior Ministry has issued summonses for 41 people who are members of above-ground peasant and worker groups it has identified as fronts for the Popular Revolutionary Army.
The Zapatistas "burst into our national life by means of violence, not terrorism," Mr. Zedillo said. He said the security forces had been instructed not to "affect the rights of third parties" in their operations.
Reinforced army and police units were deployed in the states of Mexico, Oaxaca, Guerrero, Chiapas, Puebla, Hidalgo and Veracruz. Security was also stepped up on federal highways and around key electrical and petroleum installations, officials said.
Mexicans reacted with shock as they began to realize what groups are behind the Popular Revolutionary Army. By the admission of its own commanders, the central organization is a long-time clandestine revolutionary party whose origins date back to a stubborn guerrilla war waged by peasants in the mountains of Guerrero in the 1970's.
The organization has claimed responsibility over the years for a series of political bombings and killings, including a bombing in the parking lot of a Mexico City mall in 1994. Many of its targets have been members of other less radical leftist groups.
"The first reaction, now that everyone realizes they are really for real, will be fear," said Jorge Castaneda, an expert on leftist movements in Mexico and elsewhere in Latin America.

 
 
Military Convoy Attacked
 MEXICO CITY, Aug. 30 (Reuters) -- Heavily armed men attacked a military convoy today in Michoacan State, in western Mexico, killing one soldier and wounding two, a local police commander said.
 A Radio Red broadcast quoted a police official in the town of Tacambaro, 50 miles south of the state capital, as saying 40 to 50 armed men attacked the convoy.
 Gonzalo Montoya, a police commander in Tacambaro, said in a radio interview that the attackers were armed with AK-47's and other high-powered assault rifles and wore military-style fatigues.
 The commander said he thought the attackers were linked to drug trafficking or kidnapping in the area. "We often see people dressed in military-style clothing here," he said.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
Dole Promises Energy Department Cuts Won't Shut New Mexico Laboratories

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: ALBUQUERQUE, N.M., Aug. 30

 BODY:
Bob Dole reassured voters here today that despite having previously urged abolishing the Energy Department, he would preserve the national defense laboratories in New Mexico.
The statement that the labs are "going to remain open" again points up potential difficulties in Mr. Dole's economic plan to balance the budget and cut taxes. The two laboratories at Los Alamos and Sandia account for about 10 percent of the Federal Department of Energy budget. Including the Lawrence Livermore laboratory in California would raise that to about 15 percent. 
 To a boisterous afternoon crowd of about 1,000 people at the Spanish Village of the New Mexico State Fairgrounds, Mr. Dole offered assurances to the 14,000 workers employed by the Energy Department at the Los Alamos and Sandia nuclear laboratories that he did not mean to suggest they would lose their jobs.
"When I talk about getting rid of the Energy Department, I'm talking about getting rid of the micromanagement from Washington, abolishing the bureaucracy in Washington," he asserted. "That does not mean eliminating the labs in New Mexico."
"When I'm President of the United States, the labs are going to remain open because they have an important job to do, as Pete Domenici has told me," he said of the Senator from New Mexico. "So don't be scared." Mr. Dole told reporters later that his plans were to "downsize" the Energy Department, not eliminate it.
He also mocked President Clinton for saying in his acceptance speech on Thursday night that he wanted to build a bridge to the future -- a turnaround of Mr. Dole's pledge in his own acceptance speech that he wanted to build a bridge to the past.
"He raised enough taxes to build a big, big bridge -- all the way from L.A. to New York," Mr. Dole said of President Clinton. "We need a bridge from Washington, D.C., to Little Rock, Ark. That's where the bridge ought to be built, and it ought to be one way. And it ought to be open for one day, Jan. 20."
Before flying to New Mexico, Mr. Dole bid farewell to his vacation state of California at a rally in Costa Mesa, but he pledged to return.
"I want to touch every hand in California," Mr. Dole said as the morning sun beat down in the dusty horse ring of the Orange County Fairgrounds. "I've still got about 37 million to go. But I'm working on it."
The Dole campaign is giving every indication so far of contesting California, where the latest Field poll a week ago showed that Mr. Dole had cut Mr. Clinton's 20-point lead in half after the Republicans convention.
Both Mr. Dole and Jack Kemp, his running mate, spent the last week vacationing on the California coast and have insisted at every stop that they will devote time and resources to California, which President George Bush wrote off in 1992, to his detriment.
Kenneth Khachigian, a top Dole strategist and adviser in California, said that the campaign had spent one-third of its entire national media budget in California since mid-August. But aides have suggested that the campaign will make a final determination on spending much later when the shape of the race becomes clearer.
Orange County calls itself "the most Republican county in America." Thomas A. Fuentes, the Republican county chairman, said the billing was based on the fact that the county has not elected a single Democrat at any level of government. Of the county's one million registered voters, there are 235,700 more Republicans than Democrats.
Taking the podium at the fairgrounds, Mr. Dole said: "Twelve years ago, President Ronald Reagan kicked off his campaign re-election right here in Orange County, and from here he went on to a big victory in November, and we're going to do it again on Nov. 5, 1996."
President Reagan drew about 50,000 people to that event, held at a nearby park; today, Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp drew a fraction of that number, perhaps 3,000, to the arena, which seats 8,000.
Because of the Labor Day holiday, local Republicans were worried about attendance and organized a telephone tree to try to coax out all 15,000 activists and volunteers listed in the party's data base at county headquarters, Bill Christiansen, the party's executive director, told The Los Angeles Times.
The Dole campaign also bought radio time to increase turnout. Mr. Dole himself called Mr. Fuentes, the chairman, on Wednesday to generate enthusiasm. And the Kemp family bused in 27 relatives.
"We're on very fertile ground here," Mr. Dole announced. "I told your chairman last night we would settle for a margin in Orange County of say, oh, let's say 425,000 votes. That would be a good margin in Orange County."
Mr. Dole stayed away from the topic of Dick Morris, President Clinton's top strategist who resigned Thursday over allegations involving a prostitute. Nelson Warfield, Mr. Dole's spokesman, said there was no need for Mr. Dole to take up the charge. "Word is getting out without a megaphone," he said.
Dole aides also released a letter that Scott Reed, Mr. Dole's campaign manager, wrote to Peter Knight, Mr. Clinton's campaign manager, seeking to arrange the fall Presidential debates. Former Gov. Carroll A. Campbell Jr. of South Carolina, who had been under consideration as Mr. Dole's running mate, is to represent the Dole camp in the debate negotiations.
Mr. Warfield said the Dole campaign at this point had "no position" on whether Ross Perot, the third-party Presidential contender, should join the debates.
Mr. Kemp had no comment on his debate plans. "Quarterbacks don't talk about the game before the game," he said.
 
In His Own Words
 
 BOB DOLE

 At a rally at the Orange County Fairgrounds in Cosa Mesta, Calif.:
 
"We have the most detailed accounting of how to cut taxes and balance the budget of any Presidential campaign in history, because I will not make a promise unless I know I can keep it.
 "And this is a promise I have made, and this is a promise I will keep. . . .
 "And it is not that complicated, I would say to our critics. We're not talking nuclear physics here, but we are talking Nobel Prizes. The other day, three of the world's most famous and respected Nobel Prize-winning economists endorsed our plan, and they're not into politics. They wouldn't have stood with me and spoken out unless they knew we could do it.
 "These people aren't in politics as we are. They looked at our plan, they examined our plan, they analyzed our plan, they studied our plan, they said it's a good plan.
 "It's a good plan to get America moving again after wage stagnation in the last four years, where actual wage rates have gone down one cent an hour, not up, but one cent down under President Clinton.
 "So we're going to get it done. "
